What Is Mantiq?

5 min
Mushtarak: The Common Word
Author defines common word and discusses usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mushtarak word and determine whether it can be used in all its meanings simultaneously as haqiqah according to the author.

Core Idea
A common word is coined for multiple meanings from the start; using all meanings at once is real (haqiqah).

In Islamic legal theory, words can be coined in different ways. One important category is the mushtarak (common word), which is coined from the outset for multiple distinct meanings. This classification affects how we interpret texts, especially the Qur'an and Hadith. The author clarifies that, contrary to some, using a common word in all its meanings is permissible and considered real (haqiqah). Let's examine the definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic of mushtarak
A mushtarak word is coined for multiple unrelated meanings from the start, without any prior transfer. The passage states: 'فمشترك' indicating that if a word like 'أنا' is coined initially for multiple meanings, it is common.
The example 'أنا' is used here to illustrate a word coined initially for multiple meanings.
2
Recognize the scholarly disputes about mushtarak
The passage mentions disputes over possibility, occurrence, and use for opposites. The majority affirms its reality even in divine speech, with opposites like 'قرء' for menstruation and purity.
These disputes are foundational in usul al-fiqh.
3
Determine the author's position on using all meanings
The author takes the position that intending all meanings of a common word is real (haqiqah). The text says: 'وهو ههنا إرادة كل معانيه حقيقة عندنا'.
This is contrary to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him).
4
Contrast with other positions
Some allow using all meanings as one metaphorical meaning (majaz) or one meaning with the other as a partial instance; these are agreed upon.
The disagreement is specifically about using all meanings as real.
5
Apply the concept to an Islamic example
The word 'قرء' (qur') is a classic example: it means both menstruation and purity. It is a mushtarak word, and the author holds it can mean both simultaneously as haqiqah.
This has implications for the 'iddah ruling in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الألفاظ بأوضاع، فيخرج نحو أنا ابتداء، بلا تخلل نقل فمشترك. اختلف تقسيم الاسم الكثير المعنى ومنقول وحقيقة أولاً في إمكان المشترك، ثم في وقوعه، ثم في كونه للضدين، والحق عند الجماهير أن المشترك واقع في كلام العرب، بل في كلام الله تعالى، حتى بين الضدين، كالقرء للحيض والطهر، لكن لا غنوم فيه، وهو ههنا إرادة كل معانيه حقيقة عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله تعالى، وأما إرادة جميع معانيه على أنه معنى واحد مجازي، أو أحد معانيه بأن الآخر من جزئياته فجائز اتفاقاً، والتفصيل في كتب الأصول. والمرتجل، وهو اللفظ المنقول عن معناه الأصلي إلى آخر، من غير مناسبة، قيل من المشترك؛ لأنه لما لم يكن بين المنقول عنه والمنقول إليه مناسبة فكأنه وضع لكل منهما ابتداء، والجمهور على أنه قسيم له، وقيل من المنقول. وإلا يكن وضعه لهما ابتداء، بل للأول فقط، فإن اشتهر في الثاني المنقول إليه، بأن يفهم من غير قرينة فمنقول: شرعي، إن كان الناقل
A chapter on words with coinages, so what is like 'I' initially, without intervening transfer, is common. The division of the noun with many meanings and transferred and real differed: first regarding the possibility of the common, then its occurrence, then its being for opposites. The truth according to the majority is that the common occurs in the speech of the Arabs, indeed in the speech of God Most High, even between opposites, like qur' for menstruation and purity, but there is no benefit in it, and here it is intending all its meanings as real according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him. As for intending all its meanings as one metaphorical meaning, or one of its meanings such that the other is from its particulars, it is permissible by agreement. The detail is in the books of usul. And the coined, which is the word transferred from its original meaning to another without appropriateness. It is said to be from the common, because since there is no appropriateness between what is transferred from and what is transferred to, it is as if it were coined for each initially. The majority hold that it is co-ordinate with it, and it is said to be from the transferred. And if it is not coined for both initially, but only for the first, and it becomes famous in the second, the transferred to, such that it is understood without a context, then it is a transferred: shar'i, if the transferrer is...
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory discussion of words in terms of how they are coined (وضع). The author first defines مشترك (common word) as one that is coined for multiple meanings from the start without any prior transfer. The text then notes a scholarly dispute about the possibility and reality of common words, affirming that they do exist even in divine speech, and can even signify opposites (like قرء meaning both menstruation and purity). The author takes the position that such a word can be used in all its meanings simultaneously as real (حقيقة), contrary to al-Shafi'i. However, using it in multiple meanings as one metaphorical meaning or one meaning with the other as a particular is agreed. Next, المرتجل (coined word) is defined as a word transferred from its original meaning to another without any appropriateness (مناسبة). This is debated: some consider it a type of common word (since it seems like two separate coinages), but the majority treat it as a separate category. Finally, the passage introduces المنقول (transferred word) as one where the coinage is only for the original, but it becomes famous in the transferred meaning such that it is understood without context, e.g., shar'i terms. The discussion is foundational for usul al-fiqh, as it informs how legal texts are interpreted. The author follows the order of the matn (Sullam al-Ulum), expanding on each category with opinions and examples.

Key Terms
مشترك
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling (according to the author and the majority) on using a common word like 'قرء' to mean both menstruation and purity at the same time?
A) It is impossible because that would be contradictory.
B) It is allowed but only metaphorically (majaz).
C) It is allowed and considered real (haqiqah) usage.
D) It is allowed only if it is a transferred term.

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak word is coined for multiple meanings; according to the author, it is permissible and real to intend all meanings at once, contrary to al-Shafi'i.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find the word 'qur'' in the Qur'an (Surah al-Talaq, verse 4). Explain why it is considered mushtarak and how the author's position on using all meanings simultaneously would affect the interpretation of the 'iddah ruling.

Murtajal: The Coined Word Without Appropriateness
Author defines coined word and discusses its relation to common.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish murtajal from other word types and explain the debate about its classification.

Core Idea
A murtajal word is transferred from its original meaning to another without any relation; it is like two separate coinages.

Some words are not originally coined for their current meaning but are transferred. The murtajal is a special case where the transfer happens without any appropriateness (munasaba) between the old and new meanings. This creates ambiguity: is it a type of common word or a separate category? The author presents the majority view that it is co-ordinate with mushtarak. Let's dissect this definition.

Guided Path
1
Define murtajal according to the passage
The passage defines murtajal as 'اللفظ المنقول عن معناه الأصلي إلى آخر، من غير مناسبة' – a word transferred from its original meaning to another without appropriateness.
This lack of appropriateness distinguishes it from manqul.
2
Understand the controversy
Some scholars consider murtajal a type of mushtarak because without appropriateness, it is as if the word was coined separately for each meaning. The majority, however, treat it as co-ordinate with mushtarak (قسيم له).
The passage notes three opinions: from mushtarak, co-ordinate, and from manqul.
3
Compare with mushtarak
Mushtarak is coined for multiple meanings initially; murtajal involves transfer from one original meaning to another without appropriateness.
The key difference is presence of transfer.
4
Identify the key distinguishing feature
Murtajal has a prior original meaning that is transferred away from, whereas mushtarak has no prior meaning; both meanings are original coinages.
The transfer without appropriateness makes it seem like a new coinage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الألفاظ بأوضاع، فيخرج نحو أنا ابتداء، بلا تخلل نقل فمشترك. اختلف تقسيم الاسم الكثير المعنى ومنقول وحقيقة أولاً في إمكان المشترك، ثم في وقوعه، ثم في كونه للضدين، والحق عند الجماهير أن المشترك واقع في كلام العرب، بل في كلام الله تعالى، حتى بين الضدين، كالقرء للحيض والطهر، لكن لا غنوم فيه، وهو ههنا إرادة كل معانيه حقيقة عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله تعالى، وأما إرادة جميع معانيه على أنه معنى واحد مجازي، أو أحد معانيه بأن الآخر من جزئياته فجائز اتفاقاً، والتفصيل في كتب الأصول. والمرتجل، وهو اللفظ المنقول عن معناه الأصلي إلى آخر، من غير مناسبة، قيل من المشترك؛ لأنه لما لم يكن بين المنقول عنه والمنقول إليه مناسبة فكأنه وضع لكل منهما ابتداء، والجمهور على أنه قسيم له، وقيل من المنقول. وإلا يكن وضعه لهما ابتداء، بل للأول فقط، فإن اشتهر في الثاني المنقول إليه، بأن يفهم من غير قرينة فمنقول: شرعي، إن كان الناقل

What Is Mantiq?

Manqul: The Transferred Word
Author introduces transferred word and its characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a manqul word and distinguish it from murtajal, and explain what makes a transfer 'shar'i' according to the passage.

Core Idea
A manqul word is coined for one meaning, then becomes famous in a new meaning without context, e.g., shar'i terms.

Many technical terms in Islamic sciences are manqul – originally coined for a general meaning, then transferred to a specific legal or theological meaning. The passage defines manqul and gives the shar'i type as an example. Understanding manqul is essential for interpreting revealed texts correctly, as words may have shifted in meaning.

Guided Path
1
Define manqul according to the passage
The passage says: 'وإلا يكن وضعه لهما ابتداء، بل للأول فقط، فإن اشتهر في الثاني المنقول إليه، بأن يفهم من غير قرينة فمنقول' – if the word is not coined for both meanings initially but only for the first, and it becomes famous in the transferred second meaning such that it is understood without context, it is manqul.
The condition of fame without context is crucial.
2
Contrast with murtajal
Murtajal also involves transfer but without appropriateness; manqul may or may not have appropriateness, but the key distinction is that manqul is based on a single original coinage and later fame, whereas murtajal is seen as separate coinages.
Some scholars even consider murtajal as a type of manqul, as noted in the passage.
3
Identify the subclass: shar'i
The passage indicates that if the transferrer is the Lawgiver (Shari'), the transferred word is called 'shar'i'. This is a specific type of manqul.
Examples include 'salah', 'zakah', 'sawm'.
4
Note the condition of fame without context
The transferred meaning must be so prevalent that it is understood without any contextual clue; otherwise, it may be a metaphorical usage (majaz) or require context.
This distinguishes manqul from haqiqah and majaz.

Source Text

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

Condition for Using Metaphor: Hearing the Types, Not Particulars
Author sets condition for metaphor use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why only the types of metaphorical relations need to be heard, not every particular usage.

Core Idea
One heard type licenses countless particulars.

This lesson addresses a common misconception: that every metaphorical expression must be individually attested from the Arabs. The author clarifies that only the type of relationship (e.g., cause-effect, part-whole) needs to be heard; individual instances follow by analogy. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly identifying metaphors in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
The author states that it is not required (lā yushtaraṭ) to hear every particular metaphorical usage. This is the main proposition.
2
Distinguish particulars from types
'Particulars' (juz’iyyāt) are specific metaphorical phrases. 'Types' (anwā‘) are the general relations (e.g., isti‘āra tashbīhiyya, majāz mursal) that generate particulars. The author says: yes, you must have heard the types.
This distinction is crucial in uṣūl al-fiqh for qiyās.
3
Understand the rationale
If only types are required, then a single attested relation can generate infinite metaphorical expressions by analogy. This makes the language system productive.
This rationale protects the language user from needing exhaustive memorization.
4
Recognize the opposing view
A small group (shardhama) demanded hearing every particular. The author dismisses them as negligible, reinforcing the standard position of the majority.
You may encounter this minority view in some works; know that it is not followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَلَا يُشْتَرَطُ (۲)) في استعمال المجاز (سَمَاعُ الْجُزْئِيَّاتِ)، أي جزئياته المستعملة ، خلافا لشرذمة قليلة لا اعتداد بهم. (نَعَمْ، يَجِبُ سَمَاعُ أَنْوَاعِهَا (۳)) لفظ بها في غير الموضوع له. (وَعَلَامَةُ الْحَقِيقَةِ التَّبَادُرُ (۱)) ، أي تبادر معناه المستعمل فيه، (وَالْعَرَاء عن الْقَرِينَةِ) الصارفة ، (وَعَلَامَةُ) الْمَجَازِ الْإِطْلَاقُ عَلَى الْمُسْتَحِيلِ، وَاسْتِعْمَالُ اللُّفْظ بَعْض الْمَعْنَى (٢) ، كَالدَّابَّةِ عَلَى الْحِمَارِ ) ، والتفصيل في كتب الأصول.
(And it is not required (2)) in using metaphor (hearing the particulars), i.e., its used particulars, contrary to a small group not regarded. (Yes, hearing its types is obligatory (3)), pronounced in other than its proper subject. (And the sign of reality is direct understanding (1)), i.e., immediate understanding of its used meaning, (and being free from context) that diverts; (and the sign of metaphor) is applying it to what is impossible, and using the word for part of the meaning (2), like 'dābba' for the donkey, and the detail is in the books of uṣūl.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions and signs for identifying reality (ḥaqīqa) and metaphor (majāz). The author first states that it is not required to hear every particular metaphorical usage; it suffices to have heard the types of relational categories (e.g., cause-effect). He refutes a small minority who demand hearing every instance. Then he gives two signs for reality: (1) immediate understanding (tabādur) meaning the first thing that comes to mind when hearing a word without context, and (2) the absence of a diverting context (qarīna). For metaphor, two signs are given: (1) applying the word to something impossible in its literal sense (e.g., 'dābba' for a donkey, since dābba literally means any land animal, but calling a donkey 'dābba' is metaphorical because it specifies part of the meaning), and (2) using the word for part of its literal meaning. The example 'dābba' for a donkey illustrates that the word is used for a subset of its literal extension. This is a standard discussion in uṣūl al-fiqh, and the author refers to uṣūl works for more detail.

Key Terms
سماع
الجزئيات
الأنواع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for the validity of a metaphorical expression?
A) Hearing every particular metaphorical usage.
B) Hearing the type of metaphorical relation.
C) Both hearing particulars and types.
D) No hearing is required.

Key Takeaway
For metaphor, you must know the attested types of relations, not every specific phrase.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example of a metaphorical type (e.g., part for whole) in a fiqh manual, and explain why the particular instance is valid if the type is attested.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
5 min
Signs of Reality and Metaphor: Tabādur and Qarīna
Author lists signs for reality and metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the signs of reality (ḥaqīqa) and metaphor (majāz) and apply them to linguistic examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Immediate understanding without context signals reality; context or impossibility signals metaphor.

The author provides clear diagnostic signs to distinguish literal from figurative language. Reality is identified by tabādur (immediate understanding) and the absence of a qarīna (contextual clue). Metaphor is identified by applying the word to something impossible in its literal sense or using it for part of its meaning. These tools are essential for interpreting the Qur'an, Ḥadīth, and legal texts with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the signs of reality
The passage gives two signs: (1) immediate understanding (tabādur) of the meaning without requiring context, and (2) the absence of a qarīna (contextual clue that would divert from the literal meaning).
If both hold, the word is used literally.
2
Understand tabādur and qarīna
Tabādur means the first meaning that springs to mind when the word is heard in isolation. Qarīna is any linguistic or situational context that prevents that immediate understanding.
In uṣūl, tabādur is often tested by asking a native speaker.
3
Identify the signs of metaphor
The passage gives two signs: (1) applying the word to something impossible in its literal sense (e.g., 'dābba' meaning any land animal, but used for the specific donkey), and (2) using the word for only part of its literal meaning.
The first sign is stronger; the second is often a corollary.
4
Apply to the example 'dābba'
'Dābba' literally means any crawling or moving animal. Using it for a donkey is metaphorical because it restricts the meaning to a part (donkey) of the larger class, and it is impossible for 'dābba' in its literal sense to refer exclusively to a donkey.
This example illustrates both signs of metaphor simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَلَا يُشْتَرَطُ (۲)) في استعمال المجاز (سَمَاعُ الْجُزْئِيَّاتِ)، أي جزئياته المستعملة ، خلافا لشرذمة قليلة لا اعتداد بهم. (نَعَمْ، يَجِبُ سَمَاعُ أَنْوَاعِهَا (۳)) لفظ بها في غير الموضوع له. (وَعَلَامَةُ الْحَقِيقَةِ التَّبَادُرُ (۱)) ، أي تبادر معناه المستعمل فيه، (وَالْعَرَاء عن الْقَرِينَةِ) الصارفة ، (وَعَلَامَةُ) الْمَجَازِ الْإِطْلَاقُ عَلَى الْمُسْتَحِيلِ، وَاسْتِعْمَالُ اللُّفْظ بَعْض الْمَعْنَى (٢) ، كَالدَّابَّةِ عَلَى الْحِمَارِ ) ، والتفصيل في كتب الأصول.
(And it is not required (2)) in using metaphor (hearing the particulars), i.e., its used particulars, contrary to a small group not regarded. (Yes, hearing its types is obligatory (3)), pronounced in other than its proper subject. (And the sign of reality is direct understanding (1)), i.e., immediate understanding of its used meaning, (and being free from context) that diverts; (and the sign of metaphor) is applying it to what is impossible, and using the word for part of the meaning (2), like 'dābba' for the donkey, and the detail is in the books of uṣūl.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions and signs for identifying reality (ḥaqīqa) and metaphor (majāz). The author first states that it is not required to hear every particular metaphorical usage; it suffices to have heard the types of relational categories (e.g., cause-effect). He refutes a small minority who demand hearing every instance. Then he gives two signs for reality: (1) immediate understanding (tabādur) meaning the first thing that comes to mind when hearing a word without context, and (2) the absence of a diverting context (qarīna). For metaphor, two signs are given: (1) applying the word to something impossible in its literal sense (e.g., 'dābba' for a donkey, since dābba literally means any land animal, but calling a donkey 'dābba' is metaphorical because it specifies part of the meaning), and (2) using the word for part of its literal meaning. The example 'dābba' for a donkey illustrates that the word is used for a subset of its literal extension. This is a standard discussion in uṣūl al-fiqh, and the author refers to uṣūl works for more detail.

Key Terms
التبادر
القرينة
الإطلاق على المستحيل

Key Takeaway
Reality is known by immediate understanding without context; metaphor is known by impossibility in literal application or part-for-whole usage.

Priority Rules for Word Ambiguity and Locus of Metaphor
Author gives priority rules and locates essential metaphor in nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the priority rules among naql, majaz, and ishtirak to resolve word ambiguity, and identify that essential metaphor occurs only in nouns.

Core Idea
When a word is ambiguous, metaphor is king – but only in nouns.

In the Islamic linguistic tradition (usul al-fiqh and logic), words can have multiple meanings through transfer (naql), metaphor (majaz), or sharing (ishtirak). This lesson from Bahr al-‘Ulum establishes the hierarchy of preference among these classifications and clarifies where essential metaphor resides. Understanding these rules is crucial for interpreting ambiguous terms in the Quran and hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three classifications
First, understand naql (transfer of a word to a new meaning by convention), majaz (metaphorical use with a relation and clue), and ishtirak (one word with multiple unrelated meanings). Note the author's claim that ishtirak is rare compared to the others.
Rarity is the basis for preference.
2
Apply the preference rules
When a word is ambiguous between naql and ishtirak, prefer naql because ishtirak is rarer. Similarly, between majaz and ishtirak, prefer majaz. Between naql and majaz, prefer majaz because naql is rarer than majaz.
The hierarchy: majaz > naql > ishtirak (where > means preferred).
3
Determine the locus of essential metaphor
Essential metaphor (majaz bi al-dhāt) occurs only in nouns (ism), excluding adjectives. It is found in common nouns and proper nouns, not in verbs or particles.
Adjectives are excluded because they derive meaning from nouns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(النَّقْلُ وَالْمَجَازُ أَوْلَى مِنَ الاِشْتِرَاكِ (۳)؛ لقلته بالنسبة إليهما، فإذا دار على غيره عند اللفظ بين النقل والاشتراك فالنقل أولى ، وكذا بين الاشتراك والمجاز ، فيحمل على المجازية ، (وَالْمَجَازُ أَوْلَى مِنَ النَّقْل )؛ لقلته بالنسبة إلى المجاز، فإذا دار اللفظ بينهما ثبت المجازية ، وكذا إذا دار بينهما وبين الاشتراك . أين يقع المجاز بالذات (وَالْمَجَازُ بِالذَّاتِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الاِسْمِ (٤)، أي ما ليس بصفة، سواء كان اسم جنس أو علما (٥).
(Transfer and metaphor are more preferable than sharing (3); because of its rarity relative to them, so when the word is ambiguous between transfer and sharing, then transfer is more preferable. Likewise between sharing and metaphor, it is taken as metaphorical. (And metaphor is more preferable than transfer); because of its rarity relative to metaphor, so when the word is ambiguous between them, metaphoricalness is established. Likewise if it is ambiguous between them and sharing. Where does essential metaphor occur? (And essential metaphor is only in the noun (4), i.e., what is not an adjective, whether it is a genus noun or a proper noun (5).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage establishes priority rules among three linguistic phenomena: naql (transfer of meaning from one subject to another, like 'salāt' from duʿāʾ to ritual prayer), majāz (metaphor), and ishtirāk (one word having multiple unrelated meanings). The author states that naql and majāz are to be preferred over ishtirāk because ishtirāk is rarer. If a word is ambiguous, one should first assume transfer over sharing, and metaphor over sharing. Then between metaphor and transfer, metaphor is preferred because transfer is rarer than metaphor. Finally, he locates essential metaphor (majāz bi al-dhāt) in nouns, not verbs or prepositions, because nouns are independent in meaning. Adjectives are included in the noun category? He specifies 'what is not an adjective' but then says 'whether genus noun or proper noun'. This implies that adjectives (ṣifa) are excluded; essential metaphor occurs in ism (noun) not in verbs or particles. This is a standard position in logic and rhetoric.

المجاز

Key Takeaway
When ambiguity arises, metaphor is the most preferred classification (over transfer and sharing), and essential metaphor occurs only in nouns (excluding adjectives).

Substitution of Synonyms: When Is It Obligatory?
Author discusses the obligation of substituting synonyms and cites scholarly disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when synonym substitution is permissible based on linguistic conventions and the principle of 'awarid (accidents).

Core Idea
Synonyms share meaning but not accidents — substitution is not always obligatory.

This lesson explores a key logical-linguistic principle: synonyms (mutaradifat) are not always interchangeable in composed expressions. The author clarifies that while in enumeration substitution is obligatory by consensus, in composition it depends on linguistic accidents ('awarid) such as appropriate pairing with agent or object. Understanding this distinction prevents erroneous application in Quranic exegesis and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
Find the author's main claim: 'And it is not obligatory in it... that each one takes the place of the other.' This is the foundational principle.
The rule applies to composition, not enumeration.
2
Distinguish composition from enumeration
Note the exception: in enumeration (e.g., listing synonyms), substitution is obligatory by agreement (ittifāqan). The debate is only about composed expressions with agent and object.
This distinction is crucial for applying the rule correctly.
3
Examine the concept of 'awarid (accidents)
The author explains that the correctness of pairing a synonym with a specific agent or object depends on 'awarid (linguistic conventions), not on meaning alone. Unity of meaning does not imply unity of accidents.
'Awarid here refers to contextual appropriateness, not philosophical accidents.
4
Apply the rule to the example
Analyze the example: 'Salla 'alayhi' (May Allah bless him) is correct for the Prophet, but 'Da'a 'alayhi' (May Allah curse him) is not, even though both are verbs of supplication. The accident of reverential context prevents substitution.
This illustrates that linguistic custom ('urf) governs usage.
5
Evaluate scholarly opinions
Note the differing views: some say absolute obligation, others restrict to same language. The author sides with the more correct view that substitution is not obligatory generally.
Knowing the range of opinions helps in subsequent scholarly debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَجِبُ فِيهِ)، أي في تكثر اللفظ مع توحد المعنى قِيَامُ كُلِّ مَقَامَ الْآخَر، وَإِنْ كَانَا مِنْ لُغَةٍ) حال التركيب، مع العامل والمعمول، محققا أو مقدرا، أما في حال التعداد فيجب اتفاقا، وقيل: يجب مطلقا، قال في الحاشية: هو الصحيح عند ابن الحاجب. وقيل: إن كانا من لغة، وهو الأصح، (فَإِنَّ صِحَّةِ الضَّمِّ) مع العامل أو المعمول (مِنَ الْعَوَارِض) ، ولا يلزم من اتحاد المعنى اتحاد العوارض، (يُقَالُ: صَلَّى عَلَيْهِ»، أي صلى الله على خاتم النبيين، (وَلَا يُقَالُ «دعا عَلَيْهِ») ، العياذ بالله.
And it is not obligatory in it — i.e., in multiplicity of expression with unity of meaning — that each one takes the place of the other, even if they are from the same language, in the state of composition, with the agent and the object, actual or estimated. As for the state of enumeration, it is obligatory by agreement. And it is said: it is obligatory absolutely. He said in the Hashiya: that is the correct view according to Ibn al-Hajib. And it is said: only if they are from the same language, and that is the more correct. For the correctness of conjoining with the agent or the object is among the accidents, and unity of meaning does not necessitate unity of accidents. It is said: 'Salla 'alayhi' (May Allah bless him), meaning may Allah bless the Seal of the Prophets, and it is not said: 'Da'a 'alayhi' (May Allah curse him), Allah forbid.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule regarding synonyms (mutaradifat) and whether one synonym can substitute for another in all contexts. The author clarifies that it is not obligatory for each synonym to take the place of another, even if both are from the same language, when the expression is in a composed state with an agent and object, whether actual or estimated. However, in the case of enumeration (like listing synonyms), it is obligatory by consensus. Some scholars hold that substitution is obligatory absolutely, but the correct view according to Ibn al-Hajib is that it is not obligatory. Another opinion restricts mandatory substitution only when the synonyms are from the same language, which is deemed more correct. The rationale is that the correctness of pairing a particular synonym with a specific agent or object is based on linguistic conventions (accidents), and unity of meaning does not imply unity of these accidents. An illustrative example: one says 'Salla 'alayhi' (May Allah bless him) for the Prophet, but one cannot say 'Da'a 'alayhi' (May Allah curse him) because the latter is inappropriate, even though both verbs are synonymous in terms of invoking a supplication. Thus, context and usage restrict substitution.

Key Terms
الترادف
قيام كل مقام الآخر
العوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because 'salla' and 'da'a' are not synonyms.
B) Because the correctness of pairing is among the accidents, and unity of meaning does not necessitate unity of accidents.
C) Because the names are considered a special case (muta'abbad bih).
D) Because it is obligatory absolutely, but this example is an exception.

Key Takeaway
Synonym substitution is not obligatory in composed expressions because correctness of pairing depends on linguistic accidents, which are not implied by mere synonymy.

Particular and Universal: The Role of Thisness
Author defines particular and universal via thisness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a particular concept from a universal concept based on the inclusion of individualizing thisness and identify the mode of perception appropriate to each.

Core Idea
Particulars include a thisness that blocks sharing; universals lack it and are perceived by intellect.

The author of Bahr al-Ulum defines the foundational logical concepts of 'particular' and 'universal'. He begins from the classification of single meanings and introduces a key term: 'thisness' (الهذية). Understanding this distinction is crucial for all subsequent logical operations, as definitions and syllogisms rely on universals. Let us unpack his definitions carefully.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of single meaning
The author first classifies concepts: some include a 'thisness' preventing sharing, others do not. Find this binary in the Arabic.
Look for the phrase 'ما هو مشتمل على الهذية' and 'ما هو بخلافه'.
2
Extract the definition of particular (جزئي)
The particular includes thisness and is perceived by sense or presence. Note the phrase 'يشتمل على الهذية'.
3
Extract the definition of universal (كلي)
The universal lacks thisness and is perceived by the intellect. It is the negation of thisness.
The author says 'والثانية لاتدرك إلا بالتعقل'.
4
Understand disposition vs privation
The author initially says universal is disposition and particular is privation, but then corrects: actually particular is disposition (having thisness) and universal is privation (lacking it).
See the text: 'الجزئي ملكة والكلي عدم' after the correction.
5
Apply to examples
The author gives examples: the Necessary and Possible are universals; the phoenix is a nonexistent particular. Verify which class each belongs to.
Consider whether each example includes a thisness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعاني المفردة
كاللاشيء وأمثاله ، أَوْ لَا يمتنع ، إِمَّا مَعْدُومٌ ، كالعنقاء، أَوْ مَوْجُودٌ في شخص أو أكثر، كَالْوَاجِبِ وَالْمُمْكِنِ، وقد يطلق الكلي على ما يمكن أفراده المتكثرة موجودة أو معدومة، وقد يطلق على ما يوجد فرد منه، مع إمكان الغير أو امتناعه، وَإِلَّا يُجَوِّز تكثُرَه من حيث نفس تصوره فَجُزْئِي ظاهر هذا يدل على أن الكلي ملكة والجزئي عدم، ونص السيد قدس سره على خلافه.
وتفصيل المقام: أن من المفهومات ما هو مشتمل على الهذية المانعة من الشركة، ومنها ما هو بخلافه، فالأول لا يدرك إلا بإحساس أو إدراك حضوري، والثانية لاتدرك إلا بالتعقل ؛ لأن شأن العقل ذلك، ونحن لا ننكر وجودها في الحواس ؛ فإن حصول الصورة فيها، وإن كان شبحا كما يراه الشيخ في صور المبصرات مستلزم لحصول ماهيتها الكلية ؛ فإن الشبح وإن كان مباينا للمعلوم، لكنه موافق لها في الماهية ، فحصولها مستلزم لحصول الماهية ، بل إنما ننكر إحساسها ؛ لأن إدراك كل حاسة مقتصر على إدراك بعض المدركات . اللون والطعم، مع أنه لا تري المطعوم يحصل في الذائقة ألا يرد أن يدري إلا الثاني، وهذا من الفطريات عندهم، وهو أجدر بذلك، فلا الكليات كما يحصل في العقل يحصل في الحواس، فالحكم بتعقل العقل دون الحواس تحكم، والأول يسمى جزئيا، والثاني كليا، والقوم لما رأوا الكلية والجزئية من المعقولات الثانية لم يعتبروا الإدراك الحضوري فيهما، وقالوا: مناط الكلية والجزئية على نحو الإدراك، فما هو مدرك بالحواس جزئي، وما هو مدرك بالعقل كلي، فالجزئي الأمر المشتمل على الهذية، والكلي سلبه، شأنه الاشتمال على الهذية، فالجزئي ملكة والكلي عدم.
ولعلك تقول: هذا لا يصح على مذهب من يجعلهما من صفات المعلوم؛ فإن معلوم الإبصار غير حاصل في مجمع النور ، إنما الحاصل شبحه، فاستمع أن الحق المطابق لرأي المشائين أن الشخص الموجود له هوية مميزة عن جميع الأغيار ، ذهنية كانت أو عينية، وله عوارض مختصة لا توجد في الغير أصلا، وعوارض مختصة بالنظر إلى الأشخاص العينية الأخر من نوعه، فإذا جرد عن العوارض الأولى يبقى له هوية مميزة عن الأشخاص العينية الأخرى، وهذه الهوية تحصل في الحاسة ويعرضها العوارض الذهنية، فهي مشتركة بين الشخص الخارجي والخاصل في الحاسة، وموجودة في ضمن الشخص الخارجي بوجود أصلي، بل عينه، وفي ضمن الشخص الذهني بوجود ظلي، فتلك الهوية من
Chapter on single meanings. Like nothingness and its likes, or does not prevent, either nonexistent like the phoenix, or existent in one person or more, like the Necessary and the Possible. The universal is sometimes applied to what can have multiple individuals existing or nonexistent, and sometimes applied to what has one individual, with possibility of another or impossibility. And if it does not allow multiplicity in terms of its own conception, then it is particular. This shows that the universal is a disposition and the particular is a privation, but al-Sayyid (may his secret be sanctified) stated the contrary. The detailed treatment of the topic: some concepts include a 'thisness' that prevents sharing, and others are opposite. The first is not apprehended except by sense or presence, the second is not apprehended except by intellection, because the function of the intellect is that. We do not deny their existence in the senses, for the obtaining of the form in them, even if it is a phantom as the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] sees in the forms of visible objects, entails the obtaining of its universal quiddity, for the phantom although different from the known, it agrees with it in quiddity, so its obtaining entails obtaining the quiddity. Rather we deny sensing them, because the perception of each sense is limited to some perceptibles, like color and taste, and you do not see the tasted thing obtains in the tastebud but the second is not perceived except the second, and this is among the innate principles with them, and it is more deserving. Thus universals do not obtain in the senses as they obtain in the intellect, so judging that the intellect understands without the senses is arbitrary. The first is called particular, the second universal. When the people [logicians] saw that universality and particularity are among the secondary intelligibles, they did not consider present perception in them, and said: the criterion of universality and particularity is according to the mode of perception: what is perceived by the senses is particular, what is perceived by the intellect is universal. So the particular is the thing including the thisness, and the universal is its negation, its function being inclusion of thisness. Thus the particular is a disposition and the universal is a privation. You might say: this is not valid according to the view that makes them attributes of the known, for the object of sight does not obtain in the sensorium, only its phantom does. So listen: the truth in accordance with the Peripatetics is that the existing individual has an identity distinguishing it from all others, mental or concrete, and has accidents specific to it not found in another at all, and accidents specific with respect to other concrete individuals of its kind. When stripped of the first accidents, it retains an identity distinguishing it from other concrete individuals. This identity obtains in the sense and is accidental to mental accidents, so it is shared between the external individual and the one in the sense, existing in the external individual with original existence, indeed it is the same, and in the mental individual with shadowy existence. That identity is from...
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion from the commentary Bahr al-Ulum on the logical poem al-Sullam al-‘Ulum. The author defines the concepts of 'particular' (جزئي) and 'universal' (كلي) by starting with the matn's classification of single meanings. He explains that a concept either includes a 'thisness' (هذية) that prevents it from being shared among multiple individuals, or it does not. The first, which includes thisness, can only be perceived by sense or presence (إحساس or إدراك حضوري), making it particular. The second, which lacks thisness, is perceived by the intellect and is universal. The author clarifies that he does not deny that sensory forms (like the phantom of a seen object) entail the universal quiddity, but he denies that senses themselves perceive universals; each sense is limited to its own proper objects (e.g., vision to colors, taste to flavors). Thus, the criterion for particularity is inclusion of thisness, and for universality its negation. He notes that the logicians treat universality and particularity as secondary intelligibles, not dependent on presence. He then addresses a potential objection: if one considers these as attributes of the known object (not the mode of perception), then the object of sight does not actually exist in the sensorium—only its phantom does. The author defends the Peripatetic view: the concrete individual has a unique identity that is preserved across sensory and mental existence. This identity is what obtains in the sense, and its accidents are mental. Thus, the identity itself is shared between the external individual and the sense impression, existing in the former originally and in the latter as a shadow. This supports the idea that particularity is about the individualizing thisness, not merely mode of perception. The passage uses examples from metaphysics (the Necessary, the Possible) and mythical creatures (the phoenix) to illustrate types of existence.

Key Terms
جزئي (juz'ī): The particular: a concept that includes an individualizing 'thisness' (هذية) preventing its sharing among multiple individuals, perceived by sense or presence. [Note: Stable term across logical tradition; here defined via thisness rather than mere mode of perception.]
كلي (kullī): The universal: a concept that does not include the 'thisness' preventing sharing, perceived by the intellect and capable of having multiple individuals. [Note: Stable term; here defined as privation of thisness.]
الهذية (al-hudhiyya): The individualizing 'thisness' or 'haeccity' that makes a concept particular and prevents it from being shared. [Note: Rare term; not commonly used in standard logic texts; may be equivalent to 'al-māhiyya al-shakhsiyya'.]
ملكة (malaka): Disposition: a quality that is naturally present and actively realized; here applied to the particular as having the trait of including thisness. [Note: In logic, often contrasts with 'adam' (privation); here used metaphorically.]
عدم (‘adam): Privation: absence of a disposition; here applied to the universal as lacking the thisness. [Note: Standard logical term for non-existence or privation; here in a technical sense.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of Bahr al-Ulum, what is the defining characteristic of a particular concept (جزئي)?
A) It exists in the external world.
B) It includes a 'thisness' (هذية) that prevents it from being shared among multiple individuals.
C) It is perceived only by the intellect.
D) It can have many individuals.

Key Takeaway
The particular is defined by inclusion of individualizing thisness, making it indivisible in conception; the universal is its negation, divisible and perceived by the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Look at the following concepts and determine whether each is particular or universal according to the author's criterion: (a) 'Human' (b) 'This specific man, Zaid' (c) 'The necessary existent' (d) 'The color red in general'. Explain your reasoning using thisness.

The Particular and Universal: Definitions Based on Mode of Perception
Author defines particular and universal by mode of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given mental content is considered particular or universal based on whether it is perceived by the senses or the intellect.

Core Idea
Particular is what the senses perceive; universal is what the intellect grasps.

This passage from a classical logic text defines the particular (جزئي) and universal (كلي) not as fixed properties of objects, but as relative to the mode of perception. Understanding this is key to seeing why definitions in Islamic sciences must be precise.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
Find where the author states: 'what is perceived by the senses is particular, what is perceived by the intellect is universal' (ما هو مدرك بالحواس جزئي، وما هو مدرك بالعقل كلي). Note that the criterion is mode of perception.
2
Understand the phantom concept
The author mentions that sensory forms are phantoms (شبح) sharing the quiddity of external objects. This helps explain why universals are not directly in senses.
Phantom shares the quiddity but is still particular because it is in the senses.
3
Apply the criterion to an example
Consider the concept 'human being' – it is perceived by the intellect, so it is universal. Consider the image of a specific person – it is perceived by the senses, so it is particular.
4
Reflect on why senses cannot perceive universals
Each sense is limited to specific qualities (e.g., sight to color, taste to flavor). Universals require abstraction beyond sensory qualities, which only the intellect can perform.
This is why the author says 'the universals do not obtain in the senses as they obtain in the intellect.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجودها في الحواس ; فإن حصول الصورة فيها، وإن كان شبحا كما يراه الشيخ في صور المبصرات مستلزم لحصول ماهيتها الكلية ; فإن الشبح وإن كان مباينا للمعلوم، لكنه موافق لها في الماهية ، فحصولها مستلزم لحصول الماهية ، بل إنما ننكر إحساسها ; لأن إدراك كل حاسة مقتصر على إدراك بعض المدركات . اللون والطعم، مع أنه لا ألا أن تری المطعوم يحصل في الذائقة يرد أن يدرك إلا الثاني، وهذا من الفطريات عندهم، وهو أجدر بذلك، فلا الكليات كما يحصل في العقل يحصل في الحواس، فالحكم بتعقل العقل دون الحواس تحكم، والأول يسمى جزئيا، والثاني كليا، والقوم لما رأوا الكلية والجزئية من المعقولات الثانية لم يعتبروا الإدراك الحضوري فيهما، وقالوا: مناط الكلية والجزئية على نحو الإدراك، فما هو مدرك بالحواس جزئي، وما هو مدرك بالعقل كلي، فالجزئي الأمر المشتمل على الهوية، والكلي سلبه، شأنه الاشتمال على الهوية، فالجزئي ملكة والكلي عدم. لكن من ولعلك تقول: هذا لا يصح على مذهب من يجعلهما من صفات المعلوم; فإن معلوم الإبصار غير حاصل في مجمع النور ، إنما الحاصل شبحه، فاستمع أن الحق المطابق لرأي المشائين أن الشخص الموجود له هوية مميزة عن جميع الأغيار ، ذهنية كانت أو عينية، وله عوارض مختصة لا توجد في الغير أصلا، وعوارض مختصة بالنظر إلى الأشخاص العينية الأخر من نوعه، فإذا جرد عن العوارض الأولى يبقى له هوية مميزة عن الأشخاص العينية الأخرى، وهذه الهوية تحصل في الحاسة ويعرضها العوارض الذهنية، فهي مشتركة بين الشخص الخارجي والخاصل في الحاسة، وموجودة في ضمن الشخص الخارجي بوجود أصلي، بل عينه، وفي ضمن الشخص الذهني بوجود ظلي، فتلك الهوية من حيث إنها معروضة للعوارض الحسية علم، ومع قطع النظر عنها معلوم ومعروض للجزئية ، والشخص الخارجي معلوم بالعرض. ولما كان التعين الذهني المميز التام مغايرا للتعين الخارجي حكم بأن شخص الحسي شبح للخارجي قال النصير الطوسي: فالصور المحسوسة منتزعة انتزاعا ناقصا مشروطا بحضور المادة، والخيالية منتزعة انتزاعا أكثر، لكنه غير تام والعقلية منتزعة انتزاعا تاما هذا غاية التقرير من قبلهم، والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب.

ثم لما كان من أقسام الجزئي ما لا يُجَوِّز العقل تكثره أصلا ، كزيد مثلا ، ومنها الفرد المنتشر وهو يطلق على معنيين: الأول ما كان معينا غير قابل للشركة في الواقع، إلا أن العقل اشتبه عليه، فيحكم عليه تارة بأنه هذا أم ذلك على البدل، والثاني ما يكون في الواقع قابلا للشركة على البدل، وكان يتوهم انتقاض تعريف الكلي منعا والجزئي جمعا أراد أن يشير إلى دفعه، وقال: (فَمَحْسُوسُ الطِّفْلِ في مَبْدَأِ الْوِلَادَةِ الذي هو فرد منتشر بالمعنى الثاني، وَشَيْخِ ضَعِيفِ الْبَصَرِ ، وَالصُّورَةِ الْخَيَالِيَّةِ مِنَ الْبَيْضَةِ الْمُعَيَّنَةِ الذين هما فردان منتشران بالمعنى الأول كُلُّهَا جُزْئِيَّاتٌ ، لأَنَّ شَيْئًا مِنْهَا لَا يُجَوِّزُ الْعَقْلُ تَكَثُرَهُ عَلَى سَبِيلِ الِاجْتِمَاعِ) ، بأن يجوز العقل في وقت واحد أنه هذا وهذا. (وَهُوَ الْمُرَادُ في تعريف الكلي والجزئي ، وقال بعض الشراح: لا أكاد أفقه واقعية الفرد المنتشر بالمعنى الثاني ; لأن الانتشار لا يكون إلا بتقيد
Their existence in the senses: for obtaining the form in them, even if it is a phantom as the Sheikh sees in the forms of visible things, entails obtaining their universal quiddity; for the phantom, although it is different from the known [object], yet it agrees with it in quiddity, so obtaining it entails obtaining the quiddity. Rather, we only deny its sensation because the perception of each sense is limited to perceiving some of the perceived things: color and taste, despite that you see that the tasted thing is obtained in the taste faculty; it is denied that it perceives except the second. This is among the primal intuitions for them, and it is more worthy of that. So the universals do not obtain in the senses as they obtain in the intellect. Thus the judgment that the intellect grasps to the exclusion of the senses is arbitrary. The first is called particular, the second universal. And the people, when they saw that universality and particularity are among the secondary intelligibles, did not consider direct perception in them and said: The criterion of universality and particularity is on the mode of perception: what is perceived by the senses is particular, what is perceived by the intellect is universal. So the particular is the thing comprising identity, and the universal is its negation; its affair is comprising identity. So the particular is a possession and the universal is a privation. But perhaps you say: This is not correct according to the view of one who makes them both attributes of the known; for the known of sight does not obtain in the focal point of light; rather, what obtains is its phantom. So listen that the truth consistent with the view of the Peripatetics is that the existing individual has an identity distinct from all others, whether mental or external, and has specific accidents that are not found in others at all, and accidents specific in relation to other external individuals of its kind. When it is stripped of the first accidents, there remains for it an identity distinct from other external individuals, and this identity obtains in the sense and is accidented by mental accidents. So it is shared between the external individual and the one present in the sense, and it exists within the external individual with an original existence, nay it is identical to it, and within the mental individual with a shadowy existence. That identity, insofar as it is a substrate for sensory accidents, is known; and when considered apart from them, it is known and a substrate for particularity, while the external individual is known accidentally. And since the mental determination, complete and distinct, is different from the external determination, it is judged that the sensory individual is a phantom of the external one. Al-Nasir al-Tusi said: Thus sensory forms are abstracted with an incomplete abstraction conditioned by the presence of matter, imaginative forms are abstracted with a more [complete] abstraction but not complete, and intellectual forms are abstracted with a complete abstraction. This is the utmost of the exposition from them, and true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.

Then, since among the divisions of the particular is that which the intellect does not allow its multiplication at all, like Zayd for example, and among them is the scattered individual (al-fard al-muntashir), which is used in two senses: first, what is determined, not actually capable of sharing, but the intellect is confused about it, so it judges it sometimes as this, sometimes as that alternatively; second, what is actually capable of sharing alternatively. And it was understood that the definition of the universal would be refuted in prevention and the particular would be refuted in inclusion, so he wanted to indicate its repulsion, and said: "So the sensation of the infant at the beginning of birth, which is a scattered individual in the second sense, and the old man with weak sight, and the imaginary form from a specific egg, which are two scattered individuals in the first sense, all are particulars, because the intellect does not allow their multiplication in the mode of coexistence" — meaning that the intellect does not allow at one time that it is this and this. "And it is what is meant in the definition of the universal and the particular." And some commentators said: I can barely understand the reality of the scattered individual in the second sense, because scattering does not occur except by being restricted.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines the particular (جزئي) and universal (كلي) and defends the definition against a potential objection using the concept of the 'scattered individual' (الفرد المنتشر). The author begins by discussing the nature of sensory perception: although sensory forms are phantoms (شبح) of external objects, they still share the same quiddity (ماهية), so obtaining the form entails obtaining the quiddity. However, universals are not obtained in the senses as they are in the intellect because each sense is limited to its specific objects (e.g., sight perceives color, taste perceives flavor). The author then states that the criterion for particularity and universality is the mode of perception: what is perceived by the senses is particular, and what is perceived by the intellect is universal. The particular is defined as 'the thing comprising identity' (الأمر المشتمل على الهوية), and the universal is its negation. The author anticipates an objection: this view seems incompatible with the idea that both particularity and universality are attributes of the known object itself, not just modes of perception. He responds by explaining the Peripatetic view: an external individual has a distinct identity and specific accidents; when stripped of the first accidents, the remaining identity is shared between the external object and its sensory phantom. This identity, as a substrate for sensory accidents, is known, but when considered apart from those accidents, it is both known and a substrate for particularity. Thus the sensory individual is a phantom of the external one, and the complete mental determination differs from external determination. The author then cites al-Nasir al-Tusi on the degrees of abstraction: sensory forms are abstracted incompletely, imaginative forms more completely, and intellectual forms completely. In the second part, the author turns to divisions of the particular: some particulars cannot be multiplied by the intellect at all (e.g., Zayd), while others are 'scattered individuals' (الفرد المنتشر) in two senses: (1) a specific individual that is not actually shareable but the intellect is confused and judges it as one of two alternatives; (2) an individual that is actually shareable alternatively (like a vague image). The author claims that the definition of the universal and particular might be thought to be refuted by these scattered individuals, so he provides examples: the sensation of a newborn infant (a scattered individual in the second sense), the vision of an old man with weak sight, and the imaginary form from a specific egg (both scattered individuals in the first sense). All these are particulars because the intellect does not allow them to be instantiated in multiple individuals simultaneously (i.e., at the same time, it cannot be both this and that). This is what is meant in the definition of the universal and particular. Finally, the author notes a commentator's difficulty in understanding the reality of the scattered individual in the second sense, since scattering implies restriction, but leaves the matter unresolved.

Key Terms
جزئي (juz'ī): The particular: what cannot be mentally instantiated in multiple individuals simultaneously; its definition depends on mode of perception (sense) or on comprising identity. [Note: No significant shift; standard definition in post-Avicennan logic.]
كلي (kullī): The universal: what can be mentally instantiated in multiple individuals; negation of the particular's defining property. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
شبح (shabah): Phantom: the sensory form or image that represents an external object but is not identical to it; shares the quiddity. [Note: Used in Avicennan psychology; consistent in the Peripatetic tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines whether something is particular or universal?
A) Its inherent nature as an object
B) Whether it can be instantiated in multiple individuals
C) The mode of perception (sensory or intellectual)
D) Its physical size or location

Key Takeaway
Particularity and universality are not fixed properties but are determined by whether we perceive something through the senses or through the intellect.

?

What Is Mantiq?

Defending the Definition: The Scattered Individual (al-Fard al-Muntashir)
Author defends definition of particular/universal using scattered individual examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a 'scattered individual' into its two types and explain why it does not undermine the definition of particular and universal.

Core Idea
The scattered individual, though vague, remains a particular because the intellect cannot instantiate it multiply simultaneously.

The author anticipates an objection: what about vague or confused sensory images that seem to be neither clearly particular nor universal? He introduces the concept of the 'scattered individual' (الفرد المنتشر) and shows that both types are still particulars, thus defending the definition.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of 'scattered individual'
Locate where the author introduces الفرد المنتشر and its two meanings: (1) what is actually determinate but mentally confused, (2) what is actually indeterminate and shareable alternatively.
2
Identify the examples
Note the three examples: infant's sensation (type 2), old man's weak vision (type 1), imaginary form from a specific egg (type 1). Recognize they are all scattered individuals.
3
Understand why they are particulars
The author explicitly says: 'all are particulars, because the intellect does not allow their multiplication in the mode of coexistence.' That is, the intellect cannot simultaneously judge them as two different individuals.
This is the same condition that defines particularity: not allowing simultaneous multiplicity.
4
See how this defends the definition
If scattered individuals were not particulars, the definition would be defective. But by showing they satisfy the condition (non-simultaneous multiplicity), the definition stands.

(وَهُوَ الْمُرَادُ في تعريف الكلي والجزئي ، وقال بعض الشراح: لا أكاد أفقه واقعية الفرد المنتشر بالمعنى الثاني ; لأن الانتشار لا يكون إلا بتقيد
Their existence in the senses: for obtaining the form in them, even if it is a phantom as the Sheikh sees in the forms of visible things, entails obtaining their universal quiddity; for the phantom, although it is different from the known [object], yet it agrees with it in quiddity, so obtaining it entails obtaining the quiddity. Rather, we only deny its sensation because the perception of each sense is limited to perceiving some of the perceived things: color and taste, despite that you see that the tasted thing is obtained in the taste faculty; it is denied that it perceives except the second. This is among the primal intuitions for them, and it is more worthy of that. So the universals do not obtain in the senses as they obtain in the intellect. Thus the judgment that the intellect grasps to the exclusion of the senses is arbitrary. The first is called particular, the second universal. And the people, when they saw that universality and particularity are among the secondary intelligibles, did not consider direct perception in them and said: The criterion of universality and particularity is on the mode of perception: what is perceived by the senses is particular, what is perceived by the intellect is universal. So the particular is the thing comprising identity, and the universal is its negation; its affair is comprising identity. So the particular is a possession and the universal is a privation. But perhaps you say: This is not correct according to the view of one who makes them both attributes of the known; for the known of sight does not obtain in the focal point of light; rather, what obtains is its phantom. So listen that the truth consistent with the view of the Peripatetics is that the existing individual has an identity distinct from all others, whether mental or external, and has specific accidents that are not found in others at all, and accidents specific in relation to other external individuals of its kind. When it is stripped of the first accidents, there remains for it an identity distinct from other external individuals, and this identity obtains in the sense and is accidented by mental accidents. So it is shared between the external individual and the one present in the sense, and it exists within the external individual with an original existence, nay it is identical to it, and within the mental individual with a shadowy existence. That identity, insofar as it is a substrate for sensory accidents, is known; and when considered apart from them, it is known and a substrate for particularity, while the external individual is known accidentally. And since the mental determination, complete and distinct, is different from the external determination, it is judged that the sensory individual is a phantom of the external one. Al-Nasir al-Tusi said: Thus sensory forms are abstracted with an incomplete abstraction conditioned by the presence of matter, imaginative forms are abstracted with a more [complete] abstraction but not complete, and intellectual forms are abstracted with a complete abstraction. This is the utmost of the exposition from them, and true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.

Then, since among the divisions of the particular is that which the intellect does not allow its multiplication at all, like Zayd for example, and among them is the scattered individual (al-fard al-muntashir), which is used in two senses: first, what is determined, not actually capable of sharing, but the intellect is confused about it, so it judges it sometimes as this, sometimes as that alternatively; second, what is actually capable of sharing alternatively. And it was understood that the definition of the universal would be refuted in prevention and the particular would be refuted in inclusion, so he wanted to indicate its repulsion, and said: "So the sensation of the infant at the beginning of birth, which is a scattered individual in the second sense, and the old man with weak sight, and the imaginary form from a specific egg, which are two scattered individuals in the first sense, all are particulars, because the intellect does not allow their multiplication in the mode of coexistence" — meaning that the intellect does not allow at one time that it is this and this. "And it is what is meant in the definition of the universal and the particular." And some commentators said: I can barely understand the reality of the scattered individual in the second sense, because scattering does not occur except by being restricted.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines the particular (جزئي) and universal (كلي) and defends the definition against a potential objection using the concept of the 'scattered individual' (الفرد المنتشر). The author begins by discussing the nature of sensory perception: although sensory forms are phantoms (شبح) of external objects, they still share the same quiddity (ماهية), so obtaining the form entails obtaining the quiddity. However, universals are not obtained in the senses as they are in the intellect because each sense is limited to its specific objects (e.g., sight perceives color, taste perceives flavor). The author then states that the criterion for particularity and universality is the mode of perception: what is perceived by the senses is particular, and what is perceived by the intellect is universal. The particular is defined as 'the thing comprising identity' (الأمر المشتمل على الهوية), and the universal is its negation. The author anticipates an objection: this view seems incompatible with the idea that both particularity and universality are attributes of the known object itself, not just modes of perception. He responds by explaining the Peripatetic view: an external individual has a distinct identity and specific accidents; when stripped of the first accidents, the remaining identity is shared between the external object and its sensory phantom. This identity, as a substrate for sensory accidents, is known, but when considered apart from those accidents, it is both known and a substrate for particularity. Thus the sensory individual is a phantom of the external one, and the complete mental determination differs from external determination. The author then cites al-Nasir al-Tusi on the degrees of abstraction: sensory forms are abstracted incompletely, imaginative forms more completely, and intellectual forms completely. In the second part, the author turns to divisions of the particular: some particulars cannot be multiplied by the intellect at all (e.g., Zayd), while others are 'scattered individuals' (الفرد المنتشر) in two senses: (1) a specific individual that is not actually shareable but the intellect is confused and judges it as one of two alternatives; (2) an individual that is actually shareable alternatively (like a vague image). The author claims that the definition of the universal and particular might be thought to be refuted by these scattered individuals, so he provides examples: the sensation of a newborn infant (a scattered individual in the second sense), the vision of an old man with weak sight, and the imaginary form from a specific egg (both scattered individuals in the first sense). All these are particulars because the intellect does not allow them to be instantiated in multiple individuals simultaneously (i.e., at the same time, it cannot be both this and that). This is what is meant in the definition of the universal and particular. Finally, the author notes a commentator's difficulty in understanding the reality of the scattered individual in the second sense, since scattering implies restriction, but leaves the matter unresolved.

Key Terms
فرد منتشر (fard muntashir): Scattered individual: a particular that the intellect cannot clearly determine as one definite individual; two types: (1) actually determinate but mentally confused, (2) actually indeterminate (shareable by alternatives). [Note: This term appears in later commentaries on al-Sullam; not widely used in earlier logic texts.]
جزئي (juz'ī): The particular: what cannot be mentally instantiated in multiple individuals simultaneously; its definition depends on mode of perception (sense) or on comprising identity. [Note: No significant shift; standard definition in post-Avicennan logic.]
كلي (kullī): The universal: what can be mentally instantiated in multiple individuals; negation of the particular's defining property. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author classify the sensation of a newborn infant as a particular despite it being a scattered individual?
A) Because it is perceived by the senses
B) Because the intellect does not allow its multiplication at one time
C) Because it is a phantom of an external object
D) Because it is a universal in disguise

Key Takeaway
The scattered individual, though vague, remains a particular because the intellect cannot instantiate it in multiple individuals at the same time, thus preserving the definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a person who sees a blurred figure at a distance and cannot tell if it is a man or a post. Is this a scattered individual? Which type? Explain why it is still a particular according to the author.
?
In a debate about the 'illa of riba, a jurist argues that 'increase' is a universal cause. Objector says: 'But increase is sometimes vague.' How would you use the scattered individual concept to respond?

Universality and Particularity: Attributes of the Known
Author defines the status of universality and particularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the view that universality and particularity are attributes of the known from the alternative view that they are attributes of knowledge, and explain why the preferred view is correct.

Core Idea
Universality is not in things but in how we know them.

This passage clarifies a foundational point in logic: universality and particularity are not real properties existing in external objects. Rather, they are attributes of our concepts (the known). The author argues that attributing them to the act of knowledge itself is less precise. By understanding this, we avoid the mistake of reifying universals as Platonic forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Locate the sentence where the author states that universality and particularity are attributes of the known (صفة المعلوم). The passage begins with this assertion and later reiterates it.
The claim is: 'والكلية والجزئية صفة المعلوم'
2
Understand the alternative view
The author mentions that some said it is an attribute of knowledge (صفة العلم). Find the reasoning for this view: individuation in the mind is mental, not external.
The alternative view is mentioned but not endorsed.
3
Analyze the reasoning for the preferred view
The author explains that mental individuation only distinguishes one mental form from another, not from external individuals. Therefore, an intellectual concept can be conceived as applicable to many external individuals. This shows universality is a property of the concept (the known), not the act of knowing.
Key phrase: 'فالصورة العقلية المشخصة بالتشخص الذهني لا ينقبض العقل باعتبار نفس تصورها عن فرضها مشتركة في الأعيان'
4
Consider the practical implication
If universality were a property of knowledge, then each act of knowing would be particular. But concepts like 'human' are universal in the mind, not in external existence. This distinction is crucial for avoiding confusion in theological and jurisprudential discussions.

فصل في المعاني المفردة. ذلك المفهوم، وقس عليه حال الجزئية، فصارت الكلية والجزئية من المعلوم، وإلى هذا يشير شرح التلويحات أيضا، وهذا محمل صحيح للسيد المحقق قدس سره الشريف. بل الجواب أن المراد في تعريف الكلي (تكثر بالمشهوم بحسب الخارج، فالصورة الحاصلة من زيد باعتبار الأذهان يستحيل أن تتكثر في الخارج: بل كلها هوية زيد): فإنها تصدق على كل واحد منها؛ بناء على التحقيق الذي أشير إليه أنها لو وجدت في الخارج كانت عين زيد، وهو المراد بحصول الأشياء بأعيانها لا بأشباحها، كذا في «الحاشية». لعل السر فيه أنك قد عرفت أن الصورة المأخوذة في الحواس ليس لها مناسبة إلا بواحد شخصي، ولها هوية بها يميزها العقل عن أغيارها، بخلاف الصورة المأخوذة في العقل، فيصدق على القبيلة الأولى أنها لو وجدت في الخارج كانت عين واحد معين، دون الثاني، وقد عرفت سابقا أن المحسوس لا يكون إلا ما هو مشتمل على الهوية المانعة عن الاشتراك، فهو جزئي لا يجوزه العقل مشتركا، بخلاف معلوم الصورة العقلية، فأتقن هذا، ولا تلتفت إلى القيل والقال. (وأما الكليات الفرضية والمعقولات الثانية فلعدم اشتمالها على الهوية ينقبض العقل بمجرد تصورها عن تجويز تكثرها في الخارج)؛ لما عرفت أن المانع هو العلم الإحساسي، وهو لا يتعلق إلا بما له هوية، (حتى قيل: إن الكليات الفرضية بالنسبة إلى الحقائق الموجودة كليات): لأن العقل لا ينقبض بمجرد تصورها عن الصدق عليها، بل إذا لاحظ المنافاة بينها، أو أقام البرهان. هذا ما تلقاه الجمهور من المحققين بالقبول، كالسيد المحقق قدس سره الشريف، والمحقق الدواني. والأظهر أنها على ضربين منها ما له أفراد ممكنة معدومة، ويصدق عليها بعد فرض الوجود، كالعنقاء وسائر المفاهيم الممكنة المعدومة، وكليتها بالنظر إليها، ومنها ما لا فرد له أصلا، كمفهوم ضروري الطرفين وسائر المحالات، وكليتها بالنسبة إلى الحقائق الموجودة والمعدومة معا، (هذا)، والله أعلم بالصواب. (والكلية والجزئية صفة المعلوم)، كما بينا سابقا، (وقيل صفة العلم). صفة المعلوم، وتحقيق هذا القول: إن التشخص تابع لنحو الوجود عند أصحاب هذا القول، العلم فالتشخص الذي يحصل لمفهوم في الذهن إنما يميزه عن الصور الذهنية الأخر، لا عن الأشخاص الخارجية، بخلاف التعين الخارجي؛ فإنه تابع للوجود الخارجي، فيميزه عن الأشخاص الخارجية الأخر، فالصورة العقلية المشخصة بالتشخص الذهني لا ينقبض العقل باعتبار نفس تصورها عن فرضها مشتركة في الأعيان، بمعنى أنها لو وجدت لكانت عين الأشخاص الخارجية من نوعه، بخلاف الصور الحسية والخيالية؛ لاشتمالها على هوية مانعة عن التكثر في الخارج. فإن قلت كما يجوز الشركة في الصور العقلية لم لا يجوز شركة الشخص الخارجي في الصور الحاصلة في الحاسة، فيجوز أن يحصل من غير تجريد في الذهن؟ قلت لو سلم جواز الشركة بحسب نفس التصور، فلا يلزم منه إمكان الحصول في نفس الأمر؛ لعروض العوارض الخارجية المنافية للتشخص
Chapter on singular meanings. That concept, and measure upon it the case of particularity, so universality and particularity become from the known, and to this refers the Sharh al-Talwihat also, and this is a correct interpretation of al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq, may his secret be sanctified. Rather the answer is that what is meant in the definition of the universal is 'it multiplies by the concept in respect of the external', so the form obtained from Zayd by consideration of the minds is impossible to multiply in the external; rather all of it is the identity of Zayd: for it is true of each one of them; based on the verification alluded to that if it existed in the external it would be the very Zayd, and that is what is meant by the obtaining of things by their very essences not by their phantoms, thus in the Hashiya. Perhaps the secret in it is that you have known that the form taken in the senses has no correspondence except with a single particular, and it has an identity by which the intellect distinguishes it from its others, unlike the form taken in the intellect, so it is true of the first kind that if it existed in the external it would be a specific single one, not the second, and you have known previously that the sensible is only that which includes the identity that prevents sharing, so it is particular; the intellect does not permit it to be shared, unlike the known of the intellectual form. So master this, and do not pay attention to gossip. (And as for hypothetical universals and secondary intelligibles, because they lack identity, the intellect contracts upon mere conception of them from deeming it possible for them to multiply in the external); because you have known that the preventing factor is sensory knowledge, and it pertains only to that which has identity, (so it is said: the hypothetical universals in relation to existing realities are universals): because the intellect does not contract upon mere conception of them from being true of them, unless it considers the incompatibility between them or establishes a proof. This is what the majority of verifiers have accepted, such as al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq, may his secret be sanctified, and al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani. And the most apparent is that they are of two types: among them is that which has possible non-existent individuals, and is true of them after assuming existence, like the 'anqa' and other possible non-existent concepts, and their universality is with respect to them; and among them is that which has no individual at all, like the concept of necessary of both sides and other impossibilities, and their universality is with respect to both existing and non-existing realities together. (This), and God knows best what is correct. (And universality and particularity are attributes of the known), as we have explained previously, (and it was said: attribute of knowledge). Attribute of the known, and the verification of this statement: that individuation follows the mode of existence according to the proponents of this statement, knowledge so the individuation that occurs for a concept in the mind only distinguishes it from other mental forms, not from external individuals, unlike external determination; for it follows external existence, so it distinguishes it from other external individuals. So the intellectual form individualized by mental individuation, the intellect does not contract from considering its conception itself to the assumption of it being shared in external entities, meaning that if it existed it would be the very external individuals of its type, unlike sensory and imaginative forms; because they include an identity preventing multiplication in the external. If you say: as sharing is permissible in intellectual forms, why is it not permissible for the external individual to share in the forms obtained in the sense, so that it could be obtained without abstraction in the intellect? I say: if sharing is conceded according to the conception itself, it does not entail the possibility of obtaining in reality; because of the occurrence of external accidents contrary to individuation.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of universality and particularity, arguing that these are attributes of the known (the concept) rather than of external existence. The author begins by stating that both 'universality' and 'particularity' are properties of the known (المعلوم), as indicated in the Sharh al-Talwihat. He then addresses a potential objection: how can the definition of the universal (as that which can be predicated of many) apply to mental forms? The answer is that the form of Zayd in the mind, if it were to exist externally, would be identical to Zayd himself; hence the mental form is the same as the external individual in terms of identity. This is the 'hushul al-ashya' bi a'yaniha' (obtaining things by their essences). The author explains that sensory images have a particular identity that prevents them from being shared, while intellectual concepts lack such identity, allowing them to be predicated of many. He then discusses 'hypothetical universals' (الكليات الفرضية) and 'secondary intelligibles' (المعقولات الثانية), which are universals that never exist externally (like impossible concepts) or only in potential individuals (like 'anqa'). For these, the intellect does not immediately assume they can be instantiated externally; it only does so after considering compatibility or proof. The author notes two types: those with possible but non-existent individuals, and those with no possible individuals at all. Finally, he clarifies the two positions on whether universality/particularity are attributes of knowledge (العلم) or the known (المعلوم). The preferred view is that they are attributes of the known, because individuation in the mind is mental, not real; mental individuation only distinguishes between mental forms, not external ones. Thus, the intellect can conceive of an intellectual form as applicable to many external individuals, unlike sensory forms which are tied to a specific external identity. The passage concludes with an objection-and-answer: why can't an external individual be shared in the same way as an intellectual form? The answer is that even if conceptual sharing is granted, actual sharing in external reality is impossible due to the accidents that individuate the external object.

Key Terms
الكلية
الجزئية
المعلوم
العلم

C) Universality applies to sensory forms; particularity applies to intellectual forms.
D) Particularity is a property of knowledge; universality is a property of the known.

Key Takeaway
Universality and particularity are mental attributes of concepts, not real properties of external objects.
