Why Sensory Images Are Not Universal: The Role of Identity (al-Huwiyya)
Author explains why sensory forms are particular

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why sensory and imaginative forms are particular while intellectual forms can be universal, using the concept of al-huwiyya (identity).

Core Idea
Sensory forms have identity; intellectual forms do not.

A central distinction in this passage is why the image of a specific thing (like Zayd) cannot be applied to other things, while the concept of 'human' can. The key is al-huwiyya—a kind of identity that ties a mental form to a specific external individual. Sensory and imaginative forms carry this identity; intellectual forms are abstracted from it. This explains why only the latter can be universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of forms
The author contrasts 'the form taken in the senses' (الصورة المأخوذة في الحواس) and 'the form taken in the intellect' (الصورة المأخوذة في العقل). Find where this distinction is made.
The passage says: 'لعل السر فيه أنك قد عرفت أن الصورة المأخوذة في الحواس ليس لها مناسبة إلا بواحد شخصي'
2
Understand the concept of huwiyya
Huwiyya is described as an identity that allows the intellect to distinguish one form from another. For sensory forms, this identity ties them to a specific external individual. The passage states: 'ولها هوية بها يميزها العقل عن أغيارها'.
Huwiyya prevents sharing: 'المحسوس لا يكون إلا ما هو مشتمل على الهوية المانعة عن الاشتراك'
3
Trace the argument for particularity
Because sensory forms have huwiyya, they can only correspond to one particular external thing. If they existed externally, they would be that very thing. The author gives the example of Zayd: the mental image of Zayd is identical to Zayd himself in terms of identity.
Key phrase: 'فالصورة الحاصلة من زيد ... لو وجدت في الخارج كانت عين زيد'
4
Contrast with intellectual forms
Intellectual forms (like the concept of 'human') lack huwiyya. Therefore, the intellect can conceive of them as applicable to many external individuals. The passage says: 'بخلاف معلوم الصورة العقلية'.
The intellect does not contract from assuming they can be shared: 'لا ينقبض العقل ... عن فرضها مشتركة في الأعيان'
5
Apply the principle
Now consider any sensory image you have of a specific object. According to this principle, that image cannot be used as a universal concept. Your mental image of a particular apple is tied to that apple's identity and cannot represent all apples.

Source Text

فصل في المعاني المفردة. ذلك المفهوم، وقس عليه حال الجزئية، فصارت الكلية والجزئية من المعلوم، وإلى هذا يشير شرح التلويحات أيضا، وهذا محمل صحيح للسيد المحقق قدس سره الشريف. بل الجواب أن المراد في تعريف الكلي (تكثر بالمشهوم بحسب الخارج، فالصورة الحاصلة من زيد باعتبار الأذهان يستحيل أن تتكثر في الخارج: بل كلها هوية زيد): فإنها تصدق على كل واحد منها؛ بناء على التحقيق الذي أشير إليه أنها لو وجدت في الخارج كانت عين زيد، وهو المراد بحصول الأشياء بأعيانها لا بأشباحها، كذا في «الحاشية». لعل السر فيه أنك قد عرفت أن الصورة المأخوذة في الحواس ليس لها مناسبة إلا بواحد شخصي، ولها هوية بها يميزها العقل عن أغيارها، بخلاف الصورة المأخوذة في العقل، فيصدق على القبيلة الأولى أنها لو وجدت في الخارج كانت عين واحد معين، دون الثاني، وقد عرفت سابقا أن المحسوس لا يكون إلا ما هو مشتمل على الهوية المانعة عن الاشتراك، فهو جزئي لا يجوزه العقل مشتركا، بخلاف معلوم الصورة العقلية، فأتقن هذا، ولا تلتفت إلى القيل والقال. (وأما الكليات الفرضية والمعقولات الثانية فلعدم اشتمالها على الهوية ينقبض العقل بمجرد تصورها عن تجويز تكثرها في الخارج)؛ لما عرفت أن المانع هو العلم الإحساسي، وهو لا يتعلق إلا بما له هوية، (حتى قيل: إن الكليات الفرضية بالنسبة إلى الحقائق الموجودة كليات): لأن العقل لا ينقبض بمجرد تصورها عن الصدق عليها، بل إذا لاحظ المنافاة بينها، أو أقام البرهان. هذا ما تلقاه الجمهور من المحققين بالقبول، كالسيد المحقق قدس سره الشريف، والمحقق الدواني. والأظهر أنها على ضربين منها ما له أفراد ممكنة معدومة، ويصدق عليها بعد فرض الوجود، كالعنقاء وسائر المفاهيم الممكنة المعدومة، وكليتها بالنظر إليها، ومنها ما لا فرد له أصلا، كمفهوم ضروري الطرفين وسائر المحالات، وكليتها بالنسبة إلى الحقائق الموجودة والمعدومة معا، (هذا)، والله أعلم بالصواب. (والكلية والجزئية صفة المعلوم)، كما بينا سابقا، (وقيل صفة العلم). صفة المعلوم، وتحقيق هذا القول: إن التشخص تابع لنحو الوجود عند أصحاب هذا القول، العلم فالتشخص الذي يحصل لمفهوم في الذهن إنما يميزه عن الصور الذهنية الأخر، لا عن الأشخاص الخارجية، بخلاف التعين الخارجي؛ فإنه تابع للوجود الخارجي، فيميزه عن الأشخاص الخارجية الأخر، فالصورة العقلية المشخصة بالتشخص الذهني لا ينقبض العقل باعتبار نفس تصورها عن فرضها مشتركة في الأعيان، بمعنى أنها لو وجدت لكانت عين الأشخاص الخارجية من نوعه، بخلاف الصور الحسية والخيالية؛ لاشتمالها على هوية مانعة عن التكثر في الخارج. فإن قلت كما يجوز الشركة في الصور العقلية لم لا يجوز شركة الشخص الخارجي في الصور الحاصلة في الحاسة، فيجوز أن يحصل من غير تجريد في الذهن؟ قلت لو سلم جواز الشركة بحسب نفس التصور، فلا يلزم منه إمكان الحصول في نفس الأمر؛ لعروض العوارض الخارجية المنافية للتشخص
Chapter on singular meanings. That concept, and measure upon it the case of particularity, so universality and particularity become from the known, and to this refers the Sharh al-Talwihat also, and this is a correct interpretation of al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq, may his secret be sanctified. Rather the answer is that what is meant in the definition of the universal is 'it multiplies by the concept in respect of the external', so the form obtained from Zayd by consideration of the minds is impossible to multiply in the external; rather all of it is the identity of Zayd: for it is true of each one of them; based on the verification alluded to that if it existed in the external it would be the very Zayd, and that is what is meant by the obtaining of things by their very essences not by their phantoms, thus in the Hashiya. Perhaps the secret in it is that you have known that the form taken in the senses has no correspondence except with a single particular, and it has an identity by which the intellect distinguishes it from its others, unlike the form taken in the intellect, so it is true of the first kind that if it existed in the external it would be a specific single one, not the second, and you have known previously that the sensible is only that which includes the identity that prevents sharing, so it is particular; the intellect does not permit it to be shared, unlike the known of the intellectual form. So master this, and do not pay attention to gossip. (And as for hypothetical universals and secondary intelligibles, because they lack identity, the intellect contracts upon mere conception of them from deeming it possible for them to multiply in the external); because you have known that the preventing factor is sensory knowledge, and it pertains only to that which has identity, (so it is said: the hypothetical universals in relation to existing realities are universals): because the intellect does not contract upon mere conception of them from being true of them, unless it considers the incompatibility between them or establishes a proof. This is what the majority of verifiers have accepted, such as al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq, may his secret be sanctified, and al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani. And the most apparent is that they are of two types: among them is that which has possible non-existent individuals, and is true of them after assuming existence, like the 'anqa' and other possible non-existent concepts, and their universality is with respect to them; and among them is that which has no individual at all, like the concept of necessary of both sides and other impossibilities, and their universality is with respect to both existing and non-existing realities together. (This), and God knows best what is correct. (And universality and particularity are attributes of the known), as we have explained previously, (and it was said: attribute of knowledge). Attribute of the known, and the verification of this statement: that individuation follows the mode of existence according to the proponents of this statement, knowledge so the individuation that occurs for a concept in the mind only distinguishes it from other mental forms, not from external individuals, unlike external determination; for it follows external existence, so it distinguishes it from other external individuals. So the intellectual form individualized by mental individuation, the intellect does not contract from considering its conception itself to the assumption of it being shared in external entities, meaning that if it existed it would be the very external individuals of its type, unlike sensory and imaginative forms; because they include an identity preventing multiplication in the external. If you say: as sharing is permissible in intellectual forms, why is it not permissible for the external individual to share in the forms obtained in the sense, so that it could be obtained without abstraction in the intellect? I say: if sharing is conceded according to the conception itself, it does not entail the possibility of obtaining in reality; because of the occurrence of external accidents contrary to individuation.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of universality and particularity, arguing that these are attributes of the known (the concept) rather than of external existence. The author begins by stating that both 'universality' and 'particularity' are properties of the known (المعلوم), as indicated in the Sharh al-Talwihat. He then addresses a potential objection: how can the definition of the universal (as that which can be predicated of many) apply to mental forms? The answer is that the form of Zayd in the mind, if it were to exist externally, would be identical to Zayd himself; hence the mental form is the same as the external individual in terms of identity. This is the 'hushul al-ashya' bi a'yaniha' (obtaining things by their essences). The author explains that sensory images have a particular identity that prevents them from being shared, while intellectual concepts lack such identity, allowing them to be predicated of many. He then discusses 'hypothetical universals' (الكليات الفرضية) and 'secondary intelligibles' (المعقولات الثانية), which are universals that never exist externally (like impossible concepts) or only in potential individuals (like 'anqa'). For these, the intellect does not immediately assume they can be instantiated externally; it only does so after considering compatibility or proof. The author notes two types: those with possible but non-existent individuals, and those with no possible individuals at all. Finally, he clarifies the two positions on whether universality/particularity are attributes of knowledge (العلم) or the known (المعلوم). The preferred view is that they are attributes of the known, because individuation in the mind is mental, not real; mental individuation only distinguishes between mental forms, not external ones. Thus, the intellect can conceive of an intellectual form as applicable to many external individuals, unlike sensory forms which are tied to a specific external identity. The passage concludes with an objection-and-answer: why can't an external individual be shared in the same way as an intellectual form? The answer is that even if conceptual sharing is granted, actual sharing in external reality is impossible due to the accidents that individuate the external object.

Key Terms
الهوية
الصورة الحسية
الصورة العقلية
الجزئي
الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason sensory forms cannot be universal?
A) They are too detailed.
B) They contain an identity (huwiyya) that ties them to a specific external individual.
C) They are created by the senses, not the intellect.
D) They are always accompanied by emotions.

Key Takeaway
Sensory and imaginative forms are particular because they carry an identity that ties them to a single external individual, while intellectual forms are universal because they lack such identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the sensory image you have of a specific apple. According to the passage, can this image be applied to all apples? Why or why not? Use the concept of huwiyya.
?
Explain why the concept of 'horse' is universal but your mental image of a particular horse is not, using the author's reasoning.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
5 min
Hypothetical Universals and Secondary Intelligibles
Author discusses hypothetical universals and secondary intelligibles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any concept as an ordinary universal, a hypothetical universal (with two subtypes), or a secondary intelligible, and explain how their universality differs.

Core Idea
Some universals never exist externally, yet they are still universals in the mind.

Not all universal concepts can actually be found in the external world. The author discusses 'hypothetical universals' (like the phoenix or impossible concepts) and 'secondary intelligibles' (like the concept of impossibility itself). These lack the identity that would tie them to external individuals, but the intellect does not automatically assume they can be instantiated; it requires further consideration. Understanding this prevents confusion about the scope of universality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
The passage introduces two types of concepts that are universal but have no actual external individuals: hypothetical universals and secondary intelligibles. Find where these terms are defined.
'الكليات الفرضية' and 'المعقولات الثانية'
2
Understand why they are universal
These concepts lack identity (huwiyya), so the intellect can conceive of them as potentially applicable to many. However, the intellect does not automatically assume they can exist externally; it requires consideration of compatibility or proof.
Key: 'فلعدم اشتمالها على الهوية ينقبض العقل بمجرد تصورها عن تجويز تكثرها في الخارج'
3
Distinguish two types of hypothetical universals
First type: concepts that have possible but non-existent individuals (e.g., 'anqa' — a mythical bird). Their universality is in relation to those possible individuals. Second type: concepts that have no possible individuals at all (e.g., 'necessary of both sides' — impossibles). Their universality is in relation to all existing and non-existing realities.
The passage: 'الأظهر أنها على ضربين ...'
4
Apply the classification
Now consider a concept like 'a mountain of gold'. This is a hypothetical universal of the first type: it has possible individuals (if conditions were different) but none actually exist. In contrast, 'a square circle' is an impossible concept, a hypothetical universal of the second type.

Distinguishing 'Arad and 'Aradi by Conditions
Clarifies the distinction between accident and accidental via al-Dawani's three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'arad and 'aradi based on al-Dawani's three conditions of consideration and apply the distinction to examples from kalam.

Core Idea
The same nature is called 'arad, 'aradi, or mahall depending on how you condition the subject.

In Islamic logic, the terms 'arad (accident) and 'aradi (accidental) are often used interchangeably, but this passage from al-Dawani's commentary on al-Tajrid reveals a precise technical distinction. By analysing how the nature of an accident is considered—with or without reference to a subject—you will gain a tool to parse predications in kalam and usul al-fiqh accurately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three key terms
Locate the terms 'arad, 'aradi, and mahall (subject) in the passage. Note that the passage distinguishes between them as different ways of considering the same nature.
2
Understand the three conditions
Al-Dawani defines three conditions: (1) taken without any condition (la bi-shart shay'), (2) taken with the condition of existing in a subject (bi-shart al-mahall), and (3) taken with the condition of not existing in a subject (bi-shart la shay' al-mahall).
3
Map each condition to the corresponding term
Condition 1 gives 'aradi (accidental universal). Condition 2 gives the subject itself (mahall), meaning the accident's existence is identical to the subject's. Condition 3 gives 'arad (the accident as a category opposed to substance).
The example 'the women are four' illustrates predicate 'four' considered without condition (aradi) when predicated, but 'fourness' as an accident (arad) when considered as existing in a subject (the women) under condition 3.
4
Apply the distinction to a kalam example
Take the accident 'whiteness'. Consider it: (a) without condition → 'white' as a universal accidental; (b) with condition of subject (e.g., the body) → the body itself as white; (c) with condition of not being in a subject → whiteness as a real accident inhering in the body.

والجمهور من القوم على أن العرض غير العرضي وغير المحل حقيقة، وإنما له نسبة إلى المحل، بها يحمل عليه. قال بعض الأفاضل من المحققين، وهو المحقق الدواني في «الحواشي القديمة للتجريد»، كذا في «الحاشية»: طبيعة العرض، إذا أخذ لا بشرط شيء، أي لا بشرط المحل، عرضي، وإذا أخذ بشرط شيء، وهو المحل، المحل، أي وجوده بعينه وجود المحل، وإذا أخذ بشرط لا شيء، هو المحل، العرض المقابل للجوهر. ولذا صح النسوة أربع والماء ذراع، وتفصيل المقام: إن من الماهيات متأصلة في الوجود غير متزلزلة، ومنها ما هي مبهمة محتاجة إلى ما هي
The majority of the people hold that the accident ('arad) is other than the accidental ('aradi) and other than the subject in reality; rather it has a relation to the subject by which it is predicated. One of the virtuous investigators—namely the investigator al-Dawani in his 'Old Commentaries on al-Tajrid'—said, as in the 'Commentary': the nature of the accident, when taken without any condition, i.e., without condition of the subject, is accidental ('aradi); and when taken with a condition, i.e., the subject, it is the subject, i.e., its existence is identical to the existence of the subject; and when taken with the condition of nothing, i.e., the subject, it is the accident as opposed to substance. For this reason, it is valid to say 'the women are four' and 'the water is a cubit.' The detail of the position is that among essences there are those that are well-rooted in existence, not shifting, and among them there are those that are vague, needing what they are...
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage addresses the nuanced distinction between the terms 'arad (accident) and 'aradi (accidental) as used by logicians. The author first states the position of the majority: 'arad and 'aradi are different in meaning, and neither is identical to the subject (mahall). Rather, the accident has a relation to the subject, and through that relation it is predicated of the subject. He then quotes al-Dawani: when the nature of an accident is considered without any condition (i.e., without specifying whether it exists in a subject), it is called 'aradi (accidental). When it is considered with the condition of existing in a subject, it is called the subject itself (mahall), meaning its existence is the very existence of the subject. And when it is considered with the condition of not existing in a subject (bishart la shay'), it is the accident as opposed to substance—the philosophical accident existing in a subject. The example given ('the women are four', 'the water is a cubit') illustrates how accidental predicates work: 'four' is an accident predicated of women, but when considered without the subject, it is an accidental notion. The passage ends by noting that some essences are firmly rooted in existence while others are vague and require further specification, which may refer to the distinction between substance and accident.

C) When taken with the condition of not being in a subject

Key Takeaway
Al-Dawani's threefold condition resolves the ambiguity between 'arad and 'aradi: the same nature is called 'aradi without subject condition, mahall with subject condition, and 'arad with the condition of not being the subject.

The Identity of Existence and Essence in the Necessary Being
Author quotes al-Hashiya to argue for identity of existence and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the philosophers' conditional argument that the existence of the Necessary Being is identical to its essence, and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being's existence must be its essence or else an infinite regress follows.

In this passage, the author presents a crucial philosophical argument from al-Hashiya that defines the nature of God's existence. The argument uses a conditional form to show that assuming existence is separate from essence leads to an impossible chain. This lesson walks you through the logical structure and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
The argument begins with the assumption: 'If existence were an accident (arad) added to the essence...' This is the antecedent of the conditional.
2
Extract the consequent
The consequent is that it would be an effect (ma'lul) and require a cause. The author states: 'otherwise it would be an external accident for it, because composition is impossible, so it would be an effect'.
3
Trace the two branches of the cause
The cause would be either from itself (leading to priority of existence over itself) or from another (making the Necessary Being dependent). Both are impossible for the Necessary Being.
The text says: 'then either it is from itself, so its existence would be prior to it, so it would exist before existence; or from another, then the Necessary Being would be needful of another'.
4
Conclude the reductio
Since both branches lead to absurdity, the consequent is impossible. Therefore, by modus tollens, the antecedent must be false: existence is not an accident but identical to the essence.
The text concludes: 'this is a vicious circle' (خلف).

Chapter on the five universals. It is affirmation, and possibility is necessarily necessitating. Thus the discussion occurred. The verification of the station: that the referent of the self-subsistence of the essence, or the subsistence of the essential attributes to themselves, is the very determination of the quiddity, without considering an additional attribute for the essence. So making it is exactly making the essential attributes. According to the proponents of simple making, making the essence is exactly making self-subsistence. According to the proponents of composite making, the subsistence of the essential attributes to the essence, and the essence itself to it, is made accidentally; for its referent follows in being-made the referent of existence, and cannot be made without making the essence. And the more correct view is the divergence between the essence and the essential. As for the necessary concomitants, some are made by making the essence, like existence and possibility and the like, and some are required by the nature of the dependent thing, so they are made by a new [act of] making, but the maker is the very nature of the dependent thing. In this section, the existence of the dependent thing must first be, then these concomitants must arise from it. Thus should it be understood. Then the essential attributes and the necessary concomitants are necessary with respect to the essence and the dependent thing, meaning that when they are compared to the essence or the dependent thing, their non-existence is essentially impossible in reality. And the proof of 'the clear horizon' does not necessarily follow without this necessity; for the inseparability of the consideration of the essence from its consideration only necessitates its necessity with respect to it, and this necessity does not contradict temporality and being-made. So reflect, for it is subtle. Like the existence of the Necessary Being according to the school of the theologians. He said in the Hashiya: Know that the philosophers said that the existence of the Necessary Being is identical to it; otherwise it would be an external accident for it, because composition is impossible, so it would be an effect; for every external thing is necessarily an effect. Then either it is from itself, so its existence would be prior to it, so it would exist before existence; or from another, then the Necessary Being would be needful of another in its existence. This is a vicious circle.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and epistemological discussion about the nature of existence (wujud) and its relation to the essence (dhat), particularly focusing on the Necessary Being (al-Wajib). The author begins by addressing the five universals, but quickly moves into a detailed analysis of how existence relates to essence. He distinguishes between different views on whether existence is identical to essence or an additional attribute. The key argument from the philosophers (al-Hukama) is presented in the quote from al-Hashiya: if existence were an accident (arad) added to the essence, it would require a cause, leading to either an infinite regress or dependence on another, both of which are impossible for the Necessary Being. Therefore, existence must be identical to the essence. This is a standard philosophical argument for the simplicity of God. The author also discusses the classification of necessary concomitants (lawaazim) and the necessity of essential attributes when considered in relation to the essence. The commentary serves to expand on these points and explain the nuances between different schools of thought.

The Philosophers' Proof that Existence is Identical to the Necessary Being
Philosophers argue that existence (wujud) is identical to the necessary being (wajib).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the philosophers' reductio argument that specific existence is identical to the Necessary Being, identifying the conditional reasoning and the impossibility of separation.

Core Idea
If existence were separate from God, it would be an accident — impossible, so they are identical.

The philosophers distinguish between the self-evident concept of existence and the specific existence that is the source of that concept. They then argue that this specific existence must be identical to the Necessary Being, using a reductio ad absurdum. This lesson walks through their logical steps and shows how the argument refutes an objection from the matn.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two senses of wujud
Identify the two meanings: (1) the self-evident concept (ma'na masdari) and (2) the specific existence (wujud khas) that is the source of that concept and the referent of predication. The argument focuses on the second sense.
The first sense is a secondary intelligible; the second is a concrete reality.
2
Formulate the conditional premise
The philosophers assert: 'If specific existence were separate from the wajib, then it would be an accident (arid).' This is because composition is impossible in the wajib, so any separate entity would inhere as an accident.
The condition is that wujud khas is not identical to the wajib.
3
Trace the reductio chain
From the accident status, it follows that wujud khas would need a substrate, making it possible (mumkin) and thus requiring a cause. The cause cannot be the essence itself (would entail priority) nor another (would entail dependence). Both lead to absurdity.
This is a chain of modus tollens steps.
4
Conclude the identity and rebut the objection
Since the consequent is absurd, the antecedent is false. Therefore specific existence is identical to the wajib. The author's objection (from the matn) is thus invalid because it fails to recognize the force of the reductio.
The objection is not specified here, but the commentator's refutation is given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الوجود يطلق ويراد به المعنى المصدري البديهي التصور، ويطلق ويراد به منشأ انتزاعه ومصداق حمله، ولا بد أن يكون موجودا، وإلا فيكون له منشأ أيضا، فهو المنشأ حقيقة ، وقد يقال له الوجود الخاص. قال الحكماء: إنه عين الواجب جل شأنه، وإلا لكان خارجا عارضا؛ لامتناع التركيب، وكل عارض محتاج إلى معروضه قطعا، فيكون ممكنا، فلا بد له من علة ، فإن كان نفس الذات يلزم تقدمها بالوجود عليه، وإن كان غيره فیلزم احتياجه تعالى إلى الغير، هذا خلف، وعلى هذا لا یرد ما أورده المصنف . وإن شئت قلت بعبارة أخرى الواجب ذاته كاف في انتزاع الوجود الذي هو بديهي التصور، وإلا لكان في اتصافه بالوجود محتاجا إلى الغير، وهو محال ، فيكون الوجود الخاص هو نفسه ، وهذا أيضا لا غبار عليه.
Know that 'existence' (al-wujud) is used to mean the infinitive meaning (al-masdari) which is self-evident in conception, and it is used to mean the source of its derivation and the referent of its predication, and it must be existent, otherwise there would be for it a source as well, so it is the source in reality, and it is sometimes called 'specific existence' (al-wujud al-khas). The philosophers said: It is identical to the Necessary One (al-Wajib), may He be exalted, otherwise it would be an external accident, because composition is impossible, and every accident necessarily needs its substrate, so it would be possible, and thus it would need a cause. If it were the essence itself, then the essence would precede it by existence, and if it were other than the essence, then the Exalted would need the other, which is absurd. On this basis, what the author raised does not apply. And if you wish, you can express it in another way: The essence of the Necessary is sufficient for the derivation of existence, which is self-evident in conception; otherwise, in its being qualified by existence it would need the other, which is impossible. So specific existence is His very self, and this also is without flaw.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage defines two senses of wujud and then presents the philosophers' argument that wujud is identical to the wajib. The first sense is the infinitive meaning (wujud as a concept of being), which is self-evident. The second sense is the specific existence that is the source of derivation of the concept and the referent of predication. This specific existence must itself be existent. The philosophers argue that this specific existence is identical to the wajib, otherwise it would be an accident, leading to composition and dependence. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if it were separate, it would be an accident needing a substrate, thus possible, thus needing a cause. This cause could be the essence itself (which would cause precedence) or other (which would make the wajib need another). Both are impossible, so wujud must be identical. The author's objection (from the matn) is shown to be invalid. The second formulation reinforces that the essence is sufficient for deriving the concept of existence, needing no external attribute.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' reductio proves that specific existence is identical to the Necessary Being, because separation would entail absurd consequences.

Resolving the Infinite Regress of Necessities: Real vs. Mental Relations
Author resolves an objection of infinite regress in the chain of necessities by distinguishing real from mental relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish real entities from mental abstractions to determine whether an infinite regress is problematic.

Core Idea
A mental relation does not generate a vicious regress because it ceases when the mind stops considering it.

This passage addresses a deep objection: if necessity (luzum) itself is necessary, then each necessity requires another, leading to an infinite chain. The author resolves this by showing that necessity is a mental abstraction (i'tibari intiza'i), not a real entity, so the regress is not infinite in reality; it terminates with the act of consideration. Understanding this distinction preserves the coherence of logical relations without collapsing into paradox.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection about infinite regress
The objector argues: if necessity (the relation) is necessary for the relation to hold, then we need a separate necessity for that necessity, and so on ad infinitum. This threatens the validity of all implications.
The objection assumes that each 'luzum' is a real entity that itself requires a further luzum.
2
Distinguish real entities from mental abstractions
Real entities exist independently; mental abstractions (like relations) exist only when the mind considers them. Ask: what kind of thing is 'necessity'? Is it a substance or an attribute?
In Islamic ontology, relations are often classified as i'tibari—mental constructs, not extramental.
3
Apply the solution: mental abstraction cuts the chain
The author says necessity is a posited abstract meaning (ma'na i'tibari intiza'i). Its existence is dependent on the mind's consideration. When consideration stops, the relation does not continue; hence no infinite regress in reality.
The chain is finite because each necessity is a mental act, not a real entity requiring another.
4
Anticipate the further objection: is it then invented?
If necessity is merely mental, is it arbitrary or invented? The author responds that the source (mansha') of the abstraction is real, preserving its objective basis. For example, the father-son relation is mental but based on real father and son.
The reality of the source ensures that the abstraction is not a pure fiction.
5
Understand the preservation of self-reality (nafs al-amriyya)
For abstractions, 'self-reality' means that the source exists independently, so the abstraction is valid even if the relation itself is mental. Thus, the regress is not impossible because the subject (the mental consideration) can be absent, not because the predicate is false.
This subtle point guards against confusing a regress in mental considerations with a regress in real existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس بالضَّرُورَةِ الأولية، فلا يحتاج إلى تنبيه، فضلا عن دليل. وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ لَازِمٌ للملزوم، وَإِلَّا فيمكن انفكاكه فيمكن انفكاك اللازم، وَهَذَا يَهْدِمُ أَصْلَ الْمُلَازَمَةِ التي بين اللازم والملزوم، وهكذا لزوم اللزوم، فَيَتَسَلْسَلُ اللُّزُومَاتُ. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ مِنَ الْمَعَانِي الِاعْتِبَارِيَّةِ الِانْتِزَاعِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَيْسَ لَهَا تَحَقَّقٌ في الذِّهْنِ بَعْدَ اعْتِبَارِهِ، فَيَنْقَطِعُ بِانْقِطَاعِ الاعْتِبَارِ، فلا تسلسل. وَكَانَ لِمُتَوَهِّمٍ أَنْ يَتَوَهَّمَ أَنَّ الِاعْتِبَارِيَّةَ تُوجِبُ الِاخْتِرَاعَ فَلَمْ تَكُنْ وَاقِعِيَّةً، فَأَجَابَ بِقَوْلِهِ: نَعَمْ، مَنْشَؤُهَا وَمَنْبَعُهَا مُتَحَقِّقٌ. وَالِاخْتِرَاعِيُّ مَا لَا يَكُونُ مَوْجُودًا بِنَفْسِهِ وَلَا بِمَنْشَئِهِ. وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْحَافِظُ لِنَفْسِ الْأَمْرِيَّةِ لِلِانْتِزَاعِيَّاتِ، مُتَنَاهِيَةً أَوْ غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ، مُرَتَّبَةً أَوْ غَيْرَ مُرَتَبَةٍ: فَإِنَّ مَعْنَى نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِيَّةِ لِلَانْتِزَاعِيَّاتِ كَوْنُ مَناشِئِهَا في ذاتِها مِنْ غَيْرِ اعْتِبَارِ الْمُعْتَبِرِ، بِحَيْثُ يَصِحُّ انْتِزَاعُ تِلْكَ الِانْتِزاعِيَّاتِ مِنْهَا. فَقَوْلُهُمْ: التَّسَلْسُلُ فِيهَا لَيْسَ بِمُحَالٍ صَادِقٌ بِعَدَمِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، لَا بِتَحَقُّقِهِ وَعَدَمِ ثُبُوتِ الْمَحْمُولِ. فَتَدَبَّرْ.
Chapter on the five universals. By primary necessity, so it does not need notification, let alone proof. And here is a doubt: that the necessitation is necessary for the necessitated, otherwise it could be separated, so the necessary could be separated, and this destroys the foundation of implication between the necessary and the necessitated, and likewise the necessity of the necessity, so the necessities chain. And its solution: that necessity is among the posited abstract meanings that have no realization in the mind after its consideration, so it is cut off by the cutting off of consideration, so there is no chain. And an objector might imagine that the posited entails invention, so it is not real. He answered by saying: Yes, its source and origin are realized. And the invented is what does not exist by itself nor by its source. And that is what preserves the self-reality for the abstractions, whether finite or infinite, ordered or unordered: for the meaning of self-reality for abstractions is that their sources are in themselves without the consideration of the considerer, such that the abstraction of those abstractions from them is valid. So their saying: the chain in them is not impossible is true by the absence of the subject, not by its realization and the lack of affirmation of the predicate. So ponder.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection against the concept of necessity (luzum) in logic: if necessity itself is necessary for the necessitated, then we have an infinite regress (tasalsul). The objection is that if necessity is not necessary, the implication can break, but if it is necessary, then each necessity requires another, leading to an endless chain. The commentator resolves this by noting that necessity is a posited abstract meaning (ma'na i'tibari intiza'i), not a real entity. Such meanings exist only in the mind as a relation after considering the terms. Thus, the chain is cut off when the mental consideration ends. A further worry is that if it is merely posited, it becomes invented (ikhtira'i) and not real. The answer is that the source (mansha') of these abstractions is real; for example, the relation of fatherhood has a real basis in the existence of father and child, even though the relation itself is mental. The self-reality (nafs al-amriyya) of abstractions means their sources exist independently, allowing valid abstraction. Thus, the claim that an infinite regress in these abstractions is not impossible is true only if the subject (i.e., the mental consideration) is absent, not if the subject exists and the predicate is false. This guards against confusing real infinite regress with merely mental infinite regress.

Key Terms
لزوم
اعتباري انتزاعي
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the infinite regress of necessities not pose a problem?
A) Because necessity is itself not necessary.
B) Because necessity is a real entity that terminates the chain.
C) Because necessity is a mental abstraction that ends when the mind stops considering.
D) Because the regress is actually finite.

Key Takeaway
An infinite regress in mental relations is not impossible because the chain terminates with the act of mental consideration; the real source of the abstraction ensures its validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Kalam argument: 'The world is originated; every originated thing requires a cause.' An objector says: 'If the cause itself is originated, it requires another cause, ad infinitum.' Use the distinction between real and mental relations to explain why this regress is not vicious if the 'requiring' is a mental relation that terminates with the First Cause (God).
?
In Usul al-Fiqh, jurists identify the 'illah (effective cause) for a ruling. Practice: is the 'illah of intoxication (iskr) a real attribute of substances or a mental relation? Argue why an infinite regress of 'illahs (e.g., a cause for every cause) is not a problem here using the i'tibari distinction.

Distinguishing the Absolute and Natural Universal
Author explains that the natural universal is broader than the absolute, and the division is not self-division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the absolute and natural universal by identifying whether the condition (qayd) restricts the essence or the act of consideration.

Core Idea
The natural universal is broader than the absolute because its condition restricts the act of consideration, not the essence itself.

In Islamic logic, the distinction between the absolute and natural universal is crucial for understanding how universals apply to their instances. This passage from the matn addresses a potential confusion that these two are identical, explaining why the natural is more general. It also presents Al-Qushji's insightful re-interpretation that the division is actually a difference in the mode of considering the essence. By mastering this distinction, you will be better equipped to analyse definitions and terms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Locate 'al-mutlaqa' (absolute) and 'al-tabi'i' (natural) in the Arabic. Note that both are types of universal (kulli). The passage focuses on their relationship.
The absolute universal is considered without any additional condition; the natural universal carries a condition regarding how it is considered.
2
Find the condition (haythiyya)
The term 'haythiyya' appears in the explanation. In the absolute universal, the condition is a restriction of the essence 'in the view' (fi al-lahaz). In the natural universal, the condition is a restriction 'of the view' (li al-lahaz).
The difference is subtle: whether the condition modifies the essence or the act of considering it.
3
Compare the scope
Because the natural universal restricts the act of consideration rather than the essence, it applies to both the absolute universal and its subdivisions (the conditioned and the abstracted). Thus, the natural is more general.
This is the key logical point: the natural universal has a broader extension.
4
Understand Al-Qushji's alternative
Al-Qushji argues that this is not a real division of a genus into species, but rather different ways of considering the same essence: with the condition of not being abstracted, mixed, or absolute.
This expands the discussion from a simple distinction to a methodological point about how we analyse concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كام لمتوهم أن يتوهم أن المطلقة والطبعي أمر واحد، فتقسيمه إليها تقسيم الشيء إلى نفسه وإلى غيره قال: (وَالطَّبَعِيُّ أَعَمُّ بِاعْتِبَارٍ مِنَ الْمُطْلَقَةِ (۱)؛ فإن الحيثية فيها قيد للماهية في اللحاظ في الطبيعي قيد لللحاظ فهي صادقة على المطلقة، وعلى قسميها . فَلَا يَلْزَمُ تَقْسِيمُ الشَّيْءِ إِلى نَفْسِهِ وَإِلَى غَيْرِهِ)، هذا والأظهر ما قال العلامة القوشجي إن هذا ليس تقسيما، بل اعتبارات لشيء؛ فإن الماهية قد يعتبر بشرط لا مجردة ، وقد يعتبر مخلوطة وقد يعتبر مطلقة ، هذا ، والله أعلم بالصواب.
And since there could be one who might imagine that the absolute and the natural are one thing, so dividing [the absolute] into them would be a division of a thing into itself and another, he said: 'The natural is more general in consideration than the absolute; because the whereness (al-haythiyya) in it is a restriction of the essence in the view, while in the natural it is a restriction of the view, so it is true of the absolute and its two divisions. Thus, the division of a thing into itself and into another does not necessarily follow.' This, and the most apparent is what 'Allama al-Qushji said: that this is not a division, but rather considerations of a thing; for the essence may be considered with a condition of not being abstracted, and may be considered mixed, and may be considered absolute. This, and Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential confusion: that the absolute and natural universal are identical. The author clarifies that the natural is more general in consideration. In the absolute, the qualification is a restriction of the essence itself in the act of consideration, while in the natural, the qualification is a restriction of the act of consideration. Therefore, the natural universal applies to the absolute and its two divisions (the conditioned and the abstracted). Hence, the division of the absolute into itself and another is not entailed. The author then presents al-Qushji's view that this is not a division at all, but different ways of considering the same essence: sometimes considered with the condition of not being abstracted, sometimes mixed, sometimes absolute. This is a typical logical discussion about the modes of considering quiddity.

Key Terms
المطلقة (al-mutlaqa)
الطبيعي (al-tabi'i)
الحيثية (al-haythiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the natural universal differ from the absolute universal?
A) The natural is a type of absolute
B) The natural is more general in consideration because its restriction is on the act of consideration, not the essence
C) The natural is less general than the absolute
D) They are identical

Key Takeaway
The natural universal is broader than the absolute because its condition restricts the act of consideration, while the absolute's condition restricts the essence within the consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'salat' (prayer). Is it an absolute universal or a natural universal when jurists discuss its obligation? Identify where the condition would lie: does it qualify the essence of prayer or the act of considering it?
?
Reconstruct Al-Qushji's argument for the term 'iman' (faith). Show how it can be considered as absolute, as conditioned by actions, or as abstracted from actions.

The Absolute: One in Essence, Many by Individuals
Defines the absolute and its unity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the absolute (al-mutlaqa) and explain how its unity is compatible with its multiplicity in individuals.

Core Idea
The absolute is one in essence but becomes many through individuals.

In this passage, the author introduces the absolute (al-mutlaqa), the universal nature considered in itself. He emphasizes that it is one in essence yet many accidentally due to individuals. This distinction is foundational for understanding how universals relate to particulars in Islamic logic and metaphysics. Let's unpack the definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: al-mutlaqa (المطلقة). Note that in this context it means the absolute universal nature, not an absolute proposition.
2
Extract the definition
The passage says: 'المطلقة في ذاتها واحدة' (the absolute is one in its essence) and 'وإن كانت كثيرة بالعرض بواسطة كثرة الأشخاص' (it is many accidentally by means of the multiplicity of individuals). So the definition is: one in essence, many by accident.
3
Analyze the qualification about unity
The author clarifies that this unity is not personal (شخصية), meaning it does not prevent sharing among individuals. This is crucial: the absolute's unity is not the unity of a particular person which excludes others; it is a universal unity that allows for multiple instantiations.
4
Relate existence to the absolute
The author says existence when related to the absolute is one; it becomes multiple only by the multiplicity of that to which it is added (the individuals). So existence itself is one but appears multiple through its relation to individuals.
5
Summarize the definition in your own words
Combine the points: the absolute is a universal nature that is one in its essence (as a concept) but becomes many when instantiated in various individuals. This is the basis for understanding how a universal can be predicated of many particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم: أن المطلقة في ذاتها واحدة، وإن كانت كثيرة بالعرض، بواسطة كثرة الأشخاص، وهذه الوحدة لیست شخصية ؛ حتى ينافي الاشتراك، فالوجود إذا نسب إليها واحد البتة ؛ إذ يتعدد إلا بتعدد المضاف إليه، وهو واحد، ويسمى هذا النحو من الوجود إلهيا ؛ لأنه بعناية الله فقط ، وهي متكثرة أيضا بتكثر الأشخاص، فوجوداتها أيضا متكثرة بتكثر ما أضيف إليه، وسمي هذا النحو من الوجود وجودا طبعيا، والطبيعة بهذا النحو من الوجود يتحقق بتحقق فرد وينتفي بانتفاء فرد، بخلافها بالنحو الأول منه ؛ فإنها يتحقق بتحقق فرد وينتفي بانتفاء جميع الأفراد، وهي بالوجود الإلهي متقدمة على نفسها بالوجود الطبيعي، لكن هذا النحو من التقدم متغاير للمتقدمات المشهورة الخمسة (1) وهي: التقدم بالزمان، والتقدم بالعلية، والتقدم بالطبع، والتقدم بالشرف، والتقدم بالرتبة. ثم المطلقة لها أحكام بالذات، باعتبار الوجود الإلهي، وأحكام باعتبار الوجود الطبعي، فالقبيلة الأولى لا يجب سريانها إلى الأفراد؛ لجواز كون الخصوصية أبية عنها والقبيلة الثانية ثابتة لها لا باعتبار الوجود الإلهي في الجملة ؛ لاتحادهما . فالمطلقة حاملة للنقيضين، ومن ههنا ترى الفلاسفة قاطبة يقولون: المهملتان لا تتناقضان والصوفية أيضا- كرم الله وجوههم، وكثرهم الله تعالى - يقولون إن الضدين يجتمعان في محل واحد، والمقصود أن السلب والإيجاب يجتمعان في موضوع الماهية الموجودة بالوجود الإلهي، وإنما الاختلاف في العبارات ، فاحفظ ؛ فإنه دقيق وبالحفظ حقيق، والصواب عند الله تعالى:
Know: that the absolute (al-mutlaqa) is one in its essence, though it is many accidentally, by means of the multiplicity of individuals. This unity is not personal, such that it would contradict sharing. So existence when related to it is absolutely one; for it becomes multiple only by the multiplicity of that to which it is added, which is one. This mode of existence is called divine because it is only by the care of God. It also becomes multiple by the multiplicity of individuals, so its existences also become multiple by the multiplicity of that to which it is added, and this mode of existence is called natural existence. Nature in this mode of existence is realized by the realization of an individual and is negated by the negation of an individual, unlike it in the first mode, for it is realized by the realization of an individual and is negated only by the negation of all individuals. It, by divine existence, is prior to itself by natural existence, but this type of priority is different from the well-known five priorities (1), which are: priority in time, priority in causality, priority in nature, priority in honor, and priority in rank. Then the absolute has essential judgments by virtue of divine existence, and judgments by virtue of natural existence. The first group does not necessarily apply to individuals because particularity may prevent it, and the second group is established for it, not by virtue of divine existence in general, because of their unity. So the absolute is a bearer of opposites. From here you see all the philosophers say: the two particulars are not contradictory, and the Sufis—may God ennoble their faces and multiply them—say that the two opposites can coexist in one locus. The intended meaning is that negation and affirmation coexist in the subject of the quiddity existing by divine existence; the difference is only in expressions. So memorize, for it is subtle and worthy of memorization. And the correct is with God Almighty.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory text about the concept of the absolute (al-mutlaqa) — the universal nature considered in itself. The author begins by stating that the absolute is one in essence but becomes many accidentally due to the multiplicity of individuals. This unity is not personal, meaning it does not prevent sharing among individuals. The existence relative to it is one, but when related to individuals, it becomes multiple. The author then introduces two modes of existence for this absolute: divine existence (al-wujud al-ilāhi) and natural existence (al-wujud al-ṭabī‘i). Divine existence is the absolute considered purely as an essence, without relation to individuals, and is called divine because it is only by God's care. Natural existence is the absolute as instantiated in multiple individuals, and is called natural because it follows the nature of individuals. A key distinction is that in the natural mode, the absolute is realized by the realization of an individual and negated by the negation of an individual — if one individual exists, the nature exists in that individual; if that individual perishes, the nature as a particular instance is gone. In the divine mode, however, the absolute is not negated by the perishing of one individual; it ceases to exist only if all individuals are negated. Thus, the absolute in its divine mode precedes its natural mode, but this priority is not one of the well-known five types of priority (time, causality, nature, honor, rank); it is a distinct type. Then the author explains that the absolute has two sets of judgments: those based on its divine existence (which do not necessarily extend to each individual, as particularity may prevent) and those based on its natural existence (which do extend to individuals, but not because of divine existence, since the two are united). Consequently, the absolute can be a subject for contradictory predicates: e.g., existence and non-existence, or universality and particularity. The philosophers say that two particular propositions (muhmalatan) are not contradictory, because they can both be true in different respects. The Sufis say that opposites can coexist in one locus, meaning affirmation and negation can coexist in the subject of the quiddity existing by divine existence. The difference is merely in wording. The author urges memorization due to the subtlety of the point.

Key Terms
المطلقة
الوجود الإلهي
الوجود الطبيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the unity of the absolute is not personal (شخصية) because:
A) It is a unique essence that cannot be shared.
B) It is compatible with being shared among individuals.
C) It is the same as the unity of a particular person.
D) It only applies in the divine mode.

Key Takeaway
The absolute is defined as one in essence but many accidentally, establishing that a universal can be one concept while having multiple instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the following phrase: 'الإنسان موجود'. Which aspect of the absolute (one or many) is being considered? Explain why.
?
Construct a definition of the universal 'الحركة' (motion) following the pattern: one in essence, many by individuals.

Divine and Natural Existence of the Absolute
Distinguishes divine and natural existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between divine existence and natural existence of the absolute and explain their different judgments.

Core Idea
The absolute has two modes: divine existence (pure essence) and natural existence (in individuals), each with its own logical behavior.

The author further distinguishes two modes of existence for the absolute: divine and natural. The absolute in the divine mode exists as a pure essence in God's knowledge, while in the natural mode it exists in external individuals. These modes determine how judgments apply to the absolute. Let's explore this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes
Find the terms: divine existence (الوجود الإلهي) and natural existence (الوجود الطبيعي). The author defines each.
2
Define divine existence
It is the existence of the absolute considered purely in itself, without relation to individuals. It is called divine because it is only by God's care (بعناية الله فقط).
3
Define natural existence
It is the existence of the absolute as instantiated in many individuals. It is called natural because it follows the nature of individuals.
4
Contrast their persistence
In natural mode, the absolute is realized by a single individual and negated by that individual's negation. In divine mode, it is only negated by the negation of all individuals. So the divine mode is more permanent.
5
Understand the two sets of judgments
The absolute has two kinds of attributes: those based on divine existence (which do not necessarily apply to each individual because particularity may prevent) and those based on natural existence (which apply to individuals but not because of divine existence, since the two are united in the absolute itself).

Source Text

Balancing Transcendence and Immanence: The Logical Groundwork of Islamic Theology
Author describes God as pure existence and the world as His manifestations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical definitions of tashbih and tanzih in this passage and explain how the author avoids both pure anthropomorphism and pure transcendence.

Core Idea
God is both transcendent and immanent; logic preserves the tension without collapsing into pantheism or deism.

This passage from a logic textbook opens not with a syllogism but with a theological preface. The author grounds the entire logical enterprise in the correct understanding of God's nature. Before you can define terms and argue validly, you must know who God is. Here, he presents God as pure existence, yet manifest in His creation, combining transcendence (tanzih) and similarity (tashbih). This balance is not fuzzy mysticism; it is a precise logical stance that any student of Islamic logic must grasp.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Find the Arabic terms for Necessary Being, determinations, transcendence, and similarity in the passage. Note how they are introduced and their definitions.
Terms: الواجب, التعينات, التشبيه, التنزيه
2
Extract the author's thesis on God's nature
The author says God is pure existence, and the world is His manifestations. Identify the phrases that express this: 'الواجب سبحانه موجود؛ إذ هو وجود بحت' and 'العالم كله شيون له وتعينات'.
3
Analyze the tashbih-tanzih balance
The author says God is 'منزه في نفسه' (transcendent) but also 'خلق مشبه بتعينات عدمية' (created anthropomorphized). How can both be true? He resolves it by saying creation is 'non-entity determinations' – similarity is only via non-existent aspects.
4
Evaluate logical consistency
Check if the definitions of tashbih and tanzih are mutually exclusive. The passage explicitly says 'فهو جامع بين التشبيه والتنزيه'. The logical task is to understand how tashbih and tanzih can coexist without contradiction.

ولتنوّر الكتاب بذكر الحكمة الحقة التي أوحي بها الرسل الكرام المخبرون الصادقون، وكوشف بها أولياء الله العظام، فاعلم أن الواجب سبحانه موجود ؛ إذ هو وجود بحت، وهو الوجود بنفسه، وما سواه ليس بشيء، والعالم كله شيون له تعالى وتعينات ،له جل مجده. وهو تعالى ظاهر فيه ، ونفسه واجب جاعل المتعينات ممكنة مجعولة في العين الواحد، ظهر في الكثرة، والكثرة إنما تغايره بالاعتبارات، ووجودها مستفاد منه، والموجود حقيقة هو سبحانه، فهو سبحانه وجود مطلق، لا بأن يكون الإطلاق قيدا فيه، ليس بكلي ولا جزئي ولا كثير، ولا شيء معه سواه، ولا شريك له في الوجود، وهو حق منزه في نفسه، وواجب بالذات، وهو خلق مشبه بتعينات عدمية، والواجب لا يكون ممكنا ولا الممكن واجبا، فهو جامع بين التشبيه
And to illuminate the book by mentioning the true wisdom that was revealed by the noble messengers, the truthful informers, and uncovered by the great saints of God, know that the Necessary, glory be to Him, exists; for He is pure existence, existence by Himself; other than Him is nothing. The whole world is manifestations of Him and determinations. He is manifest in it, and His Self is the Necessary maker of the determinations as possible, made in the single eye, manifest in multiplicity, and multiplicity differs from Him only by considerations; their existence is derived from Him. The true existent is He, glory be to Him. So He is absolute existence, not meaning that absoluteness is a restriction in Him; He is not universal nor particular nor many, and nothing is with Him besides Him. He is truth, transcendent in His essence, necessary by essence, and He created anthropomorphized by non-entity determinations; the necessary cannot be possible nor the possible necessary. So He combines both tashbih and tanzih.

Commentary
This passage serves as a theological preface, likely introducing a discussion on the five universals. The author states that his purpose is to illuminate the book with true wisdom from revelation and unveiling. He then expounds on the nature of the Necessary Being (al-wajib): God is pure existence, existence by Himself; everything else is nothing in itself. The world is described as 'manifestations' (shuyun) and 'determinations' (ta'yyinat) of God. God is manifest in the world, and multiplicity is only by consideration, not real distinctness. God is absolute existence, but not in the sense that 'absoluteness' is a restrictive attribute; He is not a universal, particular, or many. He is transcendent in His essence and Necessary by essence. Despite this transcendence, He 'creates anthropomorphized by non-entity determinations' (khalaqa mushabbahan bi ta'yyinat 'adamiyya), meaning that creation is similar to Him in some way but through non-existent determinations. Thus God combines both tashbih (similarity) and tanzih (transcendence). The passage sets up the tension between transcendence and immanence that will be addressed in the following section.

The Definer and its Types: Real vs Verbal Definition
Author defines the definer and distinguishes real versus verbal definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real (haqiqi) and verbal (lafzi) definitions and identify the condition that the definer must be clearer than the defined.

Core Idea
A definition must provide new knowledge; thus it cannot use terms equally unclear or circular.

In this passage, the author establishes the fundamental concept of a 'definer' (mu'arraf) in Islamic logic. A definer is not just any description—it must serve a specific purpose: either to form a mental image of something not yet known (taswir) or to interpret the meaning of a word (tafsir). This distinction gives rise to two main types of definition: real (haqiqi) and verbal (lafzi). A crucial condition is that the definer must be more evident (ajla) than the defined; otherwise, it fails to increase knowledge. Understanding this condition helps you avoid circular or uninformative definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two purposes of definition
The author states that a definer is predicated 'for formation (taswir) or for interpretation (tafsir).' Taswir means acquiring a new mental concept; tafsir means clarifying the meaning of a word. Both are legitimate but serve different functions.
Taswir corresponds to real definition; tafsir corresponds to verbal definition.
2
Distinguish real from verbal definition
A real definition (ta'rif haqiqi) provides a new form (sura) that was not previously present. If the form's existence in external reality is known, it is 'according to reality' (bi hasab al-haqiqa); if not, it is 'according to the name' (bi hasab al-ism). A verbal definition (ta'rif lafzi) merely explains the meaning of a word without providing a new concept.
The author refines the initial statement: a definer is 'that which provides the formation of an unattained matter or the bringing to mind of the word's meaning.'
3
Recognize the condition of clarity (ajla)
The definer must be clearer (ajla) than the defined because it is the means of acquiring knowledge. If the definer is equally unclear, it adds no new information. This is a logical condition: you cannot explain something unknown by something equally unknown.
The author explicitly forbids definition by what is equal (musawi) in clarity.
4
Apply the condition to an example
The author gives the example of defining a relative (mudaf) by its correlative. A relative is understood only in relation to its counterpart; defining one by the other is circular and violates the clarity condition. This illustrates why such definitions are invalid.
Example: defining 'father' as 'parent of a child' and 'child' as 'offspring of a father' would be circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعرف
مُعَرِّفُ الشَّيْءِ مَا يُحْمَلُ، أي يصح أن يحمل عَلَيْهِ، أي على ذلك الشيء تَصْوِيرًا، أي لأجل التصوير؛ تَحْصِيلًا لغير حاصل، أَوْ تَفْسِيرًا لمدلول اللفظ. والأولى أن يقال: ما يفيد تصوير أمر غير حاصل أو إحضار مدلول اللفظ. وَالثَّانِي اللَّفْظِيُّ، وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ، فَفِيهِ تَحْصِيلُ صُورَةٍ غَيْرِ حَاصِلَةٍ، فَإِنْ عُلِمَ وُجُودُهَا، أي وجود تلك الصورة، أي معلومها في الخارج فَهُوَ بِحَسَبِ الْحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِلَّا علِم فَبِحَسَبِ الإِسْم. وَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُعَرِّفُ أَجْلَى من المعرَّف؛ لأنه كاسب له، فَلَا يَصِحُ بِالْمُسَاوِي مَعْرِفَةً وَجَهَالَةٌ، فلا يصح تعريف مضايف بمضايفه.
Chapter on the Definer. The definer of a thing is that which is predicated, i.e., it is correct to predicate of it, i.e., of that thing, for formation (taswir), i.e., for the sake of formation, to acquire something not yet acquired, or for interpretation (tafsir) of the meaning of the word. It is better to say: that which provides the formation of an unattained matter or the bringing to mind of the word's meaning. The second is verbal, and the first is real. In it is the acquisition of a form not yet acquired. If its existence is known, i.e., the existence of that form, i.e., its known in the external, then it is according to reality; otherwise it is according to the name. The definer must be clearer than the defined, because it is an acquirer for it. Therefore definition by what is equal (musawi) is not valid for knowledge while there is ignorance. So it is not valid to define a relative (mudaf) by its correlative.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'definer' (mu'arraf) and classifies its types. The author begins by stating that the definer is that which is predicated of the thing for the purpose of formation (taswir) or interpretation (tafsir). Formation means acquiring a mental form that was not previously present; interpretation means clarifying the meaning of a word. He then notes that the preferable definition is 'that which provides formation of an unattained matter or brings to mind the word's meaning.' The second type (tafsir) is called verbal definition, and the first (taswir) is called real definition. Within real definition, if the existence of the form is known in external reality, it is a definition according to reality (haqiqa); otherwise it is according to name (ism). A crucial condition is that the definer must be clearer (ajla) than the defined, because it is the means of acquiring knowledge. Therefore, defining by something that is equal in clarity (musawi) is invalid, as that would be a vicious circle. For example, one cannot define a relative (mudaf) by its correlative, since each requires the other.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must provide new knowledge; therefore the definer must be clearer than the defined, and circular or equally unclear definitions are invalid.

The Definer: Real vs Verbal
Defines the definer and its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real definer (ḥaqīqī) and a verbal definer (lafẓī) and identify their respective purposes.

Core Idea
A definer either creates a new concept or clarifies a known term.

In this passage, the author defines the definer (muʿarraf) as that which is predicated of a thing for conceptualization or explanation. He then divides it into two types: real and verbal. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying definitions in Islamic sciences, where defining terms precisely is fundamental.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definer
In a given definition, locate the term being defined (the definiendum) and the expression used to define it (the definer).
For example, in 'al-insān is a rational animal', 'al-insān' is the definiendum and 'rational animal' is the definer.
2
Determine the purpose of the definer
Ask: does the definer aim to produce a new mental form of something unknown (taṣwīr), or does it merely clarify the meaning of an already known word (tafsīr)?
If the concept is new, it is real; if it clarifies a word, it is verbal.
3
Classify as real or verbal
If the definer brings about a new conceptual form, it is a real definer (ḥaqīqī). If it only explains the meaning of a word, it is verbal (lafẓī).
The passage also mentions that if the external existence of the form is known, the definition is according to reality; otherwise it is nominal.
4
Assess clarity
The definer must be clearer than the defined, otherwise it fails its purpose. Check if the definer is more understandable than the definiendum.
This condition applies to both real and verbal definers; the definer is the 'acquirer' of knowledge.

فصل في المعرف
(مُعَرِّفُ الشَّيْءِ مَا يُحْمَلُ، أي يصح أن يحمل (عَلَيْهِ)، أي على ذلك الشيء (تَصْوِيرًا)، أي لأجل التصوير؛ [تَحْصِيلًا](۱) لغير حاصل، (أَوْ تَفْسِيرًا) لمدلول اللفظ والأولى أن يقال: ما يفيد تصوير أمر غير حاصل أو إحضار مدلول اللفظ (٢). [وَ] الثَّانِي اللَّفْظِيُّ، وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ (۳)، فَفِيهِ تَحْصِيلُ صُورَةٍ غَيْرِ حَاصِلَةٍ، فَإِنْ عُلِمَ وُجُودُهَا، أي وجود تلك الصورة، أي معلومها في الخارج (فَهُوَ بِحَسَبِ الْحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِلَّا) علِم فَبِحَسَبِ الإِسْم. وَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُعَرِّفُ أَجْلَى) من المعرَّف؛ لأنه كاسب له، (فَلَا يَصِحُ بِالْمُسَاوِي مَعْرِفَةً [وَجَهَالَةٌ ] (٤) ، فلا يصح تعريف مضايف بمضايفه.
(وَ [لا] بِالْأَخْفَى)، وهذا أردأ، ومن ههنا يظهر عدم صحة التعريف الدوائر، وعدم استعمال اللفظ الوحشي والمشترك والمجاز بلا قرينة، وهذا الشرط متفق عليه بين القدماء والمتأخرين.
(وَ لا بُدَّ] أَنْ يَكُونَ مُسَاوِيًا للمعرَّف في الصدق، (فَيَجِبُ الاِطَّرَادُ)، أي المنع عن دخول أفراد غير المعرَّف فيه، (وَالانْعِكَاسُ)، أي الجمع لجميع أفراد المعرَّف كلها، فَلَا يَصِحُ التَّعْرِيفُ ] (۱) بِالْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصَّ).
ولما كان لقائل أن يقول: إن هذا منقوض بالتعريف بالمثال، كما يقول الأدباء الاسم كزيد أجاب بقوله: (وَالتَّعْرِيفُ بِالْمِثَالِ تَعْرِيفٌ بِالْمُشَابَهَةِ الْمُخْتَصَّةِ)، فمرادهم ما كان كزيد في الاستقلال وعدم الاقتران، هذا ما عليه المتأخرون، وَالْحَقُّ جَوَازُهُ بِالْأَعَمّ، وعليه المتقدمون، من عدم اشتراط المساواة. قالوا: إن كان الغرض تحصيل ماهية المعرف فلا يجوز إلا بجميع الأجزاء، وإن كان الغرض الامتياز عن كل ما عداه فقط فلا يجوز إلا بالمساوي أو الأخص، إن لم يكن الأعم ذاتيا له، وإن كان الغرض الامتياز عن بعض الأغيار فيجوز بالأعم والأخص والمساوي، وأما المباين فإن كان يوصل إلى الماهية فيجوز، وإن كان يورث الامتياز فلا حجر في التعريف به، لكنه نادر جدا.
ووجه حقيَّة (۲) هذا المذهب ظاهر؛ فإن الحاجة إلى جميع الأقسام المذكورة ثابتة، فإسقاط البعض عن درجة الاعتبار غير لائق.
(وَهُوَ)، أي المعرف (حَدٌ، إِنْ كَانَ الْمُمَيَّزُ) المذكور فيه (ذَاتِيَّا)
Section on the Definer
(The definer of a thing is what is predicated, i.e., it is valid to predicate (upon it), i.e., upon that thing (for conceptualization), i.e., for conceptualization; [to obtain] something not yet obtained, (or for explanation) of the meaning of the word. The better way is to say: what benefits the conceptualization of something not yet obtained or the bringing to mind of the meaning of the word. The second is verbal, and the first is real. So in it there is obtaining a form not yet obtained. If its existence is known, i.e., the existence of that form, i.e., its known thing in the external world, (then it is according to reality, and if not) known, then according to name. And it is necessary that the definer be clearer than the defined; because it is the acquirer of it. (So definition by the equal is not valid [while ignorance remains]), so it is not valid to define a correlate by its correlate. (And [not] by the more obscure), and this is worse. From here the invalidity of circular definition appears, and the non-use of strange words, homonyms, and metaphor without context. This condition is agreed upon between the earlier and later scholars. (And it is necessary) that it be coextensive with the defined in truth, (so universality is required), i.e., prevention of entry of individuals not belonging to the defined into it, (and convertibility), i.e., inclusion of all individuals of the defined entirely. So definition (by the more general and the more specific is not valid). And since someone could say: This is refuted by definition by example, as literary scholars say "name like Zaid", he answered by saying: (And definition by example is a definition by specific resemblance), so their intent is something like Zaid in independence and lack of conjunction. This is what the later scholars hold. But the truth is permissibility of definition by the more general, and the earlier scholars held that, from the non-requirement of coextension. They said: If the purpose is to obtain the quiddity of the defined, then it is not permissible except with all the parts; and if the purpose is distinction from everything other than it only, then it is not permissible except with the coextensive or the more specific, unless the more general is essential to it; and if the purpose is distinction from some others, then it is permissible with the more general, the more specific, and the coextensive. As for the completely different, if it leads to the quiddity then it is permissible, and if it yields distinction then there is no obstacle to defining with it, but it is very rare. And the correctness of this school is apparent, because the need for all the mentioned categories is established, so dropping some from the degree of consideration is not appropriate. (And it), i.e., the definer, (is a definition by essential properties if the distinguisher) mentioned in it (is essential).
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage from Bahr Ulum defines what a definer (mu'arraf) is and lays out the conditions for correct definition. The author begins by stating that the definer of something is that which is predicated of it for the purpose of conceptualization (taswir) or explanation (tafsir). He notes two types: a real definer (haqiqi) which brings about a new mental form of something previously unknown, and a verbal definer (lafzi) which merely clarifies the meaning of a word. If the existence of the form is already known externally, the definition is according to reality; otherwise it is merely nominal. The first essential condition is that the definer must be clearer (ajla) than the defined, since it serves to acquire knowledge of the defined. Thus defining by something equally clear (al-musawi) or more obscure (al-akhfa) is invalid. Circular definitions and the use of rare words, homonyms, or metaphor without context are also invalid. This condition is universally agreed upon. The second condition is coextension (musawa) in truth: the definer must apply to exactly the same things as the defined. This is expressed by two technical terms: ittirāḍ (universal applicability, i.e., no counterexamples) and inʿikās (convertibility, i.e., covering all individuals of the defined). Hence defining by something more general or more specific is invalid. However, the author then discusses a potential objection concerning definition by example (bi'l-mithāl), such as when literary scholars say 'a name like Zaid'. The response is that such definitions work by specific resemblance (al-mushabaha al-mukhtaṣṣa), meaning the example shares the relevant property without being coextensive. The author then notes a difference between earlier and later scholars: the earlier ones allow definition by the more general depending on the purpose. If the goal is to obtain the quiddity (māhiyya) of the defined, all essential parts are needed; if only to distinguish it from everything else, coextension or specificity is required; if only to distinguish it from some others, any of the three may suffice. Even a completely different term (mubāyin) can be used if it leads to the quiddity, though rarely. The author finds this earlier view more correct because definitions serve various needs. Finally, the passage introduces the two main types of definition: a ḥadd (definition by essence) if the distinguisher is an essential property (dhātī), and by implication (since the text breaks off) a rasm if the distinguisher is accidental. This prepares the reader for the subsequent discussion of the five universals and their roles in definition.

Key Terms
المعرف
التصوير
التفسير
الحقيقي
اللفظي

Conditions of a Valid Definer: Clarity and Coextension
Lays out conditions of clarity and coextension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of clarity and coextension (ittirād and ʿinʿikās) to evaluate the validity of any definition.

Core Idea
A valid definer must be clearer than the defined and exactly match its extension.

The passage lays out two essential conditions: the definer must be clearer (ajlā) than the defined, and it must be coextensive (musāwī) with it in truth. These conditions ensure that the definition succeeds in conveying understanding without confusion or ambiguity. The condition of coextension is further expressed by two technical terms: ittirād (preventing non-members from entering) and ʿinʿikās (including all members).

Guided Path
1
Check clarity of the definer
The definer must be easier to understand than the defined. If it is equally clear or more obscure, the definition fails. This also rules out circular definitions and the use of rare words, homonyms, or metaphors without context.
The passage emphasizes this condition is agreed upon by earlier and later scholars.
2
Verify ittirād (non-inclusion of non-members)
Ensure that the definer does not apply to any individual that is not part of the defined. In other words, the definer should be no broader than the defined.
For example, defining 'man' as 'animal' would fail ittirād because 'animal' includes non-human animals.
3
Verify ʿinʿikās (inclusion of all members)
Ensure that the definer applies to every individual of the defined. The definer should be no narrower than the defined.
For example, defining 'man' as 'rational' would fail ʿinʿikās because rationality is a property, not a category covering all humans? Actually better: defining 'man' as 'having two legs' would fail ʿinʿikās because some humans lose legs, but still are human. But standard: 'rational animal' includes all humans.
4
Check coextension
The definer must satisfy both ittirād and ʿinʿikās simultaneously. If it does, the definer is coextensive (musāwī) with the defined.
If either condition fails, the definer is either too broad or too narrow, and thus invalid according to the later scholars.
