فصل في المعرف
(مُعَرِّفُ الشَّيْءِ مَا يُحْمَلُ، أي يصح أن يحمل (عَلَيْهِ)، أي على ذلك الشيء (تَصْوِيرًا)، أي لأجل التصوير؛ [تَحْصِيلًا](۱) لغير حاصل، (أَوْ تَفْسِيرًا) لمدلول اللفظ والأولى أن يقال: ما يفيد تصوير أمر غير حاصل أو إحضار مدلول اللفظ (٢). [وَ] الثَّانِي اللَّفْظِيُّ، وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ (۳)، فَفِيهِ تَحْصِيلُ صُورَةٍ غَيْرِ حَاصِلَةٍ، فَإِنْ عُلِمَ وُجُودُهَا، أي وجود تلك الصورة، أي معلومها في الخارج (فَهُوَ بِحَسَبِ الْحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِلَّا) علِم فَبِحَسَبِ الإِسْم. وَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُعَرِّفُ أَجْلَى) من المعرَّف؛ لأنه كاسب له، (فَلَا يَصِحُ بِالْمُسَاوِي مَعْرِفَةً [وَجَهَالَةٌ ] (٤) ، فلا يصح تعريف مضايف بمضايفه.
(وَ [لا] بِالْأَخْفَى)، وهذا أردأ، ومن ههنا يظهر عدم صحة التعريف الدوائر، وعدم استعمال اللفظ الوحشي والمشترك والمجاز بلا قرينة، وهذا الشرط متفق عليه بين القدماء والمتأخرين.
(وَ لا بُدَّ] أَنْ يَكُونَ مُسَاوِيًا للمعرَّف في الصدق، (فَيَجِبُ الاِطَّرَادُ)، أي المنع عن دخول أفراد غير المعرَّف فيه، (وَالانْعِكَاسُ)، أي الجمع لجميع أفراد المعرَّف كلها، فَلَا يَصِحُ التَّعْرِيفُ ] (۱) بِالْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصَّ).
ولما كان لقائل أن يقول: إن هذا منقوض بالتعريف بالمثال، كما يقول الأدباء الاسم كزيد أجاب بقوله: (وَالتَّعْرِيفُ بِالْمِثَالِ تَعْرِيفٌ بِالْمُشَابَهَةِ الْمُخْتَصَّةِ)، فمرادهم ما كان كزيد في الاستقلال وعدم الاقتران، هذا ما عليه المتأخرون، وَالْحَقُّ جَوَازُهُ بِالْأَعَمّ، وعليه المتقدمون، من عدم اشتراط المساواة. قالوا: إن كان الغرض تحصيل ماهية المعرف فلا يجوز إلا بجميع الأجزاء، وإن كان الغرض الامتياز عن كل ما عداه فقط فلا يجوز إلا بالمساوي أو الأخص، إن لم يكن الأعم ذاتيا له، وإن كان الغرض الامتياز عن بعض الأغيار فيجوز بالأعم والأخص والمساوي، وأما المباين فإن كان يوصل إلى الماهية فيجوز، وإن كان يورث الامتياز فلا حجر في التعريف به، لكنه نادر جدا.
ووجه حقيَّة (۲) هذا المذهب ظاهر؛ فإن الحاجة إلى جميع الأقسام المذكورة ثابتة، فإسقاط البعض عن درجة الاعتبار غير لائق.
(وَهُوَ)، أي المعرف (حَدٌ، إِنْ كَانَ الْمُمَيَّزُ) المذكور فيه (ذَاتِيَّا)
Section on the Definer
(The definer of a thing is what is predicated, i.e., it is valid to predicate (upon it), i.e., upon that thing (for conceptualization), i.e., for conceptualization; [to obtain] something not yet obtained, (or for explanation) of the meaning of the word. The better way is to say: what benefits the conceptualization of something not yet obtained or the bringing to mind of the meaning of the word. The second is verbal, and the first is real. So in it there is obtaining a form not yet obtained. If its existence is known, i.e., the existence of that form, i.e., its known thing in the external world, (then it is according to reality, and if not) known, then according to name. And it is necessary that the definer be clearer than the defined; because it is the acquirer of it. (So definition by the equal is not valid [while ignorance remains]), so it is not valid to define a correlate by its correlate. (And [not] by the more obscure), and this is worse. From here the invalidity of circular definition appears, and the non-use of strange words, homonyms, and metaphor without context. This condition is agreed upon between the earlier and later scholars. (And it is necessary) that it be coextensive with the defined in truth, (so universality is required), i.e., prevention of entry of individuals not belonging to the defined into it, (and convertibility), i.e., inclusion of all individuals of the defined entirely. So definition (by the more general and the more specific is not valid). And since someone could say: This is refuted by definition by example, as literary scholars say "name like Zaid", he answered by saying: (And definition by example is a definition by specific resemblance), so their intent is something like Zaid in independence and lack of conjunction. This is what the later scholars hold. But the truth is permissibility of definition by the more general, and the earlier scholars held that, from the non-requirement of coextension. They said: If the purpose is to obtain the quiddity of the defined, then it is not permissible except with all the parts; and if the purpose is distinction from everything other than it only, then it is not permissible except with the coextensive or the more specific, unless the more general is essential to it; and if the purpose is distinction from some others, then it is permissible with the more general, the more specific, and the coextensive. As for the completely different, if it leads to the quiddity then it is permissible, and if it yields distinction then there is no obstacle to defining with it, but it is very rare. And the correctness of this school is apparent, because the need for all the mentioned categories is established, so dropping some from the degree of consideration is not appropriate. (And it), i.e., the definer, (is a definition by essential properties if the distinguisher) mentioned in it (is essential).
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage from Bahr Ulum defines what a definer (mu'arraf) is and lays out the conditions for correct definition. The author begins by stating that the definer of something is that which is predicated of it for the purpose of conceptualization (taswir) or explanation (tafsir). He notes two types: a real definer (haqiqi) which brings about a new mental form of something previously unknown, and a verbal definer (lafzi) which merely clarifies the meaning of a word. If the existence of the form is already known externally, the definition is according to reality; otherwise it is merely nominal. The first essential condition is that the definer must be clearer (ajla) than the defined, since it serves to acquire knowledge of the defined. Thus defining by something equally clear (al-musawi) or more obscure (al-akhfa) is invalid. Circular definitions and the use of rare words, homonyms, or metaphor without context are also invalid. This condition is universally agreed upon. The second condition is coextension (musawa) in truth: the definer must apply to exactly the same things as the defined. This is expressed by two technical terms: ittirāḍ (universal applicability, i.e., no counterexamples) and inʿikās (convertibility, i.e., covering all individuals of the defined). Hence defining by something more general or more specific is invalid. However, the author then discusses a potential objection concerning definition by example (bi'l-mithāl), such as when literary scholars say 'a name like Zaid'. The response is that such definitions work by specific resemblance (al-mushabaha al-mukhtaṣṣa), meaning the example shares the relevant property without being coextensive. The author then notes a difference between earlier and later scholars: the earlier ones allow definition by the more general depending on the purpose. If the goal is to obtain the quiddity (māhiyya) of the defined, all essential parts are needed; if only to distinguish it from everything else, coextension or specificity is required; if only to distinguish it from some others, any of the three may suffice. Even a completely different term (mubāyin) can be used if it leads to the quiddity, though rarely. The author finds this earlier view more correct because definitions serve various needs. Finally, the passage introduces the two main types of definition: a ḥadd (definition by essence) if the distinguisher is an essential property (dhātī), and by implication (since the text breaks off) a rasm if the distinguisher is accidental. This prepares the reader for the subsequent discussion of the five universals and their roles in definition.

Key Terms
الاطراد
الانعكاس
المساوي
الأعم
الأخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is definition by a more general term (al-aʿamm) invalid?
A) Because it violates ittirād (includes non-members).
B) Because it violates ʿinʿikās (excludes some members).
C) Because it is always more obscure.
D) Because it leads to circularity.

Key Takeaway
A valid definer must be clearer than the defined and coextensive with it, satisfying both ittirād and ʿinʿikās.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Evaluate the definition 'Ghusl is washing the whole body with water' against the conditions of clarity and coextension. Does it satisfy ittirād? Does it satisfy ʿinʿikās? Why or why not?
?
Find a definition from a classical mantiq text and check if it meets both conditions.

What Is Mantiq?

The Dispute on Definition by a More General Term
Discusses the dispute about definition by a more general term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the earlier scholars' position on definition by a more general term and identify the purposes for which it is permissible.

Core Idea
Earlier scholars allowed definition by a'amm when the purpose is partial distinction, not complete essence.

The passage raises an objection: if strict coextension is required, how can definition by example be valid? The author then presents the view of earlier scholars who allowed definition by a more general term depending on the purpose of the definition. Understanding this dispute clarifies that definition serves multiple cognitive goals, and the strict condition of coextension is not absolute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection to strict coextension
An objector points out that definition by example (like saying 'a name like Zaid') contradicts the requirement of coextension, since the example is neither more general nor more specific in a strict sense.
The objection challenges the later scholars who insist on strict coextension.
2
Understand the response: definition by example
The author explains that definition by example works by 'specific resemblance' (al-mushābaha al-mukhtaṣṣa): the example shares the intended property (e.g., independence) without being coextensive with the defined.
This response defends the later position by showing that example definitions are not true counterexamples.
3
Distinguish between later and earlier scholars
Later scholars require strict coextension; earlier scholars permit definition by a more general term depending on the purpose. The author sides with the earlier scholars, finding their view more practical.
The earlier scholars' position allows flexibility based on the goal of the definition.
4
List the three purposes of definition
The earlier scholars identify three purposes: (1) obtaining the quiddity (māhiyya) — requires all parts; (2) distinguishing from everything else — requires coextension or specificity; (3) distinguishing from some others — allows any of general, specific, or coextensive.
If the purpose is only partial distinction, a more general definer suffices.
5
Apply the earlier criteria
Given a definition, determine its purpose. If it aims only to distinguish from certain others, a more general definer is permissible. This validates many practical definitions used in Islamic sciences.
Even a completely different (mubāyin) term can be used if it leads to the quiddity, though rarely.

(The definer of a thing is what is predicated, i.e., it is valid to predicate (upon it), i.e., upon that thing (for conceptualization), i.e., for conceptualization; [to obtain] something not yet obtained, (or for explanation) of the meaning of the word. Commentary
This passage from Bahr Ulum defines what a definer (mu'arraf) is and lays out the conditions for correct definition. The author begins by stating that the definer of something is that which is predicated of it for the purpose of conceptualization (taswir) or explanation (tafsir). He notes two types: a real definer (haqiqi) which brings about a new mental form of something previously unknown, and a verbal definer (lafzi) which merely clarifies the meaning of a word. If the existence of the form is already known externally, the definition is according to reality; otherwise it is merely nominal. The first essential condition is that the definer must be clearer (ajla) than the defined, since it serves to acquire knowledge of the defined. Thus defining by something equally clear (al-musawi) or more obscure (al-akhfa) is invalid. Circular definitions and the use of rare words, homonyms, or metaphor without context are also invalid. This condition is universally agreed upon. The second condition is coextension (musawa) in truth: the definer must apply to exactly the same things as the defined. This is expressed by two technical terms: ittirāḍ (universal applicability, i.e., no counterexamples) and inʿikās (convertibility, i.e., covering all individuals of the defined). Hence defining by something more general or more specific is invalid. However, the author then discusses a potential objection concerning definition by example (bi'l-mithāl), such as when literary scholars say 'a name like Zaid'. The response is that such definitions work by specific resemblance (al-mushabaha al-mukhtaṣṣa), meaning the example shares the relevant property without being coextensive. The author then notes a difference between earlier and later scholars: the earlier ones allow definition by the more general depending on the purpose. If the goal is to obtain the quiddity (māhiyya) of the defined, all essential parts are needed; if only to distinguish it from everything else, coextension or specificity is required; if only to distinguish it from some others, any of the three may suffice. Even a completely different term (mubāyin) can be used if it leads to the quiddity, though rarely. The author finds this earlier view more correct because definitions serve various needs. Finally, the passage introduces the two main types of definition: a ḥadd (definition by essence) if the distinguisher is an essential property (dhātī), and by implication (since the text breaks off) a rasm if the distinguisher is accidental. This prepares the reader for the subsequent discussion of the five universals and their roles in definition.

Checkpoint
According to the earlier scholars, under which condition is definition by a more general term (al-aʿamm) permissible?
A) When the purpose is to obtain the quiddity of the defined.
B) When the purpose is to distinguish the defined from everything else.
C) When the purpose is to distinguish the defined from some others only.
D) Never, because it always violates coextension.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of definition by a more general term depends on the purpose; earlier scholars allowed it for partial distinction, while later scholars required strict coextension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the definition 'Al-Khamr is any intoxicating beverage' which is a definition by a more general term (since not all intoxicating beverages are khamr in the specific sense), explain under which purpose of the earlier scholars this definition would be permissible.

What Is Mantiq?

Definition (Hadd) vs Description (Rasm): Complete and Incomplete
Defines hadd and rasm by essential vs accidental, and outlines complete/incomplete forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any definiens as a hadd or rasm and determine whether it is complete or incomplete based on the inclusion of proximate genus and differentia.

Core Idea
A definition reveals essence; a description uses accidents. Only complete definitions include proximate genus and differentia.

In Islamic logic, definitions (hadd) and descriptions (rasm) are the two ways to define a term. This passage from a classical text explains how to distinguish them by their attributes and the conditions for completeness. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of distinction
Find in the Arabic where the author says 'if it is essential, it is a definition; if accidental, a description.' Note the two schools of thought mentioned.
2
Check for completeness
Determine if the definition includes the proximate genus. If it does, it's complete; if not, incomplete. Apply this to the example of 'rational animal'.
3
Understand the complete definition
Recognize that a complete definition requires both proximate genus and differentia, leading to the essence (al-kunn).
4
Note the truth (al-haqq)
The author says the true complete definition includes all parts, but logicians exclude external parts. Understand why external parts are excluded.
5
Apply to a real example
Try defining 'human' as 'rational animal' and evaluate if it is a complete hadd or a description. State which part is genus and which is differentia.

وَإِلَّا يَكُنْ ذَاتِيًّا بَلْ عَرَضِيًّا فَهُوَ رَسْمٌ، هَذَا مَا عَلَيْهِ الْمُتَأَخِّرُونَ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى رَأْيِ الْقُدَمَاءِ إِنْ كَانَ بِالذَّاتِيَّاتِ حَدٌّ، وَإِنْ كَانَ بِالْعَوَارِضِ رَسْمٌ، وَهُوَ تَامٌ إِنِ اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى الْجِنْسِ الْقَرِيبِ حَدًّا كَانَ أَوْ رَسْمًا، وَإِلَّا يَشْتَمِلُ عَلَيْهِ فَنَاقِصٌ، فَالْحَدُّ التَّامُّ مَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى الْجِنْسِ وَالْفَصْلِ الْقَرِيبَيْنِ، وَهُوَ الْمُوصِلُ إِلَى الْكُنْهِ، هَذَا مَا هُوَ الْمَشْهُورُ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ مَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى جَمِيعِ الْأَجْزَاءِ، خَارِجِيَّةً أَوْ عَقْلِيَّةً، لَكِنَّ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ لَمْ يَعْتَبِرُوا التَّحْدِيدَ بِالْأَجْزَاءِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ؛ لِعَدَمِ إِيصَالِهِ إِلَى مَجْهُولٍ. وَيُسْتَحْسَنُ تَقْدِيمُ الْجِنْسِ عَلَى الْفَصْلِ فِي الْحَدِّ التَّامِّ، وَيَجِبُ تَقْيِيدُ أَحَدِهِمَا بِالْآخَرِ، لَا عَلَى أَنَّهُ خَارِجٌ عَنْهُ، بَلْ عَلَى أَنَّهُ مُحَصِّلٌ لِحَقِيقَتِهِ. قَالَ النَّصِيرُ الطُّوسِيُّ فِي مَنْطِقِ التَّجْرِيدِ: الْمُقَوِّمَاتُ إِنْ لَمْ تَتَرَتَّبْ عَلَى تَرْتِيبٍ، كَمَا يُقَالُ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الْإِنْسَانِ نَاطِقٌ حَيَوَانٌ كَانَ الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنْهُمَا رَسْمًا، فَالْحَدُّ عِنْدَهُ مُعَرِّفٌ مُشْتَمِلٌ عَلَى جَمِيعِ الْمُقَوِّمَاتِ تَرْكِيبًا طَبِيعِيًّا، وَقَدْ وَقَعَ فِي بَعْضِ عِبَارَاتِ «الشِّفَاءِ»: يَجِبُ فِي التَّحْدِيدِ تَقْدِيمُ الْجِنْسِ عَلَى الْفَصْلِ، وَلَمْ يَظْهَرْ لِهَذَا الْعَبْدِ إِلَى الْآنَ وَجْهُهُ، وَلَعَلَّ اللهَ يُحْدِثُ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ أَمْرًا.
And if it is not essential but accidental, then it is a description. This is the position of the later scholars. As for the position of the ancients: if it is by essentials, it is a definition; if by accidents, it is a description. And it is complete if it includes the proximate genus, whether definition or description, and if it does not include it, then it is incomplete. Thus the complete definition is that which includes the proximate genus and differentia, and it leads to the essence. This is the well-known view. But the truth is that it includes all parts, whether external or mental, but the logicians did not consider definition by external parts because it does not lead to the unknown. And it is recommended to present the genus before the differentia in the complete definition, and it is necessary to bind one with the other, not as something external to it, but as actualizing its reality. Al-Nasir al-Tusi said in Mantiq al-Tajrid: If the constitutives are not arranged in an order, as is said in the definition of man as 'rational animal', then the composite of them is a description; so according to him the definition is a definiens that includes all constitutives in a natural composition. And it occurs in some expressions of al-Shifa': it is obligatory in definition to present the genus before the differentia, and this servant has not yet seen its reason; perhaps Allah will bring about something after that.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between a real definition (hadd) and a description (rasm). A real definition is by essential attributes, while a description is by accidental ones. The author presents two schools: the later scholars (mutakkhirun) hold that if the definiens includes any essential, it is a definition, but if only accidents, it is a description; the ancients (qudama) restrict definition to exclusively essential attributes. The passage then subdivides definitions (and descriptions) into complete and incomplete based on inclusion of the proximate genus. A complete definition includes both the proximate genus and the proximate differentia and leads to the essence (kunn). However, the author notes that the true complete definition includes all parts, whether external or mental, but logicians exclude external parts because they do not yield new knowledge. The author then discusses the recommended order of genus before differentia, citing al-Tusi and Ibn Sina. Al-Tusi argues that if the parts are not ordered naturally, the composite is merely a description; the definition must have a natural composition. Ibn Sina is quoted as stating that presenting genus before differentia is obligatory, but the author (the commentator) admits not understanding the full rationale.

الرَّسْم
الْجِنْس الْقَرِيب
الْفَصْل الْقَرِيب
التَّامّ
النَّاقِص

Which of the following best describes a 'complete definition' (hadd tamm) according to the majority of logicians in this passage?
A) A definition that includes any genus and any differentia.
B) A definition that includes the proximate genus and proximate differentia and leads to the essence.
C) A definition that includes all external and mental parts.
D) A definition that uses only accidental attributes.

Key Takeaway
A complete definition requires the proximate genus and differentia; without them, it is either an incomplete definition or a mere description.

Purpose of Verbal Definition and Its Distinction from Lexical Inquiry
Author explains the purpose of verbal definition and its distinction from lexical inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a verbal definition (al-ta'rif al-lafzi) and a lexical inquiry (al-bahth al-lafzi al-lughawi) and identify the conditions under which a verbal definition serves only tasdiq.

Core Idea
Verbal definition's purpose depends on whether the meaning is already present—it can be either tasawwuri or tasdiqi.

In this passage, the author explores a subtle but crucial distinction: verbal definitions can either create a new conception or simply confirm an existing one. This has implications for how we define terms in logic and other sciences. Understanding this distinction will help you avoid confusing a definition with a lexical inquiry, which is a common mistake even among scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of verbal definition
Read the author's opening: 'The purpose of verbal definition is to present the meaning insofar as it is the meaning of the word.' Note that under this aspect, the meaning is not yet present. This implies the definition aims to bring something new to mind.
The key phrase is 'from where it is the meaning of the word' — the definition targets the relation between word and meaning.
2
Understand the potential objection
The author flags a problem: if verbal definition brings a new meaning, it seems to be a tasdiqi matter (requiring assent), not mere tasawwur. This would blur the line between definition and proof.
The objection is that defining a word might require establishing its usage, which is a tasdiqi claim.
3
Distinguish verbal definition from lexical inquiry
The author states that proving a word's usage (wad'iyya) in answer to 'Is this word used for this meaning?' is a lexical investigation, which is indeed tasdiqi. Al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq is cited as confusing the two.
Lexical inquiry belongs to the science of lexicography (lugha), not to logic. It requires evidence.
4
Analyze the author's resolution
When the definiendum is present and attended to, the verbal definition only confirms the known meaning — thus it is tasdiqi. If the meaning is forgotten (though stored in the mind), the definition can serve both tasawwur and tasdiq. The criterion is the definer's intention.
The author ends with 'fa-ta'ammal' — urging careful reflection on this nuanced point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت الغرض من التعريف اللفظي إحضار المعنى من حيث إنه مدلول اللفظ، وبهذه الحيثية غير حاضر، ولا يذهب عليك أنه على هذا التقدير يلزم تحصيل أمر لم يكن حاصلا من قبل؛ فإن المعنى من حيث المدلولية لم يكن حاصلا قط، وإن جعل الحيثية تعليلية خارجة عن المطلوب يلزم إحضار الحاضر قطعا. نَعَمْ، بَيَانُ مَوْضُوعِيَّةِ اللَّفْظِ فِي جَوَاب هَلْ هَذَا اللَّفْظُ مَوْضُوعٌ لِمَعْنَى بَحْتُ لَفْظِيٌّ، يُقْصَدُ إِثْبَاتُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فِي عِلْمِ اللُّغَةِ، فهو من المطالب التصديقية، فَمَنْ قَالَ إِنَّهُ مِنَ الْمَطَالِبِ التَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ لَمْ يُفَرِّقُ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الْبَحْثِ اللَّفْظِيِّ اللُّغَوِيِّ. القائل السيد المحقق، والتحقيق في هذا المقام أنه إذا عرفت بالتعريف اللفظي عند حضور المعرف والتفاته لا يكون المقصود منه إلا التصديق، وإذا لم يكن ملتفتا بل ذهل عنه وكان موجودا في الخزانة فيحتمل الأمرين، فالمدار على قصد المعرف، فتأمل.
I said: The purpose of verbal definition is to present the meaning insofar as it is the meaning of the word, and under this aspect it is not present. And it is not hidden from you that on this assumption the attainment of something that was not obtained before is necessary; for the meaning in terms of being a meaning was never obtained. And if the aspect is made explanatory and external to the desired thing, then the presentation of that which is present necessarily occurs. Yes, the demonstration of the word's being used in answer to 'Is this word used for a meaning?' is a purely lexical investigation, intended to be proven by evidence in the science of lexicography, so it is among subject matters of assent. So whoever says that it is among subject matters of assent did not distinguish between it and lexical verbal investigation. The speaker is al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq. The investigation in this place is that if you understand by verbal definition when the definiendum is present and attended to, then nothing is intended by it except assent. And if it is not attended to but rather forgotten while it exists in the mind, then it is possible for both aspects; so the criterion is the intention of the definer. So reflect.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the nature of verbal definition (al-ta'rif al-lafzi). The author begins by stating the purpose of verbal definition: to bring to mind the meaning insofar as it is the meaning of the word. Under this aspect, the meaning was not already present (because the hearer may not have connected the word to that meaning). This raises a potential problem: if verbal definition brings something new, it seems to be a tasdiqi (assent) matter, not merely tasawwuri (conception). But the author notes a distinction: if we consider the verbal definition as a clarification of the word's usage (wad'iyya), that is a lexical investigation (bahth lafzi) which is indeed a subject of assent (tasdiq). He then cites al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq who says that some scholars mistakenly treat verbal definition as purely tasdiqi, not distinguishing it from lexical inquiry. The author's own investigation (tahqiq) resolves this: when the definiendum is already present and attended to, the verbal definition's purpose is only to give assent (tasdiq) that this word means that meaning. But if the definiendum is forgotten (though present in the mind), then the definition can serve both tasawwur and tasdiq. The intended purpose depends on the definer's aim. The author ends with 'fa-ta'ammal' urging careful reflection.

Key Terms
التعريف اللفظي
المطالب التصديقية
البحث اللفظي اللغوي
السيد المحقق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a verbal definition serve only to give assent (tasdiq) rather than conception (tasawwur)?
A) When the definiendum is forgotten.
B) When the definiendum is present and attended to.
C) When the definition is lexical only.
D) When the definition is based on evidence.

Key Takeaway
The purpose of a verbal definition shifts between conception and assent depending on whether the listener already has the meaning present in mind — a distinction that separates genuine definition from lexical inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'salah' (prayer). A scholar says: 'Salah is the specific set of actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim.' Is this a verbal definition or a lexical inquiry? Explain your reasoning based on the author's criteria.
?
In a debate about the definition of 'khabar mutawatir', one interlocutor claims that a verbal definition can only provide tasawwur, not tasdiq. Using the passage, evaluate whether this claim is accurate. Provide conditions under which each applies.

Definition of Judgment and Its Two Types
Definition of judgment and its two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of judgment (hukm), distinguish its general and detailed types, and explain why the relation (nisba) is secondary in judgment.

Core Idea
Judgment is the mental bond; it comes as immediate grasp or articulated proposition.

This passage introduces one of the most fundamental concepts in Islamic logic: judgment (al-hukm). Before we can analyze propositions or syllogisms, we must understand what a judgment is and how it arises in the mind. The author defines judgment as a mental bond (al-'aqd al-mun'aqid) between subject and predicate, mediated by a relation. He then distinguishes two types: the immediate, holistic grasp (ijmali) and the detailed, articulated proposition (tafsili). Pay close attention to the point about the relation (al-nisba): it enters judgment only secondarily, which will protect you from a common mistake later when you encounter propositional analysis.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum
Find the Arabic term being defined. The passage begins with 'الْحُكْمُ' (al-hukm). What is it being defined as? Identify the main definiens: 'العقد المنعقد من الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة'.
The definiendum is 'al-hukm'; the definiens includes the components of the mental bond.
2
Extract the two divisions
The author divides judgment into 'إجمالي' (general) and 'تفصيلي' (detailed). For each, note the distinguishing feature: the general is an immediate disclosure (انكشاف الاتحاد دفعة واحدة); the detailed requires multiple mental forms and is called the proposition (القضية).
The general judgment is like an instantaneous perception; the detailed is a structured proposition.
3
Analyze the relation (al-nisba)
The author states that the relation enters judgment only 'بِالتَّبَعِيَّةِ' (secondarily). Why? Because it is a relational meaning (من المعاني الحرفية) that cannot stand alone; it serves as a mirror for the two extremes.
This means the relation is not an independent concept; it is a tool to connect subject and predicate.
4
Identify the essential nature of judgment
The passage concludes that the object of judgment must be something independent in concept (مستقلا بالمفهومية). How does this relate to the relation? The judgment as a whole must be independently conceivable, but the relation within it is not.
This distinguishes judgment from mere relational awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْحُكْمُ، أي العقد المنعقد من الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة (مِنْهُ إِجْمَالِيُّ، وَهُوَ انْكِشَافُ الاتِّحَادِ)، الاتحاد المنكشف (بَيْنَ الْأَمْرَيْنِ دَفْعَةٌ وَاحِدَةً)، كما إذا فتحنا العين ورأينا الجدار الأبيض، فيحصل العقد المنعقد بينهما دفعة، (وَمِنْهُ تَفْصِيلِيُّ ، [وَهُوَ]) العقد المنعقد (الْمَنْطِقِيُّ الَّذِي يَسْتَدْعِي صُوَرًا مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ مُفَصَّلَةٌ)، وهو المتعارف بالقضية. ( وَالنِّسْبَةُ) إِنَّمَا تَدْخُلُ فِي مُتَعَلَّقِ الحُكْم والتصديق (بِالتَّبَعِيَّةِ ؛ لأَنَّهَا)، أي النسبة مِنَ الْمَعَانِي الْحَرْفِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَا تُلَاحَظُ بِالِاسْتِقْلَالِ ، إِنَّمَا هِيَ مِرْأَةٌ لِمُلَاحَظَةِ حَالِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، ومتعلق الحكم يجب أن يكون أمرا مستقلا بالمفهومية.
Judgment (al-hukm) is the mental bond composed of subject, predicate, and relation. It is of two types: general (ijmali), which is the immediate disclosure of the union between two things at once, like when we open the eye and see a white wall, so the mental bond between them occurs instantly; and detailed (tafsili), which is the logical mental bond that requires multiple detailed mental forms, and this is conventionally called a proposition. The relation (al-nisba) only enters into the object of judgment and assent secondarily, because it is among the relational meanings (al-ma'ani al-harfiyya) that are not considered independently; rather it is a mirror for observing the state of the two extremes, and the object of judgment must be something independent in conceptual meaning.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage defines judgment (al-hukm) as the mental bond composed of subject, predicate, and relation. It then classifies judgment into two types: general (ijmali) and detailed (tafsili). The general judgment is an immediate, holistic grasp of the union between two things, illustrated by the example of seeing a white wall in one glance. The detailed judgment is the articulated logical proposition that involves multiple distinct mental representations. The passage then clarifies the status of the relation (al-nisba): it enters into the judgment only secondarily, as it is a relational meaning (ma'na harfi) that is not independently conceptualized; rather it serves as a mirror for the two extremes. The primary object of judgment must be an independently conceivable entity. This distinction is foundational in Islamic logic for understanding propositions.

Key Terms
الحكم
العقد المنعقد
الإجمالي
التفصيلي
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the detailed judgment (tafsili) called?
A) The immediate disclosure of union
B) The conventional proposition (al-qadiyya)
C) The mental bond without articulation
D) The relation (al-nisba)

Key Takeaway
Judgment is the mental bond of subject, predicate, and relation; it is either immediate (ijmali) or articulated (tafsili, i.e., proposition), and the relation is only a secondary tool, not an independent concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following is a general or detailed judgment: 'I know that the Prophet (peace be upon him) is truthful' (without analyzing why). Answer: general (ijmali) if it's an immediate conviction; detailed if it is expressed as a proposition with reasons.
?
Take a fiqh ruling: 'Wine is forbidden (haram).' Break it down into subject, predicate, and relation. Then explain why the relation is secondary in this judgment.

Three Considerations of the Subject in Propositions
Author refutes a mistaken view and presents the three-fold division of subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three considerations of the subject in a proposition and classify any given proposition as personal, indefinite, natural, or quantified based on its subject.

Core Idea
The subject of a proposition has three distinct logical considerations: essence itself, essence isolated, and essence united with individuals.

In this passage, the author clarifies a common confusion about the subject in logic. He first refutes a mistaken view that there might be an intermediate stage between essence and individuals, then provides a precise three-fold division that underpins the classification of propositions. Mastering these considerations is essential for accurate logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue being discussed
The author begins by refuting a claim that there might be a stage between essence and individuals expressed by a restricted compound. Understand why this claim is problematic: it either collapses into the indefinite or the particular.
The refutation sets the stage for the author's own classification.
2
Extract the three considerations of the subject
The author states that the subject is either particular or universal. For the universal, there are three considerations: (1) the thing itself in itself, (2) the essence isolated in the mind with the condition of mental unity, (3) the essence united with individuals mentally or externally.
These are the building blocks for proposition types.
3
Map each consideration to a proposition type
The first consideration yields the indefinite proposition (المهملة) as used by early scholars. The second yields the natural proposition (الطبيعية). The third yields the quantified proposition (المحصورة) and the indefinite as used by later scholars.
Note that 'indefinite' has two senses.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Take the proposition 'الإنسان حيوان' (Man is an animal). If interpreted as a statement about the essence of man (natural), use the second consideration. If about all individuals (quantified), use the third. If without quantifier (indefinite), use the first.
Practice with other common propositions from fiqh or kalam.

فإن قلت: لعل في الخارج مرتبة سوى مرتبة الماهية من والأفراد يعبر عنها بهذا المركب التقييدي ؟ قلت: فهو إما معنى كلي مشترك في جميع الأفراد، أو في كل فرد من أفراده معنى مغاير لما في الفرد الآخر ، فعلى الأول يصير القضية مهملة، وعلى الثاني حاله كحال الأفراد، انتهى كلامه ملخصا. ولا يخفى ما فيه من الاختلال ؛ فإن في الخارج الماهية بحيث يصلح للانطباق على الأفراد، وهي اعتبار أخص من موضوع المهملة. نعم، ينعقد منه مهملة المتأخرين، لكن لا يضرنا . وإن شئت زيادة تفصيل فاستمع [إلى] التقسيم من الرأس، فنقول: الموضوع جزئي أو كلي، سواء كان ذاتيا أو عرضيا، وله اعتبارات ثلاثة: الأول نفس الشيء من حيث هي الثاني الماهية المنحازة في الذهن، بحيث لا يمكن سراية أحكام الأفراد إليها، ويعبر عنها بالماهية بشرط الوحدة الذهنية، الثالث الماهية المنحازة المتحدة مع الأفراد ذهنا أو خارجا، محققا أو مقدرا، وهذان الاعتباران أخصان من الأول، فالجزئي موضوع الشخصية، والكلي بالاعتبار الأول موضوع المهملة وبالاعتبار الثاني موضوع الطبيعية، وبالاعتبار الثالث موضوع المحصورة ومهملة المتأخرين، فتأمل، وكن على بصيرة وتوكل على الله ؛ فإنه أعلم بالصواب.
If you said: Perhaps in reality there is a stage besides the stage of essence and individuals, expressed by this restricted compound? I said: It is either a universal meaning common to all individuals, or in each individual there is a meaning different from that in another individual. In the first case the proposition becomes indefinite, and in the second its case is like that of individuals. End of his speech summarized. It is not hidden what is in it of disorder; for in reality essence is such that it is suitable for applying to individuals, and it is a consideration more specific than the subject of the indefinite proposition. Yes, the indefinite proposition of the later scholars may be formed from it, but that does not harm us. And if you wish more detail, listen to the division from the beginning, and we say: The subject is either particular or universal, whether essential or accidental, and it has three considerations: first, the thing itself in itself; second, the essence isolated in the mind such that the rulings of individuals cannot extend to it, and it is expressed as 'essence with the condition of mental unity'; third, the essence isolated and united with individuals mentally or externally, actually or hypothetically. These two considerations are more specific than the first. So the particular is the subject of the personal proposition, and the universal according to the first consideration is the subject of the indefinite proposition, according to the second the subject of the natural proposition, and according to the third the subject of the quantified proposition and the indefinite proposition of the later scholars. So reflect, and be insightful, and rely on Allah; for He knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a logical text, where the author addresses a potential objection and then provides a detailed classification of the subject (موضوع) in propositions. The objection suggests that there might be an intermediate stage between essence and individuals in reality, expressed by a restricted compound phrase. The author summarizes a previous response that such a phrase either designates a universal meaning common to all individuals, making the proposition indefinite, or a distinct meaning for each individual, reducing it to a particular. He then critiques this response, arguing that it is flawed because essence in reality is actually universal and applicable to individuals, and that the indefinite proposition of later scholars can be formed from it. To clarify, he presents a fresh division of the subject: subject can be particular or universal, and for the universal there are three considerations. The first consideration is the thing itself without any condition; this gives the indefinite proposition (المهملة). The second consideration is the essence isolated in the mind, with the condition of mental unity, preventing the rulings of individuals from extending to it; this is the natural proposition (الطبيعية). The third consideration is the essence united with individuals either mentally or externally; this yields the quantified proposition (المحصورة) as well as the indefinite proposition as used by later logicians (مهملة المتأخرين). The author emphasizes that the second and third considerations are more specific than the first. He urges the student to reflect and trust in Allah.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The subject of a proposition, qualified as particular or universal, and further subdivided into three considerations: the thing itself, essence isolated in mind, and essence united with individuals. [Note: No significant shift — this term is stable across the tradition.]
مهملة (muhmala): An indefinite proposition where the subject is universal considered in itself, without any quantifier; also later used for a proposition with a subject considered with attention to individuals. [Note: There is a distinction between the 'indefinite of the early scholars' (subject as such) and 'indefinite of the later scholars' (subject with attention to individuals).]
طبيعية (ṭabīʿiyya): A proposition whose subject is the essence isolated in the mind, with the condition of mental unity, so that rulings of individuals do not apply to it. [Note: Stable — used consistently in post-Avicennan logic.]
محصورة (maḥṣūra): A quantified proposition (universal or particular) whose subject is the essence united with individuals. [Note: No significant shift — standard term for quantified propositions.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The thing itself in itself
B) The essence isolated in mind with mental unity
C) The essence united with individuals mentally or externally
D) The particular subject

Key Takeaway
The subject in logic is not monolithic; its three considerations — the thing itself, the essence isolated in mind, and the essence united with individuals — correspond to different proposition types and are crucial for precise logical expression.

Definition of Haml and Its Two Types: Bi al-Dhat and Bi al-Arad
Author defines predication and distinguishes essential from accidental union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential predication (ḥaml bi al-dhāt) from accidental predication (ḥaml bi al-ʿaraḍ) using the criterion of existence attribution without intermediary.

Core Idea
Predication is a union of existences, not just conceptual — essential when one existence, accidental when mediated.

In this passage, the author defines predication (al-ḥaml) as the third foundational topic of logic. He explains that predication is not merely a grammatical attribution but a real relational union between two things. The critical distinction is whether the union is essential (bi al-dhāt) — where the two share a single existence — or accidental (bi al-ʿaraḍ) — where existence is attributed through an intermediary. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing definitions and propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-ḥaml' (predication). Look at how the author begins: 'الثالث: الحمل اتحاد المتغايرين...'
2
Extract the two types
Note the two types: اتحاد بالذات (essential union) and اتحاد بالعرض (accidental union).
3
Understand the criterion
Essential union: the two things exist with a single existence without an intermediary in attribution. Accidental union: the existence of one is attributed to another via an intermediary.
The author gives a detailed criterion involving inherence or abstraction of principles.
4
Apply the criterion to examples
Think of an example: 'Zayd is human' — the humanity of Zayd is essentially united with Zayd's essence. 'Zayd is writing' — the existence of the accident 'writing' is attributed to Zayd accidentally.
Essential predication corresponds to substance-attribute relations that are definitional; accidental predication concerns non-definitional accidents.
5
Analyze the author's critique
The author challenges the claim that essential union is only for essential attributes by pointing out that genus and differentia (e.g., 'animal' and 'rational') are essentially united in the species, even though they are accidents of each other.
This shows the complexity of applying the criterion to logical relations within definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثَّالِثُ : الْحَمْلُ اتَّحَادُ الْمُتَغَابِرِيْنِ فِي نحو من التعقل بحسب نحو آخر بالذات أو مِنَ الْوُجُودِ ، اتِّحَادًا بالذاتِ). المشهور في تفسيره علاقة بين الشيئين، بحيث يكون نسبة وجود أحدهما بالذات إلى الآخر من غير واسطة في العروض، بأن يكونا موجودين بوجود واحد، (أو) اتحادا (بالْعَرَض). وهو علاقة بين الشيئين بحيث ينسب وجود أحدهما بالذات إلى الآخر بوساطته بالعرض، أو وجود ثالث بالذات إليهما بالعرض. ومعياره قيام مبدأ أحدهما بالآخر، أو انتزاعه عنه، بمقايسة إلى آخر أو لا بمقايسة ، أو قيام مبدئهما بثالث، أو انتزاعهما عن ثالث. وقالوا: الأول مختص بالذاتيات، والثاني بالعرضيات. ويرد عليه ورودا ظاهرا الانتقاض باتحاد الأجناس والفصول ؛ فإن الجنس عرض عام للفصل، كما أن الفصل خاصة له، مع كون الاتحاد بينهما بالذات . ولا ينفع الجواب بأن الوجود إذا نسب إلى النوع فهو وجود للجنس والفصل بالذات، وإذا نسب إلى الجنس فهو وجود للفصل والنوع بالعرض، وهكذا حال الفصل ؛ لأن الوجود إنما يعرض لهما من حيث إنهما واحد على مذهبهم، كما تقدم فالوجود واحد لشيء واحد، ذلك الواحد بعينه الجنس والفصل ، فالوجود منسوب إليهما بالذات وأيضا لم يدل إلى الآن دليل على ذلك الاختصاص، والله أعلم بالصواب.
(The third: predication is the union of two different things in some mode of intellection according to another mode, essentially or from existence, an essential union). The well-known interpretation is a relation between two things such that the existence of one is essentially related to the other without an intermediary in attribution, such that they exist with a single existence, or an accidental union. It is a relation between two things such that the existence of one is essentially related to the other through its mediation accidentally, or the existence of a third essentially related to both accidentally. Its criterion is the inherence of the principle of one in the other, or its abstraction from it, with comparison to another or without comparison, or the inherence of their principle in a third, or their abstraction from a third. They said: the first is specific to essential attributes, the second to accidental attributes. It is refuted by a manifest counterexample: the union of genera and differentiae; for the genus is a universal accident of the differentia, just as the differentia is a special property of it, while the union between them is essential. The answer that when existence is attributed to the species it is existence for the genus and differentia essentially, and when attributed to the genus it is existence for the differentia and species accidentally, and similarly for the differentia, is not helpful; because existence only occurs to them insofar as they are one according to their doctrine, as previously stated. Existence is one for one thing, that same one being the genus and differentia, so existence is attributed to both essentially. Also, no proof has been given for that specificity, and Allah knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage defines predication (al-haml) as the third topic in a series (likely from a matn). The author explains that predication is a relation of union between two different things; the union can be essential (bi al-dhat) or accidental (bi al-arad). Essential union occurs when the two things exist with a single existence—for example, the genus 'animal' and the species 'human' are essentially united in the essence of an individual human. Accidental union occurs when the existence of one is attributed to the other through an intermediary; for example, 'Zayd is writing'—the existence of 'writer' is attributed to Zayd via the inherence of the accident of writing. The author then gives a criterion: essential union involves the inherence or abstraction of the principle of one in the other without comparison; accidental union may involve a third principle. The passage then critiques a common doctrine that essential union is only for essential attributes (like genus and differentia) and accidental union for accidents. The author points out that genus and differentia are themselves essentially united in the species—contradicting the claimed exclusivity. The attempted answer by positing different relative attributions fails because existence is one for the single thing that is both genus and differentia. This critique shows the author's engagement with the logical tradition and his willingness to challenge established views.

اتحاد بالعرض

Key Takeaway

Objection to Accidental Union and Its Refutation
Author refutes a commentator's objection that accidental union is misconceived.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the principle (al-mabdaʾ) cannot replace the derived term (al-mushtaqq) as predicate in accidental predication, and articulate the author's refutation.

Core Idea
Accidental predication is a real existential relation, not a linguistic derivation.

In this passage, the author tackles a common misunderstanding about accidental union (al-ittiḥād bi al-ʿaraḍ). Some commentators argued that since predication is based on the inherence of a principle (like 'writing' in a writer), the principle itself should be the predicate, not the derived term. The author dismisses this as absurd and clarifies the true nature of accidental union as a specific existential relation. Understanding this refutation is crucial for grasping the proper use of derived terms in logical predication.

Guided Path
1
State the Objector's Claim
Identify the objection: the commentator claims that because predication depends on the inherence of the principle, the principle (e.g., 'writing') should be the predicate, not the derived term (e.g., 'writer'). The objector thinks the principle is more deserving because it is more essential.
2
Analyze the Objector's Reasoning
The objector assumes that the cause of predication (inherence of the principle) is the same as the predicate itself. He conflates the condition (inherence) with the actual relation of predication.
This is a fallacy of conflating cause with effect.
3
Understand the Author's Refutation
The author argues that accidental union is not about mere abstraction or embodiment; it is a specific existential relation by which the existence of one thing is attributed to another. This relation exists only between the derived term and the subject, not between the principle and the subject. The inherence of the principle is only an indication of that relation.
For example, 'writer' is attributed to Zayd, but 'writing' is not.
4
Distinguish Principle from Derived Term
The only difference between the principle and the derived term is how the concept is considered: if taken as unconditioned in one way (as abstracted from the subject), it is a principle; if taken unconditioned in another way (as attributed to a subject), it is a derived term. Therefore, the principle lacks the relational quality needed for predication.
This shows the principle is inherently unsuitable as a predicate in accidental predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بعض الشراح على الاتحاد بالعرض، بأن مدار الحمل لما كان قيام المبدأ فما شأن المشتق يكون محمولا دون المبدأ، بل المبدأ أولى بالحمل ؛ لكونه منتزعا منه بالذات، والمشتق بوساطته، والمبدأ المنضم أولى من المنتزع ؛ لكونه موجودا بالذات. ولا يخفى سخافته ; فإن الحمل بالعرض ليس عبارة عن الانتزاع أو الانضمام، بل هو علاقة خاصة بها ينسب وجود أحدهما إلى الآخر ، وتلك مفقودة في المبادئ دون المشتقات . نعم، قيام المبدأ وانتزاعه أمارة لتحقق تلك العلاقة بين مشتقاتها، وإذا تحققت أن ليس بين المبدأ والمشتق فرق إلا باعتبار أنه إن أخذ بشرط لا كان مبدأ، وإن أخذ لا بشرط شيء كان مشتقا علمت أن المبدأ غير صالح لنسبة وجود موضوعه إليه بلا ريب، هذا، والله أعلم.
Some commentators objected to accidental union, saying that since the basis of predication is the inherence of the principle, what is the matter with the derived term being the predicate instead of the principle? Rather, the principle is more deserving of predication because it is abstracted from it essentially, while the derived term is through its mediation, and the embodied principle is more deserving than the abstracted one because it exists essentially. Its absurdity is not hidden; for accidental union is not an expression of abstraction or embodiment, but rather a specific relation by which the existence of one is attributed to the other, and that relation is missing in principles but present in derived terms. Yes, the inherence of the principle and its abstraction are indications of the realization of that relation between its derivatives. And when you realize that there is no difference between the principle and the derived term except that if it is taken as unconditioned it is a principle, and if taken as unconditioned in a different way it is a derived term, you know that the principle is not suitable for attributing the existence of its subject to it without doubt. This, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection raised by some commentators against the notion of accidental union (al-ittiḥād bi al-ʿaraḍ) in predication. The objectors argue that since predication is based on the inherence of a principle (mabdaʾ)—like 'writing' in Zayd—the derived term (mushtaqq) like 'writer' should not be the predicate; instead, the principle itself ('writing') should be, because it is more fundamental and exists essentially. The author dismisses this as absurd. He clarifies that accidental union is not a matter of mere abstraction or embodiment of the principle; rather, it is a specific relation by which the existence of one thing is attributed to another—for example, the existence of 'writer' is attributed to Zayd. This relation exists between the derived term and the subject, not between the principle and the subject. The principle (e.g., 'writing') does not qualify as a predicate in a proposition like 'Zayd is writing' because that would be ill-formed. The inherence of the principle is merely an indication of the relation, not the relation itself. Furthermore, the author explains that the only difference between the principle and the derived term is how they are considered: if taken as unconditioned (lā bi sharṭ) in a certain way, it becomes a principle; if taken unconditioned in another way, it becomes a derived term. Thus, the principle is inherently unsuitable for predicating the existence of its subject (e.g., we cannot say 'Zayd is writing' if writing is the principle). This refutation clarifies the precise nature of accidental predication.

First Predication: Essential Identity Requires Conceptual Distinction
Author defines the first predication where subject and predicate are identical in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'Man is man' is a genuine predication and identify when predication becomes impossible due to lack of conceptual distinction.

Core Idea
Predication is a relation; a relation needs two distinct terms, even if they refer to the same reality.

This lesson introduces the first type of predication in Islamic logic, where subject and predicate are identical in reference. At first glance, 'Man is man' seems trivial, but the author shows that genuine predication requires a conceptual distinction between the two terms. Understanding this condition is crucial for grasping how definitions and judgments work in Islamic sciences, from usul al-fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms in a proposition
In any proposition, there are two terms: subject (mawḍūʿ) and predicate (maḥmūl). In first predication, they refer to the same entity, e.g., 'Man is man'.
2
Understand that predication is a relation
The author states that predication is a relation (nisba) which inherently requires two distinct sides. Even if the reference is identical, the concepts must be distinct for a relation to exist.
Denying this is called 'mukābara' (blatant obstruction).
3
Apply the condition of conceptual distinction
If the thing is observed with a single observation and no multiplicity, predication is impossible because there is no duality. If observed with two observations but those observations are not taken as part of the meaning, predication is also impossible.
The author argues against al-Ṣadr al-Dashtakī who allowed predication in the latter case.
4
Examine the example 'Man is man'
For 'Man is man' to be a valid predication, the subject must be considered as an individual (e.g., 'this man') and the predicate as a species, or some other conceptual distinction, even though the reference is identical.
Without such distinction, the proposition is either impossible or merely a verbal repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني به (وَهُوَ ) ، أي الحمل (إِمَّا أَنْ يُعْنَى أَنَّ الْمَوْضُوعَ بِعَيْنِهِ الْمَحْمُولُ) . أن عنوان الموضوع بعينه عنوان حقيقة المحمول، (فَيُسَمَّى الْحَمْلَ الْأَوَّلِيَّ). اعلم: أن الموضوع والمحمول فيه شيء واحد، وهو على أنحاء، الأول: أن يلاحظ بلحاظ واحد، ولم يكن هناك شيء من الكثرة، وحينئذ يمتنع الحمل أيضا. والثاني أن يلاحظ شيء واحد بلحاظين، من غير أن يؤخذ اللحاظ قيدا، وحينئذ يمتنع الحمل أيضا ، خلافا للصدر (۱) المعاصر للمحقق الدواني ؛ لأن الحمل نسبة، وهي تستدعي مغايرة الطرفين، وإنكار ذلك مكابرة صريحة، وبتكرر الالتفات لا يصير الشيء اثنين، ما لم يقيد بهما، ولأنه لا بد للحمل الالتفات إلى الطرفين ومن الممتنع تكرر الالتفات من غير تكرر الملتفت إليه أو الزمان . من المحقق (۱) هو: مير صدر الدين محمد بن غياث الدين منصور بن صدر الدين محمد بن إبراهيم الدشتكي الحسيني الشيرازي (۸۲۸ - ۹۰۳هـ) ، فقيه فيلسوف شيعي إمامي، يلقب بصدر الدين الكبير ; تمييزا عن الصدر صاحب الأسفار الأربعة، له: مساجلات مع الدواني حول شرح التجريد الجديد حاشية على الكشاف وغيرهما. انظر الأعلام للزركشي: ۳۰۱/۵ ، ۳۰۲ .
He means by it (and it), i.e., predication (either it is intended that the subject is exactly the predicate). That is, the term of the subject is exactly the term of the reality of the predicate, so it is called the first predication. Know that in it the subject and predicate are one thing, and it has several aspects: First: that it is observed with a single observation, and there is no multiplicity whatsoever, in which case predication is also impossible. Second: that one thing is observed with two observations, without taking the observation as a condition, in which case predication is also impossible, contrary to al-Ṣadr, contemporary of al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawānī; because predication is a relation, and it requires distinction of the two sides, and denying that is blatant obstruction. And by repeated attention a thing does not become two unless it is conditioned by them, and because for predication one must attend to both sides, and it is impossible to repeat attention without repeating the object of attention or time.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of predication (al-ḥaml al-awwalī) where the subject and predicate are identical in essence — for example, 'Man is man'. The author explains that in this type, the subject term and predicate term refer to the same reality. He then examines the conditions for such predication: if the thing is considered with a single aspect and no multiplicity, predication is impossible because there is no distinction between the two terms. If one thing is considered with two aspects but those aspects are not taken as conditions (i.e., the aspects are not part of the meaning), predication is also impossible. This refutes the opinion of a certain scholar (al-Ṣadr al-Dashtakī) who allowed predication in such a case. The reason is that predication is a relation (nisba) that requires distinctness (mukhālafa) between the two sides (subject and predicate). Even if one attends to the same thing multiple times, that does not create two things unless the aspects are taken as part of the conceptualization. Furthermore, valid predication requires attending to both the subject and the predicate as distinct concepts; if they are identical in every respect, they cannot be related. Thus, for 'Man is man' to be a genuine predication, there must be a conceptual difference (like being an individual vs. being a species) even though the reference is the same. This precise discussion is important for understanding the nature of propositions and the role of intentional distinction in logic.

Key Terms
الحمل الأولي
الموضوع
المحمول
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is predication impossible when one thing is observed with a single observation and no multiplicity?
A) Because the mind cannot attend to the same thing twice
B) Because predication requires a relation, and a relation needs two distinct terms
C) Because the observation itself creates multiplicity
D) Because the thing is too simple to be predicated

Key Takeaway
First predication (al-ḥaml al-awwalī) requires conceptual distinction between subject and predicate even when they refer to the same entity; without such distinction, predication is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the proposition 'Wajib is wajib' in fiqh: Is this a valid first predication? Identify the conceptual distinction needed for it to be meaningful (e.g., wajib as moral obligation vs. wajib as legal duty).
?
Construct two propositions in first predication using Islamic legal terms (e.g., 'Halal is halal') and explain the conceptual distinction that makes each a genuine predication.

What Is Mantiq?

Primary and Common Predication
Defines primary and common predication and their self-negation rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between primary predication (al-haml al-awwali) and common predication (al-haml al-sha'i') and identify the impossibility of self-negation in each.

Core Idea
Primary predication is identity; common predication is inherence. Self-negation is impossible only in the first.

In Islamic logic, predication takes two forms: when a concept is said of itself by identity, or when a predicate inheres in a subject. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating propositions in Kalam and usul al-fiqh. The passage you are about to read clarifies these types and the special rule that 'negation of a thing of itself' is absolutely impossible only in primary predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of predication
In the Arabic text, find where the author defines 'al-haml al-awwali' and 'al-haml al-sha'i'. Note the key phrases: 'بالحمل الأولي' and 'حملاً شائعاً'.
2
Explain their definitions
Primary predication: a concept is predicated of itself because it is identical to itself (e.g., 'man is man'). Common predication: a predicate inheres in a subject (e.g., 'Zayd is writing'). The author notes that calling both 'predication' is homonymous.
3
Distinguish the impossibility of self-negation
For primary predication, negating a thing of itself (e.g., 'man is not man') is absolutely impossible because the contradictory is always true. For common predication, self-negation requires the subject to exist; nonexistent subjects can be truly negated of themselves.
See the gloss: negation of a thing of itself in common predication needs existence of the subject.
4
Apply to an example from Kalam
Consider the statement 'The world is originated' (common predication) vs. 'The world is the world' (primary predication). The first can be false if the world were eternal; the second is always true. Test the self-negation: 'The world is not the world' is impossible in primary, but 'The world is not originated' is possible in common.

(ثُمَّ طَائِفَةٌ مِنَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ) لقيام مبدئها بها (يُحْمَلُ عَلَى أَنْفُسِهَا حَمْلاً شَائِعًا أَيْضًا، كَالْمَفْهُوم وَالْمُمْكِن الْعَام وَنَحْوهِمَا ، وَطَائِفَةٌ لا يُحْمَلُ عَلَى أَنْفُسِهَا ذَلِكَ الْحَمْلَ، بَلْ يُحْمَلُ عَلَيْهَا نَقَائِضُهَا ) ؛ لعدم قيام مبدئها، (كَالْجُزْئِي وَاللا مفهوم). قال . قال بعض أجلة المتأخرين ويمكن ههنا. وضع ضابطة، وهي أن كل كلي يتكرر نوع مبدئه فهو محمول على نفسه وإلا فنقيضه محمول عليه. أما الأول : فلأن عروض الشيء للشيء يستلزم عروضه للمشتق منه من حيث إنه مشتق منه ، وعروض مبدأ الاشتقاق لأمر يستلزم حمل مشتقه عليه . وأما الثاني: فلأنه لو لم يكن كذلك لكان محمولا على نفسه؛ لامتناع ارتفاع النقيضين، وحمل الشيء على نفسه يستلزم عروض مبدأ الاشتقاق له، وهو يستلزم عروضه لنفسه، فيكون متكرر النوع ، وهو خلاف المفروض ، اهـ . ويرد عليه ورودا ظاهرا أن قوله عروض الشيء لمبدأ الاشتقاق مستلزم لعروضه للمشتق منه ممنوع، وكذا عروض الشيء للمشتق غير مستلزم منه العروض للمبدأ، كالمبدئية والاشتقاق العارضين للمبدأ والمشتق، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
A notice on the investigation of categorical propositions. By derivation or without mediation, and it is the predicative statement 'by way of', so it is predication by synonymy. It is more likely that the application of the term 'predication' to both is by verbal homonymy, because the reality of the first is inherence, and the second is union in existence, and there is no common ground between them such that it would be predication. Know that every concept, whether existent or nonexistent, is predicated of itself by primary predication, because its referent is identity, and every concept is identical to itself. The statement that affirmation requires the existence of the subject is perhaps specific to common predication. From that, you hear that the negation of a thing of itself is absolutely impossible, because the truth of its contradictory is necessary. In the gloss: As for the impossibility of negating a thing of itself in common predication, it requires the existence of the subject. As for the nonexistent, it negates all things of itself, and this is based on the view that the truth of an affirmative proposition about it requires the existence of the subject. Know that the negation of a thing of itself is of two types: If the description is essential or a necessary concomitant of its individuals, then it is absolutely impossible, provided the subject exists; otherwise, it is only in the perpetual and descriptional propositions. Indeed, 'No sleeper is actually sleeping' is true. This, and God knows best. Then a group of concepts, due to the occurrence of their source in them, are also predicated of themselves by common predication, like 'concept' and 'general possible' and their likes. And a group are not predicated of themselves by that predication, but rather their contradictories are predicated of them, because their source does not occur in them, like 'particular' and 'non-concept'. Some eminent later scholars said: A rule can be posited here: Every universal whose source-type is repeated is predicated of itself; otherwise, its contradictory is predicated of it. As for the first: because the occurrence of a thing to a thing entails its occurrence to that from which it is derived, inasmuch as it is derived from it, and the occurrence of the source of derivation to something entails the predication of its derivative upon it. As for the second: because if it were not so, then it would be predicated of itself, due to the impossibility of both contradictories being false. The predication of a thing of itself entails the occurrence of the source of derivation to it, which entails its occurrence to itself, so its source-type is repeated, contrary to the assumption. It is refuted in an apparent way: His statement that the occurrence of a thing to the source of derivation entails its occurrence to that from which it is derived is denied; likewise, the occurrence of a thing to the derivative does not entail its occurrence to the source, like source-hood and derivation as accidents of the source and the derivative. This, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage defines two types of predication: primary predication (al-haml al-awwali) where a concept is said of itself by identity, and common predication (al-haml al-sha'i') where a concept is said of another by inherence. The author notes that applying the term 'predication' to both is merely homonymous. He then explains that every concept, whether existent or nonexistent, is predicated of itself by primary predication because each concept is identical to itself. Negation of a thing of itself is absolutely impossible in primary predication because the contradictory (e.g., 'S is not S') would be false; but in common predication, negation of a thing of itself requires the subject to exist, as in the case of nonexistents. Next, he classifies concepts: those whose source (like 'concept' or 'general possible') occurs in themselves are predicated of themselves by common predication as well; those whose source does not occur, like 'particular' and 'non-concept', have their contradictories predicated of them. He presents a rule from later scholars: if the source-type of a universal is repeated (i.e., the source applies to itself), it is self-predicable; otherwise, its contradictory is. For the first part, he argues that the occurrence of a thing to something entails its occurrence to the derivative, so a concept like 'concept' qualifies. For the second, he argues by contraposition: if a concept like 'particular' were self-predicable, its source would apply to itself, causing repetition, contrary to the assumption. The author then notes a critique: the entailment claim is denied; for example, source-hood and derivation are accidents that apply to the source and derivative differently, so the rule's reasoning is challenged. All examples used here are technical terms in logic (e.g., koncept, particular, possible), which are part of the Islamic logical tradition.

Key Terms
الحمل الأولي
الحمل الشائع
المفهوم
الجزئي

C) Always possible
D) Possible only for nonexistents

Key Takeaway
Primary predication is identity, so self-negation is impossible; common predication is inherence and allows self-negation only when the subject exists.

What Is Mantiq?

Rule for Self-Predication by Common Predication
Classifies concepts by self-predication and presents a rule with critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify concepts as self-predicable or not by common predication based on the repetition of their source-type, and evaluate the logical entailment behind the rule.

Core Idea
A concept is predicated of itself by common predication if its source-type repeats; otherwise its contradictory is predicated.

After distinguishing the two types of predication, the author introduces a classification of concepts: some can be predicated of themselves even by common predication, while others cannot. He presents a rule from later scholars that hinges on whether the source-type of the concept repeats. However, he also records a critique of this rule. This lesson will help you apply the rule and think critically about its assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples given
Find the two groups in the Arabic: 'مفهوم' and 'ممكن عام' are self-predicable by common predication; 'جزئي' and 'لا مفهوم' have their contradictories predicated of themselves.
2
Understand the rule of source-type repetition
The rule says: if the source-type of a universal is repeated (i.e., the source applies to itself), then it is self-predicable. For 'concept', the source 'concept' applies to itself; for 'general possible', the possibility applies to itself. For 'particular', particularity does not apply to itself, so 'non-particular' (i.e., universal) is predicated.
3
Apply the rule to another concept from Islamic logic
Test 'existence' (wujud). Existence exists? In common predication, 'existence exists' is true because existence is existent. So 'existence' would be self-predicable. Test 'cause' (sabab): Is 'cause caused'? Not necessarily; a cause might not be caused itself. So 'cause' is likely not self-predicable.
4
Critically evaluate the entailment claim
The author critiques the rule's reasoning: the claim that occurrence of a thing to the source of derivation entails its occurrence to the derivative is denied. For example, 'sourcehood' and 'derivation' are accidents that apply to source and derivative differently. Consider the entailment: does 'cause is caused' necessarily follow? Not always.

Extrinsic Attribution and Mental Existence
Author defines extrinsic attribution and discusses its implications for mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of extrinsic attribution (al-ittiṣāf al-inḍimāmī) and apply it to distinguish between mental and external existence based on the effects of quiddities.

Core Idea
Mental existence lacks essential effects; external existence entails them.

In this passage, the author examines a key epistemological subtlety: that for true extrinsic attribution, both subject and attribute must exist in the same realm. This leads to a profound discussion about the nature of mental forms. While some argued that mental forms must exist externally because they have effects, the author clarifies that mental existence is defined precisely by the absence of the essential effects of the quiddity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for later discussions in Islamic philosophy and theology about the reality of mental concepts.

Guided Path
1
Define extrinsic attribution
Identify the definition given: it requires both extremes (subject and attribute) to exist in the same realm—either both external or both mental.
2
Analyze the two-aspect argument
Some scholars argued the mental form has two aspects—one as it inheres in the mind (external existence) and one in itself (mental existence). Understand why they thought effects follow from the form.
3
Understand the verification (taḥqīq)
The author states that it is known necessarily that there is a mode of existence where effects are not necessary concomitants. Investigation reveals mental existence where essential effects are absent.
Example: the mental form of fire does not burn, so it is mental.
4
Examine the note on attribution (tabṣira)
Consider the distinction between external attribution in the sense of the subject being external versus the attribute itself existing externally. The author denies an exclusive division between external and mental when the attribute does not produce essential effects.
This is illustrated by the mind being attributed with a mental form without the form’s effects.
5
Differentiate predication of 'form' vs 'knowledge'
The mind is described by knowledge derived from the form, not by the form itself. For example, we say 'the mind is knowing' but not 'the mind is hot' from the form of heat.
This clarifies that attribution of the form to the mind is indirect.
