النكتة الثالثة: الاتصاف الانضمامي، وهو ما كان بانضمام الصفة إلى الموصوف يستدعي تحقق الحاشيتين في طرف الاتصاف، إن خارجا ففي الخارج وإن ذهنا ففي الذهن، هذا ما هو المشهور، واستدل به بعض أجلة المتأخرين على أن الصورة موجدة في الخارج، وقال: لها اعتباران: اعتبارها من حيث قيامها بالذهن، ومعروضيتها للعوارض، وبهذا الاعتبار موجودة في الخارج لذلك ولترتب الآثار عليها، واعتبارها من حيث هي، مع قطع النظر عن العوارض، وهي بهذا الاعتبار معلومة وموجودة في الذهن، ولم يحفظ أن الذهن أيضا متصف به ؛ فإن حلول الشخص مستلزم لحلول الطبيعة، وقد صرح به الفاضل الجونفوري المحمود، وأيضا يترتب عليه الآثار، وهي لوازم الماهية، فالصورة الشخصية ومعلومها سواء، فما وجه الفرق؟ وتحقيق المقام أن من المعلوم بالضرورة نحوا من الوجود، وآثارها غير لوازم الماهية، ثم بعد التفتيش يظهر نحو آخر من الوجود، بحيث لا يترتب عليه تلك الآثار الضرورية، وإن كانت يترتب عليه آثار أخر ولوازم الماهية كالجسم الغير المتحيز. ولا شك أن الصورة الذهنية لا يترتب عليها آثار ماهياتها؛ فإن صورة الجسم غير متحيزة، وصورة النار غير محزقة، فيكون موجودة ذهنية، ولا نعني بالموجود الذهني إلا هذا، وأما مطلق الآثار فعسى أن لا يخلو عنه موجود. تبصرة في تحقيق المحصورات وأما الاتصاف الانضمامي الخارجي إن أريد به كون الموصوف في الخارج بحيث ينضم إليه الصفة فمن المبيّن أن ذلك لا يستدعي وجود الصفة فيه؛ فإن من الجائز أن لا يترتب عليها آثار الماهية المذكورة، بل البرهان يساعد عليه، اللهم إلا أن يراد بالوجود الخارجي الوجود خارج الأذهان سوى الموصوف، وحينئذ لا ينفع لبعض الأجلة. وإن أريد كون الموصوف في الخارج بحيث ينضم إليه الصفة فيه فعسى أن لا يكون بين الخارجي والذهني انحصار؛ فإن من الجائز أن يكون الموصوف في الخارج وينضم إليه الصفة، بحيث لا يترتب عليها الآثار، كما في اتصاف العقل بالصورة العقلية. هذا كله إن أريد بالاتصاف الحلول مطلقا، وإن أريد به تعلق خاص بحيث يحمل به مشتق الحال على المحل فاتصاف الذهن ليس اتصافا، ولا شبهة في أن الخارجي بكلا المعنيين مستلزم لوجود الصفة فيه، ولا شبهة في انحصاره في الخارجي والذهني. نقلت: يحمل على الذهن مشتق الصورة، وهو العالم، فيكون الذهن متصفا بها؟ قلت الصورة علة ومنشأ لحصول صفة انتزاعية، وهي العلم والانكشاف، والذهن متصف بها ويحمل مشتقها عليه لا مشتق الصورة، كيف ومشتق صورة الحرارة الحار، والبرودة البارد، ولا شك في امتناع حملهما عليه، فتأمل فيه واحفظ لعلك لا تجد من غيرنا، فتوكل على الله فإنه أعلم بالصواب.
The third subtlety: extrinsic attribution, which is the attribution of a quality to a subject by conjunction, requires the actual existence of both extremes in the realm of attribution: if external then in the external world, if mental then in the mind. This is what is well-known. Some later eminent scholars used this to argue that the form is existent in the external world, saying: It has two aspects: an aspect in terms of its inherence in the mind and its being subject to accidents, and in this aspect it is existent in the external world because of that and because effects follow from it; and an aspect in terms of itself, disregarding accidents, and in this aspect it is known and existent in the mind. But it has not been established that the mind is also described by it; for the instantiation of the individual entails the instantiation of the nature, and the virtuous Junpuri Mahmud stated this explicitly. Also, effects follow from it, which are the necessary concomitants of the essence. So the individual form and its known counterpart are the same. What then is the difference? The verification of the matter is that it is known necessarily that there is some mode of existence, and its effects are not necessary concomitants of the essence. Then after investigation, another mode of existence appears, such that those necessary effects do not follow from it, although other effects and necessary concomitants of the essence do follow, like a body that is not spatially extended. There is no doubt that the mental form does not have the effects of its quiddities; for the form of a body is not extended, and the form of fire does not burn, so it is a mental existent. By mental existent we mean nothing other than this. As for absolute effects, perhaps no existing thing is devoid of them. A note on the verification of the restricted propositions: As for external extrinsic attribution, if by it is meant that the subject is in the external world such that the attribute joins it, then it is clear that this does not require the existence of the attribute in it; for it is possible that the effects of that quiddity do not follow from it, rather the proof supports this. Unless external existence is meant to be existence outside the minds other than the subject, in which case it is of no use to some of the eminent scholars. If by it is meant that the subject is in the external world such that the attribute joins it in it, then perhaps there is no exclusive division between external and mental; for it is possible that the subject is external and the attribute joins it without its effects following, as in the attribution of the mind with the mental form. All this is if by attribution is meant inherence absolutely. If by it is meant a specific relation such that the participle of the state is predicated of the place, then the attribution of the mind is not an attribution. There is no doubt that external attribution in both senses requires the existence of the attribute in it, and there is no doubt that it is exclusively divided into external and mental. I was asked: Is the derivative of 'form' predicated of the mind? The mind is knowing, so it is described by it? I said: The form is a cause and source for the acquisition of an extracted attribute, which is knowledge and disclosure. The mind is described by it, and its derivative is predicated of it, not the derivative of 'form'. How so? The derivative of the form of heat is 'hot', and of cold is 'cold', and there is no doubt about the impossibility of predicating them of the mind. So ponder and memorize, perhaps you will not find this from anyone other than us. Then rely on God, for He knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the third subtlety (النكتة الثالثة) concerning extrinsic attribution (الاتصاف الانضمامي). The author begins by defining extrinsic attribution as the joining of a quality to its subject, and states that for such attribution to be real, both the subject and the attribute must exist in the same realm — either both external or both mental. This is the well-known view. Then the author reports an argument by some later eminent scholars who used this principle to prove that the mental form (الصورة الذهنية) has external existence. They argue that the form has two aspects: one as it inheres in the mind and is subject to accidental properties (in which aspect it exists externally, because effects follow from it in the external world), and another as it is considered in itself, disregarding those accidental properties (in which aspect it is known and exists in the mind). The objection raised is that it is not established that the mind is described by the form in the same way; for the instantiation of an individual entails the instantiation of its nature, and effects that are necessary concomitants of the essence follow from it. So if the individual form in the mind and the known form are the same, what distinguishes them? The author then provides the verification (تحقيق المقام): It is known necessarily that there exists a mode of existence that has effects that are not the necessary concomitants of the essence (i.e., essential properties). Through investigation, another mode of existence appears where these essential effects do not follow, though other effects (the necessary concomitants) do follow, like a body that is not spatially extended — spatial extension is an effect of a body, but mental body lacks it. Thus the mental form lacks the specific effects of its quiddity (e.g., the form of fire does not burn), so it is a mental existent. The author then clarifies in a note (تبصرة) that external extrinsic attribution does not necessarily require the attribute to have external existence in the same sense as the subject, because it is possible that the attribute does not produce the essential effects. If external existence is taken to mean existence outside the mind other than the subject, then the argument is not useful. However, if the subject is external and the attribute joins it without producing essential effects (as in the mind being attributed with a mental form), then the division between external and mental is not exhaustive. This all applies if attribution means inherence absolutely. If attribution means a specific relation such that the participle of the state is predicated of the place, then the mind is not attributed with the form in that sense (one does not say 'the mind is a form' but 'the mind is knowing'). Finally, a question-answer exchange clarifies that the mind is described by the extracted attribute of knowledge derived from the form, not by the form itself. For example, the derivative of 'heat' is 'hot', which cannot be predicated of the mind. The author advises the student to ponder and memorize this insight, attributing it to himself.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
5 min
Absolute Necessary Proposition and the First Objection
Defines absolute necessary proposition and addresses the first objection concerning predicate of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the absolute necessary proposition and distinguish it from conditional necessity, using the example of existence as a predicate.

Core Idea
Absolute necessary proposition: predicate holds as long as the subject's essence exists, not with the condition of existence.

The definition of the absolute necessary proposition is foundational for understanding modal logic in Islamic thought. This lesson will unpack the standard definition and examine a key objection when the predicate is existence itself. By the end, you will see how logicians distinguish between necessity in the time of existence and necessity with the condition of existence, preserving the precision of modal judgments.

Guided Path
1
State the definition of the absolute necessary proposition
Identify the terms: it is a proposition in which the predicate is affirmed or denied with necessity as long as the subject's essence exists. Note that this definition ties necessity to the continued existence of the subject's essence.
The definition is well-known (ishtahara).
2
Understand the first objection
If the predicate is existence itself, then the proposition 'the subject exists' would be necessary as long as the subject exists, yet the subject is a specific possible (mumkin khāṣṣ). This would imply that necessity and possibility coexist, and if not allowed, everything would be necessary.
The objection challenges the definition when the predicate is existence.
3
Analyze the response: two types of necessity
The answer distinguishes between necessity in the time of existence (ḍarūra fī zamān al-wujūd) and necessity with the condition of existence (bi-sharṭ al-wujūd). The predicate of existence in such propositions is the latter, not the former. Therefore, those propositions are not necessary in the absolute sense.
The necessary proposition considers the first type, not the second.
4
Differentiate balanced vs. judgmental necessity
The author concludes that the correct response involves affirming 'balanced necessity' (al-ḍarūriyya al-mīzāniyya) over 'judgmental necessity' (al-ḥikamiyya) based on essential necessity, and denying logical specific possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ al-manṭiqī) because it is more specific than judgmental possibility.
This refines the modal categories.

Source Text

اشْتَهَرَ تَعْرِيفُ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ بِأَنَّهَا الَّتِي يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ ثُبُوتِ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ أَوْ سَلْبِهِ عَنْهُ، مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الْمَوْضُوعِ مَوْجُودًا. وَفِيهِ شَكٌّ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ ، الْأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ إِذَا كَانَ الْمَحْمُولُ هُوَ الْوُجُودُ لَزِمَ عَدَمُ مُنَافَاةِ الضَّرُورَةِ لِلْإِمْكَانِ الْخَاصِّ؛ لِأَنَّ الْوُجُودَ ضَرُورِيٌّ لِمَوْضُوعِهِ مَا دَامَ مَوْجُودًا، وَهُوَ مُمْكِنٌ خَاصٌّ، وَإِلَّا صَارَ الْعَالَمُ كُلُّهُ وَاجِبًا. وَأُجِيبَ بِالْفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الضَّرُورَةِ فِي زَمَانِ الْوُجُودِ وَبَيْنَهَا بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ، وَالْمُتَحَقِّقُ فِيمَا مَحْمُولُهُ الْوُجُودُ الثَّانِي، وَالْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الضَّرُورِيَّةِ هُوَ الْأَوَّلُ، فَلَا نُسَلِّمُ صِدْقَ تِلْكَ الْقَضَايَا ضَرُورِيَّةً. وَفِيهِ نَظَرٌ، أَمَّا أَوَّلًا فَلِأَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَ الْمَوْجُودَ مَحْفُوفٌ بِوُجُوبَيْنِ: وُجُوبِ الْعِلَّةِ فِي زَمَانِ وُجُودِهِ، وَوُجُوبٍ لَهُ بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ، وَالْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الضَّرُورِيَّةِ مِنْ مُطْلَقِ الضَّرُورَةِ، ذَاتِيَّةً كَانَتْ أَوْ غَيْرِيَّةً مِنْ عِلَّةٍ. وَأَمَّا ثَانِيًا: فَلِأَنَّ الشَّيْخَ قَالَ فِي «الْإِشَارَاتِ»: الضَّرُورَةُ قَدْ تَكُونُ عَلَى الْإِطْلَاقِ، كَقَوْلِنَا اللَّهُ تَعَالَى حَيٌّ، وَقَدْ تَكُونُ مُتَعَلِّقَةً بِشَرْطٍ، وَالشَّرْطُ إِمَّا دَوَامُ وُجُودِ الذَّاتِ، أَوْ دَوَامُ كَوْنِ ذَاتِ الْمَوْضُوعِ مَوْصُوفًا بِمَا وُضِعَ مَعَهُ، أَوْ بِشَرْطِ وَصْفِ الْمَحْمُولِ، أَوْ وَقْتٍ مُعَيَّنٍ أَوْ غَيْرِ مُعَيَّنٍ. وَالضَّرُورَةُ بِالشَّرْطِ الْأَوَّلِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ بِالْاعْتِبَارِ غَيْرَ الضَّرُورَةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الَّتِي لَا يُلْتَفَتُ فِيهَا إِلَى الشَّرْطِ، فَقَدْ تَشْتَرِكَانِ أَيْضًا فِي مَعْنَى اشْتِرَاكِ الْأَخَصِّ تَحْتَ الْأَعَمِّ، أَوِ اشْتِرَاكِ أَخَصَّيْنِ تَحْتَ أَعَمَّ، إِذَا اشْتُرِطَ فِي الْمَشْرُوطَةِ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ لِلذَّاتِ وُجُودٌ دَائِمًا، وَمَا يَشْتَرِكَانِ فِيهِ هُوَ الْمُرَادُ مِنْ قَوْلِهِمْ ضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ. فَإِذَنِ الْحَقُّ فِي الْجَوَابِ صِدْقُ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ الْمِيزَانِيَّةِ، دُونَ الْحِكَمِيَّةِ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ، وَمَنْعُ صِدْقِ الْإِمْكَانِ الْخَاصِّ الْمَنْطِقِيِّ؛ فَإِنَّ الْإِمْكَانَ الْخَاصَّ الْمَنْطِقِيَّ أَخَصُّ مِنَ الْحُكْمِيِّ؛ لِأَنَّ سَلْبَ الْأَعَمِّ أَخَصُّ.
The definition of the absolute necessary proposition is well-known: it is that in which it is judged that the predicate necessarily holds for the subject or is negated from it, as long as the essence of the subject exists. There is a doubt in it from two aspects. The first: that if the predicate is existence, it would entail that necessity does not conflict with specific possibility, because existence is necessary for its subject as long as it exists, and it is a specific possible, otherwise the entire world would become necessary. It was answered by distinguishing between necessity in the time of existence and necessity with the condition of existence, and what is actualized in what has existence as its predicate is the second, and what is considered in the necessary proposition is the first, so we do not affirm that those propositions are necessary. There is a consideration in it. As for the first: because the existing possible is surrounded by two necessities: the necessity of the cause in the time of its existence, and a necessity for it with the condition of existence, and what is considered in the necessary proposition is absolute necessity, whether essential or accidental from a cause. As for the second: because the Shaykh said in al-Isharat: Necessity may be absolute, like our saying 'Allah is alive,' or it may be dependent on a condition. The condition is either the permanence of the existence of the essence, or the permanence of the subject's essence being described with what it is posited with, or with the condition of a predicate description, or a specific or non-specific time. The necessity under the first condition, even if it is in consideration different from the absolute necessity that does not heed the condition, they also share in the meaning of a specific under a general, or two specifics under a general, if it is conditioned in the conditional that the essence does not have perpetual existence, and what they share is what is meant by their saying 'absolute necessary.' So then the truth in the answer is the truth of the balanced necessary proposition, not the judgmental one in which it is judged by essential necessity, and the denial of the truth of the logical specific possibility; for the logical specific possibility is more specific than the judgmental, because the negation of a more general is more specific.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage expands on the definition of the absolute necessary proposition (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa) and examines an objection related to the predicate being existence. The author begins by stating the standard definition: a proposition where the predicate is necessarily affirmed or denied of the subject as long as the subject's essence exists. The first doubt raised is: if the predicate is existence itself, then the proposition would be necessary as long as the subject exists, but the subject is possible (mumkin khāṣṣ). This would imply that necessity does not contradict possibility, and if this were not allowed, the entire world would be necessary. The answer proposed distinguishes between necessity in the time of existence (ḍarūra fī zamān al-wujūd) and necessity with the condition of existence (bi-sharṭ al-wujūd). The predicate of existence in such propositions is the second type, whereas the necessary proposition considers the first type, so those propositions are not necessary in the required sense. The author then offers a critique (fīhi naẓar) of this answer. First, the existing possible (mumkin mawjūd) is surrounded by two necessities: the necessity of the cause during its existence and the necessity with the condition of existence. The necessary proposition considers absolute necessity, whether essential or accidental from a cause. Second, the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā) in al-Ishārāt noted that necessity can be absolute (e.g., 'Allah is alive') or conditional. The conditional necessity may involve the permanence of the subject's existence, or other conditions. The absolute necessary proposition is distinct from the conditional one, but they can share under a general concept. The conclusion is that the correct answer is to affirm the 'balanced' necessity (al-ḍarūriyya al-mīzāniyya) as opposed to the 'judgmental' necessity (al-ḥikamiyya) based on essential necessity, and to deny the logical specific possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ al-manṭiqī) because it is more specific than the judgmental specific possibility. This discussion preserves the precision of modal logic in distinguishing types of necessity and possibility.

Key Terms

الإمكان الخاص
الضرورة الذاتية
الضرورة الميزانية

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary proposition is defined by the predicate's necessity as long as the subject's essence exists, and the objection about existence is resolved by distinguishing necessity in the time of existence from necessity with the condition of existence.

Resolving the Paradox of the Set of Concepts and Its Contradictory
Author solves a paradox about part-whole contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical puzzle about the set of all concepts and its contradictory, and explain the solution using the distinction between conceptual and external composite.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a set of concepts can be an external part without logical inconsistency.

This passage presents a logical puzzle: if we take the set of all concepts, its contradictory must be the removal of that set. But since the set includes all concepts, the contradictory itself is a concept and thus part of the set, leading to a part being the contradictory of the whole. The author resolves this by distinguishing between a conceptual concept and an external composite, showing that the part is not predicated of the whole, so no contradiction follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Puzzle
Read the first part of the passage to understand the problem: A concept must have a contradictory (its removal). But if the set contains all concepts, then the contradictory itself is a concept and thus part of the set, implying the part is the contradictory of the whole.
The puzzle hinges on the assumption that the contradictory is included in the set it contradicts.
2
Analyze the Author's Solution
The author introduces two distinctions: the set is a 'conceptual concept' (mafhum tasawwuri) and an 'external composite' (murakkab khariji). Each concept is an 'external part' that is not predicated of the whole, nor is it predicated with the whole of a third thing.
The key is that the part and whole do not share a subject of predication in a way that would cause a contradiction.
3
Apply the Part-Whole Rule
The author argues that the utmost implication is that the contradictory of a conceptual concept is an external part of it. This is not impossible unless both are true of one subject. Since the part is not predicated of the whole, they are not true of the same subject.
The rejection of co-predication avoids the paradox.
4
Understand the Remaining Impossibility
The author notes that it remains impossible for the contradictory to be an intellectual part (juz' 'aqli) of the contradictory, because that would force them to coincide on an individual due to the inseparability of essential properties.
This distinguishes external parts (contingent) from intellectual parts (essential).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في التناقض
مفهوم، فلا بد له من نقيض هو رفع هذا المجموع، وهو أيضا معلوم للباري ، فهو أيضا داخل في هذا المجموع ، فالجزء نقيض الكل. فالصواب في الحل أن يقال: إن مجموع المفهومات مفهوم تصوري ومركب خارجي، وكل مفهوم جزء خارجي لا يحمل على الكل أصلا، ولا هو مع الكل يحملان على ثالث ، فغاية ما لزم أن يكون نقيض مفهوم تصوري جزء خارجيا له، ولا نسلم استحالة ذلك، إنما يستحيل صدقهما على موضوع واحد، وهو غير لازم؛ فإن مجموع المفهومات ،رفعه يستحيل صدقهما على شيء واحد. نعم، يستحيل كون النقيض جزء عقليا للنقيض ؛ فإن ذلك يوجب اجتماعهما على شيء واحد هو الفرد؛ لاستحالة انفكاك الذاتي، وهو مستحيل. هذا، والعلم الحقيقي عند علام الغيوب، العليم الخبير .
Section on contradiction. A concept must have a contradictory which is the removal of this set, and it is also known to the Creator, so it is also included in this set, so the part is the contradictory of the whole. The correct solution is to say: The set of concepts is a conceptual concept and an external composite, and every concept is an external part that is not predicated of the whole at all, nor is it together with the whole predicated of a third thing. So the utmost that follows is that the contradictory of a conceptual concept is an external part of it, and we do not concede the impossibility of that; rather, impossibility is only for their being true of one subject, which is not necessary. Indeed, the removal of the set of concepts makes it impossible for them to be true of one thing. Yes, it is impossible for the contradictory to be an intellectual part of the contradictory, because that would require their conjunction on one thing, namely the individual, due to the impossibility of the separation of the essential, which is impossible. This, and true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen, the All-Knowing, the All-Aware.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage addresses a logical puzzle about the contradictory of a set of concepts. The author first presents the problem: if we have a set of concepts, its contradictory must be the removal of that set. But if the set includes all concepts, then the contradictory itself would also be a concept and thus part of the set, making the part the contradictory of the whole. The solution clarifies that the set is a conceptual concept and an external composite. Each concept is an external part not predicable of the whole, nor predicable with the whole of a third. Thus, the contradictory of a conceptual concept can be an external part without the impossible situation of both being true of the same subject. However, it remains impossible for the contradictory to be an intellectual part of the contradictory, as that would require them to coincide on an individual due to the inseparability of essential properties. The passage concludes with a pious reminder that true knowledge belongs to God.

The Puzzle of Absolute Negation
Author presents and solves a puzzle about the contradictory of absolute negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the logical puzzle of absolute negation and explain why its contradictory does not lead to a paradox.

Core Idea
Absolute negation encompasses both contradictories—its contradictory resolves by removing both.

In classical logic, absolute negation (salb mutlaq) is broader than both the negation of a positive and the negation of a negation. This breadth creates a puzzle: if absolute negation is a concept, its contradictory would be a specific instance of it, leading to both being true of the same thing. How can this be? The author walks us through the problem and its elegant solution.

Guided Path
1
Define absolute negation
Absolute negation is more general than both 'negation of existence' and 'negation of negation'. It includes any kind of negation.
2
Identify the paradox
If absolute negation is a concept, its contradictory (negation of absolute negation) would be a specific instance of absolute negation. This would make the contradictory a type of its own contradictory, and both could be true of a single individual (the negation of negation).
This is the core problem.
3
Examine the rejected solution
Some scholars said that negation is not the contradictory of the negation of negation; rather, its contradictory is the negation of the negation of negation. The author points out that this fails because the negation of the negation of negation necessarily implies negation, so it is still a coextensive necessary concomitant.
This solution does not escape the circularity.
4
Understand the correct solution
Absolute negation encompasses both contradictories. Therefore, its negation (removal) is equivalent to removing both contradictories at once. Hence, there is no absurdity in absolute negation being included under its own contradictory—the impossible can imply its contradictory, especially when it involves the removal of both.
The key insight: absolute negation's scope makes its contradictory harmless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ههنا إشكال آخر، هو أن مفهوم السلب المطلق الذي هو أعم من سلب الثبوت وسلب السلب ،مفهوم ، فنقيضه سلبه، وهو حصة له، والسلب المطلق المسلوب نوع له، فيلزم أن يكون نقيض ذاتيا لنقيض، ويلزم اجتماعهما في الصدق على فرد سلب السلب. أجاب عنه بعض الأجلة أن السلب ليس نقيضا لسلب السلب، بل نقيضه سلب سلب السلب، وهو ليس محمولا عليه أصلا، ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن المرفوع وإن سلم عدم كونه نقيضا للرفع لكنه لا أقل من أن يكون لازما مساويا لنقيضه ؛ فإن سلب سلب السلب مستلزم للسلب ،قطعا، فيلزم أن يكون اللازم المساوي لنقيض سلب السلب نوعا وذاتيا له فيلزم الخلف. فالصواب في الجواب أن يقال: إن السلب الأعم من سلب الوجود وسلبه من الأمور الشاملة للنقيضين، فرفعه في قوة رفع النقيضين، فلا استبعاد في كونه مندرجا تحت نقيضه ؛ فإن غاية ما يلزم استلزامه لنقيضه، ولا استحالة ؛ فإن المحال ربما يستلزم نقيضه، لا سيما المحال المتضمن لارتفاع النقيضين فأتقن هذه المباحث، وأسلكه (۱) في سلك النفائس المختصة بهذا الكتاب، وتوكل على الله سبحانه ؛ فإنه الجواد الخبير .
Then here is another problem: the concept of absolute negation, which is more general than the negation of existence and the negation of negation, is a concept, so its contradictory is its negation, which is a specific instance of it, and the negated absolute negation is a type of it, so it would follow that the contradictory is an essential property of its contradictory, and that they are both true of an individual of the negation of negation. Some noble scholars answered that negation is not the contradictory of the negation of negation; rather its contradictory is the negation of the negation of negation, and it is not predicated of it at all, but what is in it is not hidden; for the lifted [negation], even if it is granted that it is not the contradictory of the lift, it is at least a coextensive necessary concomitant of its contradictory; for the negation of the negation of negation necessarily implies negation, so it would follow that the coextensive necessary concomitant of the contradictory of the negation of negation is a type and an essential of it, leading to a vicious regress. The correct answer is to say: absolute negation, which is more general than the negation of existence and its negation, is among things that encompass both contradictories, so its removal is equivalent to the removal of both contradictories, so there is no absurdity in it being included under its contradictory; for the utmost that follows is its implying its contradictory, and there is no impossibility; for the impossible may imply its contradictory, especially the impossible that involves the removal of both contradictories. So master these investigations, and string them into the necklace of precious gems specific to this book, and rely on God Almighty, for He is the Generous, the All-Knowing.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage presents a logical puzzle regarding absolute negation (salb mutlaq). Absolute negation is more general than both the negation of a positive and the negation of a negation. The problem arises because if absolute negation is a concept, its contradictory (its negation) would be a specific instance of it, and the negated absolute negation would be a type of it. This would make the contradictory an essential property of its own contradictory, leading to both being true of a single individual (the negation of negation). The author rejects an attempted solution that denies that negation is the contradictory of the negation of negation, arguing that this response fails because the negation of the negation of negation necessarily implies negation, making the coextensive concomitant of the contradictory a type of it, which is circular. The correct solution is that absolute negation encompasses both contradictories, so its removal is in effect the removal of both, meaning no absurdity follows from it being under its own contradictory; the impossible can indeed imply its contradictory, especially when it involves the removal of both contradictories.

Definition of Contradiction and the Eight Unities
Author defines contradictory propositions and lists the eight conditions for unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test any pair of propositions for genuine contradiction by verifying the identity of the judgmental relation and the eight unities.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires that the negation directly removes the same affirmation under identical conditions.

In logic, not every affirmation and negation are contradictory. The author explains that genuine contradiction demands that the negation be the exact removal of the same affirmation, and that eight conditions (unities) must be met. Understanding these unities helps you avoid false contradictions in reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of contradiction
The author defines contradiction as two propositions differing such that, by their essence, the truth of each implies the falsehood of the other. This is the core of logical opposition.
This definition excludes cases where the opposition is accidental or indirect.
2
Recognize the essential condition
The negation must be the removal of the very same affirmation. If the negation removes a different affirmation, or if it removes a coextensive necessary, then it is not essential contradiction.
The judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya) must be identical.
3
List the eight unities
Logicians require unity of subject, predicate, condition, addition, part/whole, potentiality/actuality, time, and place. These ensure that the two propositions are about exactly the same thing in the same respect.
Some scholars subsume all except subject and predicate under these two.
4
Apply the test to a pair of propositions
Take two propositions and check each unity. For example, 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing' are contradictory only if time, place, and other conditions are identical.
If any condition differs, the contradiction is only apparent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتَنَاقُصُ الْقَضِيَّتَيْنِ اخْتِلَافُهُمَا ، بِحَيْثُ يَقْتَضِي لِذَاتِهِ صِدْقُ كُلَّ كَذب وشروط الْأُخرى، وَبِالْعَكس)، أي يقتضي لذاته كذب كل صدق الأخرى، (وذلك بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ إِذَا كَانَ ذلك السلب (رَفْعَهُ)، أي رفع ذلك الإيجاب ( بِعَيْنِهِ ) ؛ فإنه إذا كان رفع إيجاب آخر فلا تعاند بين ذلك الإيجاب وهذا السلب، وإن كان كما بين إيجاب الملزوم ورفع لازمه المساوي فليس لذاته. (فَلَا بُدَّ من اتِّحَادِ النِّسْبَةِ الْحُكْمِيَّةِ، أي لا بد من كون النسبة التي سلبت في السالبة بعينها التي في الموجبة ، (وَحَصَرُوهُ فِي الْوَحْدَاتِ الثَّمَانِيَةِ المَشْهُورَة) وحدة الموضوع، والمحمول، والشرط، والإضافة، والجزء، والكل، والقوة، والفعل، والزمان والمكان، (وَبَعْضُهُمْ أَدْرَجَ بَعْضَهَا فِي بعض) ؛ فإن ما سوى وحدة الموضوع والمحمول مندرجة فيهما
And the contradicteriness of two propositions is their difference such that by its essence the truth of each requires the falsehood of the other, and vice versa. That is, by its essence it requires the falsehood of each truth of the other. And that is by affirmation and negation when that negation is its removal, i.e., the removal of that very affirmation. For if it is the removal of another affirmation, then there is no opposition between that affirmation and this negation, and if it is like between the affirmation of the entailing and the removal of its coextensive necessary, then it is not by its essence. So there must be unity of the judgmental relation, i.e., the relation that is negated in the negative must be the very same as that in the affirmative. And they restricted it to the famous eight unities: unity of subject, predicate, condition, addition, part, whole, potentiality, actuality, time, and place. And some of them included some of these in others; for what is other than unity of subject and predicate is included in them.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage defines the logical relation of contradiction between two propositions. It states that two propositions are contradictory if, by their very essence, the truth of each necessitates the falsehood of the other and vice versa. This occurs specifically when one is affirmative and the other is its direct negation, not the negation of a different proposition. The author also clarifies that if the negation is of a coextensive necessary, it is not essential contradiction. The essential condition is that the judgmental relation (the copula or relation between subject and predicate) must be identical in both propositions. Logicians have enumerated eight 'unities' required for genuine contradiction: unity of subject, predicate, condition (e.g., time or circumstance), addition (e.g., 'as such'), part vs. whole, potentiality vs. actuality, and time and place. However, some scholars subsume all except subject and predicate under these two, arguing that the others are reducible to modifications of the subject or predicate.

Key Terms
تناقص القضيتين
الوحدات الثمانية
الجدية الحكمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) They must be about different times.
D) They must both be affirmative.

Key Takeaway
Genuine contradiction requires that the negation directly remove the same affirmation under identical conditions (the eight unities).

The Contradictory of Negation: Affirmation or Double Negation?
Author presents and rejects a doubt about the contradiction relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the standard logical consensus that affirmation is the direct contradictory of negation and recognize the minority view that conflates it with double negation.

Core Idea
Affirmation is the direct contradictory of negation; double negation is a different logical operation.

This passage addresses a fundamental logical doubt: is affirmation truly the contradictory of negation? Some later scholars argued that the contradictory of a negative proposition is actually the negation of negation, with affirmation being merely a necessary concomitant. The author of this commentary firmly rejects this position as breaking the consensus of logicians. By examining this debate, you will understand a key distinction in propositional opposition that underpins valid inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
Read the opening phrase: 'And here there is a doubt: that affirmation is the contradictory of negation.' What exactly is being questioned?
The doubt is about the nature of the contradiction relationship between affirmative and negative propositions.
2
Extract the consensus claim
Find the statement: 'all the people of logic have agreed that affirmation is the contradictory of negation.' This is presented as an established fact supported by jma (consensus).
The author uses the term ijma, borrowed from jurisprudence, to emphasize the strength of this agreement.
3
Identify the dissenting view
Locate the scholars who denied this: al-Sadr al-Shirazi and the author of al-Ufuq al-Mubin. What did they claim? They said the contradictory of the negative is the negation of negation, and affirmation is only a necessary concomitant.
This view confuses the direct contradictory with double negation.
4
Understand why it breaks consensus
The author argues that denying affirmation as the contradictory of negation violates the unanimous agreement of logicians. Even the commentator of al-Mutali is cited in support, though the quotation is cut off.
The incomplete citation suggests the author intended to provide a stronger refutation.
5
Apply the consensus rule
Given the consensus, you can now correctly identify the contradictory of any negative proposition: it is the affirmative proposition with the same subject and predicate, not a double negation.
This rule is crucial for conversion and contraposition in categorical syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ نَقِيضُ السَّلْب)؛ فإن اختلاف الموجبة والسالبة بحيث يستلزم صدقُ كلَّ كذب الأخرى، وبالعكس لذاته، (ومن أنكره) وقال إن نقيض السالبة سلب السلب، والإيجاب لازم للنقيض، كالصدر الشيرازي المعاصر للمحقق الدواني، وصاحب «الأفق المبين» (فَخَرَق الإِجْمَاعَ ؛ فإن أهل المنطق كلهم اتفقوا على أن الإيجاب نقيض السلب، حتى قال السيد المحقق: إن قول شارح المطالع نقيض كل شيء
And here there is a doubt: that affirmation is the contradictory of negation; for the difference between the affirmative and the negative is such that the truth of each necessitates the falsehood of the other and vice versa by its essence. And whoever denies it and says that the contradictory of the negative is the negation of negation, and affirmation is a necessary concomitant of the contradictory, like al-Sadr al-Shirazi, contemporary of al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani, and the author of “al-Ufuq al-Mubin”, has broken the consensus; for all the people of logic have agreed that affirmation is the contradictory of negation, to the extent that al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq said: The statement of the commentator of al-Mutali‘: 'The contradictory of everything...'
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage raises a doubt about the nature of contradiction: is affirmation directly the contradictory of negation? The standard view is that they are contradictory, as the truth of one necessitates the falsehood of the other by essence. Some scholars, such as al-Sadr al-Shirazi and the author of al-Ufuq al-Mubin, argued that the contradictory of a negative is actually the negation of negation, and affirmation is merely a necessary concomitant of that contradictory. The author of this commentary strongly rejects this, accusing such thinkers of breaking consensus, as logicians unanimously hold that affirmation is the contradictory of negation. The passage ends mid-sentence with a quotation from the commentator of al-Mutali‘, but the argument is incomplete. The author likely intended to complete the refutation, but the text cuts off.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic logic, affirmation is directly the contradictory of negation, and this consensus is essential for valid logical operations.

Definition of Contradiction and the Eight Unities
Author defines contradiction and enumerates the eight conditions of unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight conditions of unity required for genuine contradiction and explain why the negation of negation is a distinct contradictory from affirmation.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires eight unities; the negation of negation is a different contradictory than affirmation.

This passage from a classical logic text defines contradiction as an essential opposition between affirmation and negation, but only when they share the same relational aspect. The author lists eight conditions of unity that must be met for genuine contradiction, and then addresses a debated point: whether the contradictory of a negative is affirmation or the negation of negation. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to spot genuine contradictions in Islamic theological and jurisprudential reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of contradiction
The author defines contradiction as an essential opposition where the truth of each proposition entails the falsehood of the other. This requires that the negation be the exact removal of the same affirmation, not a different one.
Note that the Arabic specifies 'رفعه بعينه' (its removal identically).
2
List the eight unities
Extract the eight conditions: unity of subject, predicate, condition (sharṭ), relation (iḍāfa), part (juz'), whole (kull), potentiality (quwwa), actuality (fiʻl), time (zamān), and place (makān). Some scholars include the last six within subject and predicate.
Different lists may combine time and place.
3
Analyze the doubt about the contradictory of negation
Some scholars (e.g., al-Ṣadr al-Shīrāzī) claimed that the contradictory of a negative proposition is the negation of negation (salb al-salb), not simple affirmation. This raises a problem: does a negative have two contradictories?
The doubt is based on the idea that negation removes existence, and its removal brings back existence, so the negation of negation equals affirmation.
4
Follow the author's refutation
The author asserts that the consensus of logicians is that affirmation is the contradictory of negation. He argues that our direct conceptual grasp of affirmation and negation shows they are mutually exclusive without needing the negation of negation.
He says rejecting this breaks not just consensus but also necessity (ḍarūra).
5
Understand the solution about negation of negation
The author clarifies that negation applies only to existence, not to another negation. The negation of negation removes the existence of the negation, effectively producing an affirmative proposition with a negative subject or predicate. Thus, the negation of negation is a separate contradictory from the simple negative, whose contradictory is affirmation.
The solution preserves the standard view while explaining the opponent's confusion.

وتَنَاقُضُ الْقَضِيَّتَيْنِ اخْتِلَافُهُمَا ، بِحَيْثُ يَقْتَضِي لِذَاتِهِ صِدْقُ كُلَّ كَذِبَ شَرْطُ الْأُخْرَى، وَبِالْعَكْسِ، أي يقتضي لذاته كذب كل صدق الأخرى، (وذلك بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ إِذَا كَانَ ذلك السلب (رَفْعَهُ)، أي رفع ذلك الإيجاب ( بِعَيْنِهِ ) ؛ فإنه إذا كان رفع إيجاب آخر فلا تعاند بين ذلك الإيجاب وهذا السلب، وإن كان كما بين إيجاب الملزوم ورفع لازمه المساوي فليس لذاته. (فَلَا بُدَّ من اتِّحَادِ النِّسْبَةِ الْحُكْمِيَّةِ، أي لا بد من كون النسبة التي سلبت في السالبة بعينها التي في الموجبة ، (وَحَصَرُوهُ فِي الْوَحْدَاتِ الثَّمَانِيَةِ المَشْهُورَة) وحدة الموضوع، والمحمول، والشرط، والإضافة، والجزء، والكل، والقوة، والفعل، والزمان والمكان، (وَبَعْضُهُمْ أَدْرَجَ بَعْضَهَا فِي بعض) ؛ فإن ما سوى وحدة الموضوع والمحمول مندرجة فيهما. (وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ نَقِيضُ السَّلْبِ)؛ فإن اختلاف الموجبة والسالبة بحيث يستلزم صدقُ كلَّ كذب الأخرى، وبالعكس لذاته، (ومن أنكره) وقال إن نقيض السالبة سلب السلب، والإيجاب لازم للنقيض، كالصدر الشيرازي المعاصر للمحقق الدواني، وصاحب «الأفق المبين» (فَخَرَق الإِجْمَاعَ ؛ فإن أهل المنطق كلهم اتفقوا على أن الإيجاب نقيض السلب، حتى قال السيد المحقق: إن قول شارح المطالع نقيض كل شيء رفعه باطل، والصحيح رفع كل شيء نقيضه، بل خرق الضرورة أيضا ؛ فإنا إذا لاحظنا مفهوم السلب والإيجاب نحكم باستحالة اجتماعهما صدقا وكذبا لذاتيهما ، من غير ملاحظة سلب السلب. وَسَلْبُ السَّلْبِ أَيْضًا رَفْعُهُ ، فَلِشَيْءٍ وَاحِدٍ) ، وهو السلب (نقيضان)، أي الإيجاب وسلب السلب ، (وَمَنْ تَثَبَّثَ بِالْعَيْنِيَّةِ) بين مفهومي الإيجاب وسلب السلب لدفع استحالة تعدد النقيض (فَقَدْ أَخْطَاً ؛ فَإِنَّ تَغَايْرَ الْمَفْهُوم)، أي تغاير مفهومي سلب السلب والإيجاب (ضَرُورِيٌّ ، وَهُوَ حَسْبِي) في توجه الشك. (وَنِعْمَ الْحَلُّ) أفعال المدح. ، ومخصوصه قوله: (إِنَّ السَّلْبَ لَا يُضَافُ حَقِيقَةً إِلَّا إِلَى الْوُجُودِ فِي نَفْسِهِ أَوْ لِغَيْرِهِ) ، ولا يضاف إلى السلب أصلا ؛ فإن الرفع من حيث إنه رفع غير قابل للمرفوعية ما لم يلاحظ له نحو من الثبوت، فَسَلْبُ السَّلْبِ رَفْعُ وُجُودِ السَّلْبِ) في نفسه أو في غيره، (وَهُوَ إِمَّا فِي قُوَّة الْمُوجَبَةِ السَّالِبَةِ الْمَوْضُوع)، إن كان رفع وجوده في نفسه، (أَوِ الْمُوجَبَةِ السَّالِبَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ) ، إن كان رفع وجوده لغيره، (فَسَلْبُ السَّلْبِ السَّالِبَة السَّالِبَة نَقِيضُ الْمُوجَبَةِ السَّالِبَةِ ، لَا السَّالِبَةُ الْمُحَصَّلَةُ) التي نقيضها الإيجاب. فَتَفَكَّرْ وَتَشَكَّرْ.
The contradiction of two propositions is their difference such that by its essence the truth of each entails the falsehood of the other condition, and vice versa, i.e., by its essence the falsehood of each entails the truth of the other. And that is by affirmation and negation when that negation is its removal, i.e., the removal of that same affirmation; because if it is the removal of another affirmation, there is no opposition between that affirmation and this negation, and if it is like between the affirmation of the entailing thing and the removal of its coextensive necessary implication, then it is not by its essence. So there must be unity of the relational aspect, i.e., the relation that is negated in the negative must be the very one in the affirmative. And they restricted it to the well-known eight unities: unity of subject, predicate, condition, relation, part, whole, potentiality, actuality, time, and place. And some of them included some within others; for what is other than unity of subject and predicate is included in them. And here there is a doubt, which is: that affirmation is the contradictory of negation; because the difference between the affirmative and the negative so that the truth of each entails the falsehood of the other, and vice versa, by its essence. And whoever denies it and says that the contradictory of the negative is the negation of negation, and affirmation is a necessary concomitant of the contradictory, like al-Sadr al-Shirazi, contemporary of al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani, and the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin', has broken the consensus; for all the logicians agreed that affirmation is the contradictory of negation, until al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq said: 'The statement of the commentator of al-Matali' that the contradictory of everything is its removal is false, and the correct is that the removal of everything is its contradictory.' Rather, he broke necessity also; for when we consider the concept of negation and affirmation, we judge the impossibility of their conjunction in truth and falsehood by their essences, without observing the negation of negation. And the negation of negation is also its removal, so for one thing, which is negation, there are two contradictories: affirmation and negation of negation. And whoever insists on the identity between the concepts of affirmation and negation of negation to refute the impossibility of multiple contradictories has erred; for the difference of concept, i.e., the difference of the concepts of negation of negation and affirmation, is necessary, and that suffices in directing the doubt. And excellent is the solution: 'Indeed, negation is not truly applied except to existence in itself or to another,' and it is not applied to negation at all; for removal, insofar as it is removal, is not receptive of being removed unless a kind of stability is observed for it. So the negation of negation is the removal of the existence of negation in itself or in another, and it is either in the power of the affirmative of the negative subject, if it is the removal of its existence in itself, or the affirmative of the negative predicate, if it is the removal of its existence for another. So the negation of negation, i.e., the negative of the negative, is the contradictory of the affirmative of the negative, not the simple negative whose contradictory is affirmation. So reflect and give thanks.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of contradiction (tanāquḍ) between two propositions. The author begins by stating that contradiction is a specific type of opposition where, by its very essence, the truth of each proposition entails the falsehood of the other, and vice versa. This opposition is realized through affirmation (ījāb) and negation (salb), but with the crucial condition that the negation must be the exact removal of the same affirmation. If the negation removes a different affirmation, or if the opposition is merely between an entailing proposition and the negation of its coextensive necessary implication, the contradiction is not essential. Therefore, there must be unity in the relational aspect (ittiḥād al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya). The logicians have enumerated eight conditions of unity: unity of subject, predicate, condition, relation, part, whole, potentiality, actuality, time, and place. Some scholars include the last six within subject and predicate. The author then addresses a doubt: is affirmation the contradictory of negation? Some, like al-Sadr al-Shirazi, argued that the contradictory of a negative is the negation of negation, and affirmation is merely a necessary concomitant. The author refutes this, stating it breaks consensus and even necessity, because our direct conceptual understanding shows that affirmation and negation are mutually exclusive in truth and falsehood without needing to invoke negation of negation. However, the negation of negation is also a removal, so a single negation has two contradictories: affirmation and negation of negation. Those who insist on the identity of the concepts of affirmation and negation of negation are mistaken; the concepts are necessarily distinct. The solution is that negation is only applied to existence, not to another negation. The negation of negation is the removal of the existence of the negation, which effectively functions as an affirmative proposition with a negative subject or predicate. Thus, the negation of negation is a different contradictory than the simple negative, whose contradictory is affirmation. The author concludes by urging reflection and gratitude.

Key Terms
التناقض
نقيض
سلب السلب
الوحدات الثمانية

Key Takeaway
Genuine contradiction requires eight specific unities, and the negation of negation is a distinct contradictory from simple affirmation.

The Definition of Contradiction and the Problem of Multiple Contraries
Author defines contradiction and refutes a mistaken view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct definition of contradiction and explain why a single proposition cannot have two distinct contraries.

Core Idea
Contradiction is a binary relation; one thing has only one contradictory.

In this passage, the author clarifies the true nature of contradiction. He rejects the imprecise definition that contradiction is 'removal of everything' and instead emphasises that it is a relation between two propositions that cannot be true together nor false together. He also argues that one and the same proposition cannot have two distinct contraries, which directly leads to the puzzle of whether 'negation of negation' is identical to affirmation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the erroneous definition
Find the phrase that states the incorrect view: 'رفعه باطل، والصحيح رفع كل شيء نقيضه'. What is being rejected?
2
Extract the author's correct definition
Locate where the author says 'فإنا إذا لاحظنا مفهوم السلب والإيجاب نحكم باستحالة اجتماعهما صدقا وكذبا'. What are the two conditions of contradiction?
3
Understand the problem of multiple contraries
The author states that for one negation, there are two contraries: affirmation and negation of negation. Why is this problematic?
4
Grasp the proposed resolution
Note how the author insists that the concepts of affirmation and negation of negation are necessarily distinct, which suffices to avoid the paradox. Then later he provides a deeper solution.

Chapter on contradiction. Its removal is false; the correct is that the removal of everything is its contrary, rather it violates necessity as well; for when we observe the concept of negation and affirmation we judge the impossibility of their conjunction in truth and falsehood due to their essences, without observing the negation of negation. And the negation of negation is also its removal; so for one thing, which is negation, there are two contraries, namely affirmation and negation of negation. And whoever affirms identity between the concepts of affirmation and negation of negation to repulse the impossibility of multiplicity of contrary has erred; for the difference of concept, that is, the difference of the concepts of negation of negation and affirmation, is necessary, and it suffices for the direction of doubt. From: 'What an excellent solution' (verb of praise), and its specific subject is his saying: 'Negation is not truly attributed except to existence in itself or to other than it,' and it is not attributed to negation at all; for removal insofar as it is removal is not receptive of being removed unless some mode of positiveness is observed for it. So negation of negation is removal of the existence of negation in itself or in other than it, and it is either in the force of an affirmative with a negative subject, if it is removal of its existence in itself, or an affirmative with a negative predicate, if it is removal of its existence in other than it. So the negation of negation of a negative-negative is the contrary of an affirmative-negative, not the absolute negative whose contrary is affirmation. So reflect and be grateful, and pay no attention to what was said against it: that negation may not be attributed to existence but to the essence itself as the partisans of simple making hold, so your statement 'negation is not attributed except to existence' is refuted; because the restriction is relative with regard to negation, and its attribution to other than existence, which is essence, is not harmful in the restriction. And also pay no attention to the response that the multiplicity of contraries for one thing is impossible only if they are distinct; for that leads to conjunction of the two contraries or their removal; but if they are equivalent then there is no impossibility in it; so negation of negation and affirmation in our case are equivalent, so there is no problem; because multiplicity of contraries for one thing is absolutely impossible; because contradiction is a relation, and a relation only exists between two, as has passed. And know that the investigator al-Dawani argued for the impossibility of the attachment of negation to negation, by saying that negation is a non-independent meaning, so negation is not attributed to it; and he was criticized by those who looked at his speech, saying that negation attaches to affirmation despite being non-independent; and they did not know that the speech of al-Dawani is based on the doctrine of the later scholars that the negative relation is a simple relation like the affirmative, different in essence from it, and relation in general is not fit for the attachment of negation. Then the impossibility of the attachment of negation to negation without observing positiveness may be necessary, and the argument is superfluous, and complete knowledge is with Allah Most High.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of contradiction (تناقض) and addresses a key puzzle in logic: whether the negation of a negation (سلب السلب) is identical to affirmation (إيجاب) or distinct. The author begins by stating that the common claim that contradiction is 'removal of everything' is false; the correct view is that it is a relation of opposition between two propositions that cannot both be true or both false. He then explains that for one thing, namely a negation, there are two contraries: affirmation and the negation of that negation. Some thinkers mistakenly identify these two concepts (إيجاب and سلب السلب) to avoid the impossibility of having two contraries for one thing. The author rejects this, insisting that the concepts are necessarily distinct, and this difference suffices to allow conceptual doubt. Next, the author presents a solution: negation (سلب) is only truly attributed to existence (وجوب), not to another negation. Therefore, negating a negation is actually removing the existence of that negation, resulting in either an affirmative with a negative subject or an affirmative with a negative predicate. Thus, the negation of a negation of a negative proposition (السالبة السالبة) is contrary to the affirmative-negative (الموجبة السالبة), not to the absolute negative. The author then refutes two objections: first, that negation may apply to essence, not just existence (refuting the restriction); second, that if two contraries are equivalent (متساويان), multiplicity is not impossible. The author emphasizes that contradiction is a relation that requires exactly two terms. Finally, he discusses al-Dawani's argument that negation cannot attach to another negation because negation is non-independent; critics countered that negation attaches to affirmation, which is also non-independent. The author clarifies that al-Dawani's view is based on the later doctrine that negative relations are simple, like affirmative relations, and relations in general cannot have negation attached. He concludes that the impossibility of this attachment may be self-evident, making the argument unnecessary.

تناقض
نقيض
سلب
إيجاب

Key Takeaway
Contradiction is a binary relation of impossibility of truth and falsehood together; a single proposition has exactly one contradictory.

The Solution: Negation Only Applies to Existence, Not to Negation
Author presents and defends the solution to the negation of negation puzzle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why negation of negation is not conceptually identical to affirmation, and analyse the different types of propositions that result from negating a negation.

Core Idea
Negation of negation is not identical to affirmation; it is a removal of existence of the negation, yielding a special type of affirmative.

The author presents a solution to the puzzle of multiple contraries. He argues that the negation of a negation is not a direct removal of negation itself, but rather the removal of the existence of that negation. This leads to the conclusion that the negation of a negation is actually an affirmative proposition with a negative subject or predicate, not a simple double negative. He then refutes objections and discusses al-Dawani's argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key principle
The author states: 'Negation is not truly attributed except to existence in itself or to other than it.' What does this mean?
2
Apply the principle to negation of negation
So 'salb al-salab' means removing the existence of the negation. This results in either an affirmative with a negative subject or an affirmative with a negative predicate.
3
Distinguish the types of propositions
Identify what is meant by 'al-mūjaba al-sāliba al-mawḍūʿ' and 'al-mūjaba al-sāliba al-maḥmūl' and 'al-sāliba al-sāliba'.
4
Refute objections
The author counters two objections: one about negation applying to essence, and one about equivalence of contraries. Grasp why these do not undermine the solution.
5
Consider al-Dawani's argument
Al-Dawani claimed that negation cannot attach to another negation because negation is non-independent. The author clarifies this based on the nature of negative relations.

رفعه باطل، والصحيح رفع كل شيء نقيضه، بل خرق الضرورة أيضا ؛ فإنا إذا لاحظنا مفهوم السلب والإيجاب نحكم باستحالة اجتماعهما صدقا وكذبا لذاتيهما، من غير ملاحظة سلب السلب.
وسلب السلب أيضًا رفعه، فلشيء واحد)، وهو السلب (نقيضان)، أي الإيجاب وسلب السلب، (ومن تثبت بالعينية) بين مفهومي الإيجاب وسلب السلب لدفع استحالة تعدد النقيض (فقد أخطأ؛ فإن تغاير المفهوم)، أي تغاير مفهومي سلب السلب والإيجاب (ضروري، وهو حسبي) في توجه الشك.
من
(ونعم الحل) أفعال المدح.، ومخصوصه قوله: (إن السلب لا يضاف حقيقة إلا إلى الوجود في نفسه أو لغيره)، ولا يضاف إلى السلب أصلا؛ فإن الرفع من حيث إنه رفع غير قابل للمرفوعية ما لم يلاحظ له نحو من الثبوت، فسلب السلب رفع وجود السلب) في نفسه أو في غيره، (وهو إما في قوة الموجبة السالبة الموضوع)، إن كان رفع وجوده في نفسه، (أو الموجبة السالبة المحمول)، إن كان رفع وجوده لغيره، (فسلب السلب السالبة السالبة نقيض الموجبة السالبة، لا السالبة المحصلة) التي نقيضها الإيجاب.
فتفكر وتشكر)، ولا تلتفت إلى ما قيل عليه: إن السلب قد لا يضاف إلى الوجود، بل إلى نفس الماهية على ما يراه شيعة الجعل البسيط، فقولكم: السلب لا يضاف إلا إلى الوجود منقوض؛ لأن الحصر إضافي بالنظر إلى السلب، وإضافته إلى غير الوجود الذي هو الماهية غير ضار في الحصر.
ولا تلتفت أيضا إلى ما يجاب من أن تعدد النقائض لشيء واحد إنما يستحيل إذا كانا متباينين؛ فإن ذلك مفض إلى اجتماع النقيضين أو ارتفاعهما، وأما إذا كانا متساويين فلا استحالة فيه، فسلب السلب والإيجاب فيما نحن فيه متساويان فلا إشكال؛ لأن تعدد النقائض لشيء واحد مستحيل مطلقا؛ لأن التناقض نسبة، والنسبة لا تكون إلا بين اثنين، كما مر.
واعلم أن المحقق الدواني استدل على استحالة تعلق السلب بالسلب، بأن السلب معنى غير مستقل، فلا يضاف إليه السلب، وشنع عليه من نظر في كلامه، بأن السلب يتعلق بالإيجاب مع كونه غير مستقل، ولم يعلموا أن كلام المحقق الدواني مبني على مذهب المتأخرين هو أن النسبة السلبية نسبة بسيطة كالإيجابية مغايرة بالذات لها، والنسبة مطلقا غير صالحة لتعلق السلب. ثم إن استحالة تعلق السلب بالسلب من غير ملاحظة الثبوت عسى أن يكون ضروريا (1) والاستدلال تبرع، والعلم التام عند الله سبحانه.
Chapter on contradiction. Its removal is false; the correct is that the removal of everything is its contrary, rather it violates necessity as well; for when we observe the concept of negation and affirmation we judge the impossibility of their conjunction in truth and falsehood due to their essences, without observing the negation of negation. And the negation of negation is also its removal; so for one thing, which is negation, there are two contraries, namely affirmation and negation of negation. And whoever affirms identity between the concepts of affirmation and negation of negation to repulse the impossibility of multiplicity of contrary has erred; for the difference of concept, that is, the difference of the concepts of negation of negation and affirmation, is necessary, and it suffices for the direction of doubt. From: 'What an excellent solution' (verb of praise), and its specific subject is his saying: 'Negation is not truly attributed except to existence in itself or to other than it,' and it is not attributed to negation at all; for removal insofar as it is removal is not receptive of being removed unless some mode of positiveness is observed for it. So negation of negation is removal of the existence of negation in itself or in other than it, and it is either in the force of an affirmative with a negative subject, if it is removal of its existence in itself, or an affirmative with a negative predicate, if it is removal of its existence in other than it. So the negation of negation of a negative-negative is the contrary of an affirmative-negative, not the absolute negative whose contrary is affirmation. So reflect and be grateful, and pay no attention to what was said against it: that negation may not be attributed to existence but to the essence itself as the partisans of simple making hold, so your statement 'negation is not attributed except to existence' is refuted; because the restriction is relative with regard to negation, and its attribution to other than existence, which is essence, is not harmful in the restriction. And also pay no attention to the response that the multiplicity of contraries for one thing is impossible only if they are distinct; for that leads to conjunction of the two contraries or their removal; but if they are equivalent then there is no impossibility in it; so negation of negation and affirmation in our case are equivalent, so there is no problem; because multiplicity of contraries for one thing is absolutely impossible; because contradiction is a relation, and a relation only exists between two, as has passed. And know that the investigator al-Dawani argued for the impossibility of the attachment of negation to negation, by saying that negation is a non-independent meaning, so negation is not attributed to it; and he was criticized by those who looked at his speech, saying that negation attaches to affirmation despite being non-independent; and they did not know that the speech of al-Dawani is based on the doctrine of the later scholars that the negative relation is a simple relation like the affirmative, different in essence from it, and relation in general is not fit for the attachment of negation. Then the impossibility of the attachment of negation to negation without observing positiveness may be necessary, and the argument is superfluous, and complete knowledge is with Allah Most High.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of contradiction (تناقض) and addresses a key puzzle in logic: whether the negation of a negation (سلب السلب) is identical to affirmation (إيجاب) or distinct. The author begins by stating that the common claim that contradiction is 'removal of everything' is false; the correct view is that it is a relation of opposition between two propositions that cannot both be true or both false. He then explains that for one thing, namely a negation, there are two contraries: affirmation and the negation of that negation. Some thinkers mistakenly identify these two concepts (إيجاب and سلب السلب) to avoid the impossibility of having two contraries for one thing. The author rejects this, insisting that the concepts are necessarily distinct, and this difference suffices to allow conceptual doubt. Next, the author presents a solution: negation (سلب) is only truly attributed to existence (وجوب), not to another negation. Therefore, negating a negation is actually removing the existence of that negation, resulting in either an affirmative with a negative subject or an affirmative with a negative predicate. Thus, the negation of a negation of a negative proposition (السالبة السالبة) is contrary to the affirmative-negative (الموجبة السالبة), not to the absolute negative. The author then refutes two objections: first, that negation may apply to essence, not just existence (refuting the restriction); second, that if two contraries are equivalent (متساويان), multiplicity is not impossible. The author emphasizes that contradiction is a relation that requires exactly two terms. Finally, he discusses al-Dawani's argument that negation cannot attach to another negation because negation is non-independent; critics countered that negation attaches to affirmation, which is also non-independent. The author clarifies that al-Dawani's view is based on the later doctrine that negative relations are simple, like affirmative relations, and relations in general cannot have negation attached. He concludes that the impossibility of this attachment may be self-evident, making the argument unnecessary.

Key Terms
سلب السلب
الموجبة السالبة الموضوع
الموجبة السالبة المحمول
السالبَة السالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is 'salb al-salab' (negation of negation) actually negating?
A) The negation itself.
B) The existence of the negation.
C) The subject of the negation.
D) The predicate of the negation.

Key Takeaway
Negation of negation is not a direct double negative; it is the removal of the existence of a negation, resulting in a special affirmative proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What is a Hujjah? The Definition of Proof in Islamic Logic
Author defines hujjah and sets up classification framework.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relationship of inclusion between the proof and the conclusion in any given argument.

Core Idea
A hujjah is defined functionally: it leads to the desired assent.

In this passage, the author defines the key term 'hujjah' (proof) and explains that its purpose is to lead to a desired predicative assent (tasdiq). He then outlines the possible logical relationships between the proof and the conclusion, which form the basis for classifying arguments into three types. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping how Islamic scholars evaluate the validity of arguments in disciplines like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of hujjah
Find the Arabic phrase 'الحجة الموصل إلى التصديق المطلوب'. This is the core definition: a proof is whatever leads to the desired assent.
Note that 'hujjah' and 'dalil' are used synonymously here.
2
Identify the three inclusion possibilities
The passage says the relationship is confined to three: either the proof encompasses the conclusion, or the conclusion encompasses the proof, or a third thing encompasses them both. Also note the special case of mere implication (istithna'i).
These three correspond to qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil respectively.
3
Recognize the function of hujjah
The hujjah must lead to 'التصديق المطلوب' (the desired assent). This means the conclusion is already known to be sought, and the proof provides grounds for it.
4
Connect to Islamic science
In kalam, the proof for the temporality of the world (huduth al-alam) is a hujjah that leads to the assent that Allah exists. The premises include 'the world is temporally originated' and 'every temporally originated thing has a cause'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحجة. الحجة الموصل إلى التصديق المطلوب (حجة ودليل. واسمة الموصل إلى المطلوب التصديقي ينحصر في ثلاث: سواء كان الحجة مشتملة عليه، أو هو مشتملا عليها، أو أمر ثالث يشتمل عليهما، أو استلزام فقط، كما في الاستثنائي، وهذا ضروري. وينحصر في ثلاثة: الاستقراء، إذا كانت الحجة بحيث يشتمل عليها المطلوب، والتمثيل، إن كان المطلوب والحجة بحيث يشتمل عليهما ثالث، والقياس، إن كان بحيث يشتمل على المطلوب ومستلزم له.
A chapter on proof. The proof that leads to the desired assent is called 'hujja' and 'dalil'. The name of that which leads to the desired predicative assent is confined to three [terms]: whether the proof encompasses it, or it encompasses the proof, or a third thing encompasses them both, or mere implication, as in the conditional [syllogism]. This is necessary. And it is confined to three: induction (istiqra'), when the proof is such that the desired conclusion encompasses it; analogy (tamthil), if the desired conclusion and the proof are such that a third encompasses them both; and syllogism (qiyas), if it is such that it encompasses the desired conclusion and necessitates it.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'hujjah' (proof) and classifies its types. The author begins by stating that a proof is whatever leads to a desired assent (tasdiq). He then notes that the term for that which reaches the desired conclusion is confined to three possibilities regarding the relationship of inclusion: either the proof includes the conclusion, or the conclusion includes the proof, or a third thing includes both. Also, there is the case of mere implication, as in conditional syllogisms. Then he gives the three standard types: (1) Induction (istiqra'): when the desired conclusion is more general and includes the proof (e.g., examining individuals to reach a universal statement). (2) Analogy (tamthil): when both proof and conclusion are subsumed under a third, more general principle (e.g., using the ruling of an original case to derive the ruling for a new case). (3) Syllogism (qiyas): when the proof includes the conclusion and necessitates it (e.g., categorical syllogism where premises entail the conclusion). This classification is essential for understanding the foundational method of reasoning in Islamic logic. The examples from Islamic jurisprudence would be: Induction: observing that wine, grape juice, etc. are intoxicants leads to the general rule that all intoxicants are forbidden. Analogy: comparing nabidh (date wine) to khamr (grape wine) based on the common effective cause of intoxication. Syllogism: Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.

Key Terms
حجة
دليل
استثنائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of a hujjah?
A) It must be a categorical syllogism.
B) It leads to the desired assent.
C) It must be true in itself.
D) It must be based on revelation.

Key Takeaway
A hujjah is any argument that yields the desired assent, and its logical structure is classified by the inclusion relationship between premises and conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following argument and identify the inclusion relationship between proof and conclusion: 'Every commanded act is good. Prayer is commanded. Therefore prayer is good.' Answer: The proof (premises) encompasses the conclusion, so it is a qiyas-type hujjah.
?
Find an example of a hujjah from everyday life (not Islamic) and classify its inclusion relationship, then explain why it does or does not fit the definition given here.

The Threefold Classification of Proof: Induction, Analogy, and Syllogism
Author classifies hujjah into three types with definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given argument as induction (istiqra'), analogy (tamthil), or syllogism (qiyas) based on the inclusion relationship described in the passage.

Core Idea
Proof is classified by which entity encompasses which: conclusion encompasses proof, third encompasses both, or proof encompasses conclusion.

Having defined hujjah broadly, the author now specifies three types based on the inclusion pattern: induction (where the conclusion encompasses the proof), analogy (where a third principle encompasses both proof and conclusion), and syllogism (where the proof encompasses the conclusion and necessitates it). These correspond to reasoning methods commonly used in Islamic jurisprudence and theology. Understanding this classification helps us analyze and construct valid arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three inclusion relationships
From the previous lesson, remember the three possibilities: (1) proof encompasses conclusion, (2) conclusion encompasses proof, (3) third encompasses both.
These are the basis for qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil respectively.
2
Match each type to its inclusion pattern
Induction (istiqra'): the desired conclusion encompasses the proof. Analogy (tamthil): a third, more general principle encompasses both proof and conclusion. Syllogism (qiyas): the proof encompasses the conclusion and necessitates it.
The passage explicitly links each term to its pattern.
3
Apply to Islamic examples
Example of induction: examining specific intoxicants (wine, grape juice) yields a general rule that all intoxicants are forbidden. Example of analogy: comparing nabidh to khamr based on the common 'illa of intoxication. Example of syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.'
These examples are from usul al-fiqh.
4
Distinguish between types
Test your understanding: given an argument, identify which type based on inclusion. For instance, if the conclusion is a universal statement derived from particular instances, it is induction. If a ruling is extended from a original case to a new case due to shared cause, it is analogy. If the conclusion is directly deduced from two premises, it is syllogism.
