The Three Essential Conditions of Qiyas
Commentary defines qiyas with its three conditions: composition, multiplicity, and necessary implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential conditions of qiyas and evaluate whether a given argument qualifies as a qiyas based on those conditions.

Core Idea
Qiyas is not just any argument—it must be composed, have multiple premises, and entail its conclusion necessarily by itself.

In this lesson, we examine the classical definition of qiyas from the Islamic logical tradition. The commentator, Bahr al-Ulum, unpacks each part of the definition to show what is included and, more importantly, what is excluded. Understanding these conditions is crucial for distinguishing a valid syllogistic argument from other forms of reasoning like induction and analogy, which are common in Islamic legal and theological discourse but do not yield certainty in the same way.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find what is being defined. In the passage, it is al-qiyas (القياس), presented as 'the pillar' (al-ʿumda) of logic.
2
Extract the three conditions
The definition states: 'a statement composed of premises that entail by themselves another statement.' Break this down: (1) composition (taʾlīf) — it must be a statement, not a single word; (2) multiplicity (al-jamʿ) — the plural 'premises' means at least two; (3) necessary implication (al-luzūm) — the conclusion follows by itself, not due to an external factor.
The plural 'qadaya' is intentional: it excludes a single premise, even if that premise implies another.
3
Understand the exclusions
Each condition excludes specific types of arguments. 'Statement' excludes simple expressions. 'Composed' excludes single words. 'Plural 'premises' excludes single-premise inferences like conversion (al-ʿaks). 'By itself' excludes reasoning that relies on an external assumption, such as incomplete induction. 'Another statement' excludes the original premises themselves.
The commentary uses induction (istiqraʾ) and analogy (tamthīl) as test cases to clarify the condition of 'by itself'.
4
Apply the definition to induction and analogy
The commentator examines whether induction and analogy satisfy the luzūm condition. Complete induction is considered a type of qiyas (called a divisive syllogism), but incomplete induction does not entail certainty. Analogy, as used in fiqh, does involve entailment because it includes a universal premise about the ʿilla, but its certainty may be weaker.
This shows that the boundaries of qiyas are debated: some forms of analogy are included if they meet the luzūm condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف القياس شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم ( وَالْعُمْدَةُ الْقِيَاسُ (۱) ، وَهُوَ قَوْلٌ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قضايا). قال المصنف: ومحترزاته الأظهر ذكر المؤلف بعد القول ؛ لئلا يذهب إلى أن ((مِنْ) تبعيضية). واحترز بإيراد الجمع عن القضية الواحدة المستلزمة لأخرى، كالعكس، سواء كانت بسيطة أو مركبة ؛ إذ لا يقال للمركبة عرفا ،قضايا والمراد بالجمع ما فوق الواحد . ( يَلْزَمُ عَنْهَا لِذَاتِهَا (٤). فصل في الحجة قَوْلُ آخَرُ (۱)) ، احترز باللزوم عن الاستقراء والتمثيل؛ إذ لا يلزم منهما شيء. فإن قلت: حاصل الاستقراء أن الحكم ثابت ؛ لأنه منحصر في هذا الجزئي وذلك إلى غير ذلك، وتلك الجزئيات كلها يثبت لها المحمول، فالموضوع ثبت له المحمول ؟ قلت: الاستقراء قسمان ،تام ويسمى قياسا مَقْسِما، وهو داخل في المحدود والحد وناقص ولا ندعي فيه الحصر، كما يجيئ إن شاء الله تعالى ، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم منه شيء. بقي أمر التمثيل، الظاهر أنه لا يخرج عن قيد اللزوم؛ لأن حاصله أن هذا الحكم في هذا الجزئي ثابت، كالحرمة في البنج ؛ لأنه مشارك للأصل كالخمر في علة الحكم كالإسكار، وكلما هو شارك للأصل في علة الحكم ، فالحكم ثابت فيه، فهذا الجزئي الحكم ثابت فيه، وهذه المقدمات مستلزمة للنتيجة قطعا.
Definition of Qiyas: Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum: 'And the pillar is qiyas, and it is a statement composed of some premises.' The author said: its most apparent exclusions require the author to mention 'statement' after 'composed', lest one think that 'min' is partitive. And he excluded, by using the plural, the single premise that entails another, like conversion, whether it is simple or compound, because a compound [sentence] is not ordinarily called 'premises'; the plural intends more than one. 'Which entails by itself another statement.' He excluded by [the condition of] entailment both induction and analogy, for nothing is entailed by them. If you object: the outcome of induction is that the judgment is established because it is limited to this particular and that particular, etc., and all those particulars have the predicate, so the subject has the predicate? I answer: Induction is of two types: complete, which is called a divisive syllogism, and it falls within the defined and the definition; and incomplete, and we do not claim completeness for it, as will come, God willing; and if so, nothing follows from it. As for analogy, it apparently does not exit the condition of entailment, because its outcome is that this judgment in this particular is established, like prohibition in cannabis, because it shares with the original (like wine) in the cause of the ruling (like intoxication); and everything that shares with the original in the cause of the ruling, the ruling is established in it, so this particular the ruling is established in it, and these premises necessarily entail the conclusion.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas (syllogism) as the foundation of logic and elaborates its essential conditions. The commentator begins by quoting the matn: 'And the pillar is qiyas, and it is a statement composed of premises which entails by itself another statement.' He then explains the exclusions due to each part of the definition. 'Statement' excludes simple expressions; 'composed' excludes single words; 'of premises' (using the plural) excludes a single premise, even if it implies another (like conversion), because 'premises' in ordinary usage means more than one. 'By itself' (lidhatiha) excludes entailment from external factors. 'Another statement' (qawl akhar) excludes the original premises themselves. The condition of entailment (luzum) is then used to exclude induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil). The commentator addresses potential objections: For induction, it is divided into complete (which is actually a type of syllogism) and incomplete (which does not yield certainty). For analogy, he argues that it does involve necessary implication because the minor premise (this particular shares the cause) and the universal premise (everything sharing the cause has the ruling) together entail the conclusion. However, the commentator acknowledges that the certainty of the minor premise may be probabilistic. The discussion uses an Islamic legal example: analogy between cannabis (bhanj) and wine (khamr) regarding prohibition based on the cause ('illa) of intoxication.

Key Terms
القياس
اللزوم
الاستقراء
التمثيل
القضية
المؤلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which of the following is excluded by the phrase 'bi nafsihi' (by itself) in the definition of qiyas?
A) Induction and analogy
B) Enthymemes that rely on an external assumption
C) Single-premise arguments like conversion
D) Syllogisms with only two premises

Key Takeaway
Qiyas must be composed of multiple premises that necessarily entail the conclusion by themselves, without reliance on external assumptions; incomplete induction and unformalized analogy do not meet this standard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Draw a table listing the three conditions of qiyas (composition, multiplicity, and necessary implication). For each condition, provide a counterexample from Islamic reasoning that fails it. For example, a single-premise argument like 'All prophets are truthful' does not produce a new conclusion without an additional premise, thus violating multiplicity. Ensure your examples are from fiqh, kalam, or usul al-fiqh.
?
2. Evaluate the following argument from usul al-fiqh: 'Al-Shafiʿi used qiyas, and the majority of scholars follow him; therefore qiyas is a valid source of Islamic law.' Does this argument meet the three conditions of qiyas? Explain why or why not, referencing the condition of necessary implication.

What Is Mantiq?

The Two Meanings of Necessity (Luzum) in Syllogistic Entailment
Author expounds the Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, Philosophers, and al-Razi's views on the entailment of the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessity in reality itself (fi nafs al-amr) and necessity with respect to knowledge (bi hasab al-'ilm), and explain the concept of succession (isti'qab) and the role of noticing the inclusion.

Core Idea
The necessity of a syllogism's conclusion can be understood as either a real concomitant or an epistemic succession.

The author begins by clarifying a subtle point: the conclusion follows not from the bare premises but from their combination with the contradictory converse of the major. He then distinguishes two senses in which the conclusion is 'necessary' for the syllogism: one ontological, one epistemic. Understanding this distinction is key to engaging with the theological debates that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of necessity
Locate where the author distinguishes 'necessity in reality itself' (fi nafs al-amr) and 'necessity with respect to knowledge' (bi hasab al-'ilm). Note which is described as the more famous.
The distinction is crucial for understanding later debates.
2
Explain necessity in reality itself
According to the passage, when taken fi nafs al-amr, the conclusion is a qualitative necessary of the syllogism: if the premises are true, the conclusion is true; if false, false. What does that imply about the relationship?
3
Explain necessity with respect to knowledge
Here necessity means 'succession' (isti'qab): knowledge of the conclusion occurs after knowledge of the syllogism, after noticing the inclusion. What is 'inclusion' according to Ibn Sina?
4
Relate to the role of the contradictory converse
The author earlier stated that the conclusion does not follow from the original premises alone but from combining the minor with the contradictory converse of the major. How does this relate to the epistemic necessity?
This shows that the logical form of the syllogism is not immediately apparent without conversion.

الأول ليست النتيجة لأصل المقدمات، بل لما يحصل من انضمام الصغرى مع عکس نقيض الكبرى، بخلاف ما يلزم منه بواسطة العكس المستوي ؛ فإن النتيجة بالحقيقة لأصل القياس والعكس المستوي واسطة في الإثبات فقط . ونحن سنبين إن شاء الله تعالى إنتاج الأشكال بوجه لمي بحيث لا يمتري واحد من العامة ، فضلا عن الخاصة، في أن النتيجة لازمة للقياس لزوما ذاتيا ، فاستقم عليه ، والعلم التام عند علام الغيوب. (ثُمَّ إِنْ أُخِذَ اللُّزُومُ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ فَبِهَا) ونِعْمَ؛ لأن النتيجة لازمة كيفية للقياس في نفس الأمر ؛ إذ بصدقه تصدق وبكذبه تكذب، (وَإِنِ اعْتُبِرَ) اللزوم حصول العلم الية من ( بِحَسَبِ الْعِلْمِ، وَهُوَ الْأَشْهَرُ ، فَالْمُرَادُ الاسْتِعْقابُ)، أي حصول النتيجة عقيب حصول القياس، (بَعْدَ تَفَطَّنِ الإِنْدِرَاج، أي اندراج النتيجة في القياس، بالنتيجة المقدمتين بالاشتمال عليه أو على نقيضه ؛ كَمَا قَالَ) الشيخ الرئيس (ابْنُ سِينَا . وَذَلِكَ الِاسْتِعْقَابُ عَلَى سَبِيلِ الْعَادَةِ)، أي جري عادة الله تعالى بإيجاد العلم بالنتيجة عقيب حصول العلم بالقياس الصحيح، من غير وجوب منه تعالى. هذا مذهب الشيخ المفضل الإمام من أئمة أهل الحق أبي الحسن الأشعري - قدس سره - وهذا بناء على أن لا خالق إلا الله تعالى ابتداء باختياره وقدرته جلت أسماؤه ، لكن لا يلزم منه أن يكون من غير وجوب، بل الوجود من غير وجوب باطل، كما بين في موضعه . أو التَّوْلِيدِ)، وهو وجود فعل بتبعية فعل آخر صادر عن مختار باختيار من غير اختيار منه، كحركة المفتاح بحركة اليد الصادر عن المختار، إليه ذهب المعتزلة خذلهم الله تعالى قالوا النظر فعل العبد وصادر من اختياره، والعبد خالقه، والعلم بالنتيجة يوجد عقيبه بقدرته من غير اختيار منه. وهذا بناء على أصلهم الكاسد أن أفعال العباد مخلوقة بقدرتهم، والعباد خالقون إياها، وهو مذهب مردود بالدلائل العقلية والنقلية، ولذا صاروا مجوس هذه الأمة كما ورد في الخبر، وشاركهم في هذا الأصل الروافض خذلهم الله تعالى. أو الْإِعْدَادِ) ، هذا مذهب الفلاسفة، قالوا: النظر يعد الذهن إعدادا تاما لقبول الفيض منه تعالى، فإذا وجد النظر واستعد الذهن للعلم بالنتيجة أفاض هو وجود العلم. وهؤلاء آمنوا بأن لا خالق إلا الله سبحانه، لكن قالوا إن الفيض إنما يصل على حسب استعداد المفاض عليه، وقالوا أيضا النتيجة تصير واجبة منه تعالى ؛ تفضلا على عباده ؛ إذ هو حكيم لا يرجح المرجوح ، وإذا ترجح وجب ، عَلَى اخْتِلَافِ الْمَذاهب. كما فصلنا. ثم ههنا مذهب ارتضى به في المسلَّم أن ، هو العلم بالنتيجة لازم للنظر الصحيح، واجب بعده بإيجابه تعالى، وليس لقدرة العبد تأثير، بل الله تعالى أوجد النظر والعلم بعده ، وجعله لازما للنظر. وإليه ذهب الإمام الهمام الرازي ، وهو غير مذهب الأشعري ؛ فإنه قال بعدم الوجوب، وقد وقع في بعض الشروح خبط في تصوير هذا المذهب، والله سبحانه أعلم بمراد عباده .
The first [view] is that the conclusion is not from the original premises, but from what results from combining the minor premise with the contradictory converse of the major premise, unlike what follows via simple conversion; for the conclusion in reality belongs to the original syllogism, and simple conversion is only a means in proof. And we will explain, God willing, the productivity of the figures in a demonstrative manner such that no one from the common people, let alone the elite, will doubt that the conclusion necessarily follows from the syllogism with an intrinsic necessity. So adhere to that, and complete knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. Then if the necessity is taken in reality itself, then it is well; because the conclusion is a qualitative necessary of the syllogism in reality; for when it is true, it is true, and when it is false, it is false. And if the necessity is considered with respect to knowledge—and this is the more famous—then what is meant is 'succession', i.e., that the conclusion occurs after the syllogism, after noticing the inclusion, i.e., the inclusion of the conclusion in the syllogism, either by containing it or its contradictory, as the chief leader Ibn Sina said. And that succession is in the manner of habit, i.e., God's custom of creating knowledge of the conclusion following knowledge of the correct syllogism, without any obligation on His part. This is the school of the excellent imam from the imams of the people of truth, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari — may God sanctify his soul — and this is based on the principle that there is no creator except God, initially by His choice and power, exalted be His names, but it does not follow that it is without obligation; rather, existence without obligation is false, as established in its place. Or generation: which is the existence of an act following another act issuing from a free agent by choice without [the second's] choice, like the movement of a key following the movement of the hand issuing from the free agent. This was held by the Mu'tazila — may God disgrace them — who said that reflection is the act of the servant and issues from his choice, and the servant is its creator, and knowledge of the conclusion occurs after it by his power without his choice. And this is based on their corrupt principle that the actions of servants are created by their power, and servants are their creators, and this school is refuted by rational and transmitted proofs, so they became the Magians of this nation as reported in the hadith, and the Rafidah shared this principle — may God disgrace them. Or preparation: this is the school of the philosophers. They said: reflection prepares the mind completely to receive the emanation from Him, so when reflection occurs and the mind is prepared for knowledge of the conclusion, He emanates the existence of knowledge. And these believed that there is no creator except God, but they said that the emanation only reaches according to the preparedness of that upon which it emanates, and they also said that the conclusion becomes necessary from Him as a favor to His servants, for He is wise and does not prefer the inferior, and if something is preferred, it becomes necessary, according to varying schools. As we have detailed. Then here is a school that is approved in al-Musallam: that knowledge of the conclusion is necessary for correct reflection, and necessary after it by His making it necessary, and the servant's power has no effect; rather, God created reflection and knowledge after it and made it necessary for reflection. This was held by the eminent imam al-Razi, and it is different from the Ash'ari school, for he denied obligation. And some commentaries made a mistake in depicting this school. And God knows best the intent of His servants.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition of different theological views on how the conclusion of a valid syllogism is known. The author begins by clarifying a subtle point: the conclusion does not follow simply from the original premises by themselves, but from the composition of the minor premise with the contradictory converse of the major. Simple conversion is merely a means in proof. The author then promises a demonstration of the figures to show the intrinsic necessity of the conclusion. He then distinguishes two understandings of 'necessity' (luzum): (1) in reality itself (fi nafs al-amr), where the conclusion is a necessary concomitant of the syllogism—if the premises are true, the conclusion is true; if false, false. (2) With respect to knowledge (bi hasab al-'ilm), which is more famous—here the necessity is understood as 'succession' (isti'qab), i.e., that knowledge of the conclusion occurs after knowledge of the syllogism, after noticing the inclusion of the conclusion (or its contradictory) in the premises. This is attributed to Ibn Sina. Then the author presents three theological schools on how this succession occurs. First, the Ash'ari view: it is 'ada (habit/custom)—God's habit to create knowledge of the conclusion after knowledge of the correct syllogism, with no obligation on God. Second, the Mu'tazila view: tawlid (generation)—the act of reflection generates the knowledge directly, with the servant as creator of his own acts. Third, the Philosophers' view: i'dad (preparation)—reflection prepares the mind, and then God emanates the knowledge. The author then presents a fourth view, approved by al-Razi: the conclusion is necessary for correct reflection, and this necessity is created by God, with no role for the servant's power. This differs from al-Ash'ari's denial of obligation. The passage thus categorizes the main kalam and falsafah positions and critiques the Mu'tazila as refuted.

Key Terms
اللزوم
الاستعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the more famous sense of necessity (luzum)?
A) Necessity in reality itself (fi nafs al-amr)
B) Necessity with respect to knowledge (bi hasab al-'ilm)
C) Both are equally famous
D) The passage does not specify

Key Takeaway
Understanding the two senses of necessity—ontological and epistemic—is essential for grasping the subsequent theological accounts of how the conclusion follows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a syllogism: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a creator; therefore the world has a creator.' Explain whether the conclusion follows by necessity in reality or by epistemic succession, according to the author's framework.

What Is Mantiq?

Theological Explanations of How the Conclusion Follows
Author expounds the Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, Philosophers, and al-Razi's views on the entailment of the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare and contrast the Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, Philosophers, and al-Razi's views on how knowledge of the conclusion occurs, identifying the role of God, human power, and causality.

Core Idea
Four schools offer rival accounts of how reflection leads to knowledge: habit, generation, preparation, and divine creation with necessity.

After establishing two senses of necessity, the author presents three major theological positions and one preferred synthesis on how the conclusion is known: the Ash'ari appeal to God's custom, the Mu'tazili theory of generation, the Philosophers' doctrine of preparation, and al-Razi's view of divine creation with necessity. Each reflects deeper commitments about causality and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four views
List the names of the four schools mentioned: Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, Philosophers, and al-Razi (the author's preference). Under each, state the key term that describes the mechanism: 'ada, tawlid, i'dad, and the fourth view.
2
Analyze the Ash'ari view ('ada)
According to the passage, the Ash'ari view is that knowledge of the conclusion follows by God's habit. What does that entail about God's obligation and human causality?
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila view (tawlid)
The Mu'tazila claim that reflection generates the knowledge. What is the basis of their claim, and how does the author critique it?
4
Analyze the Philosophers' view (i'dad)
For the Philosophers, reflection prepares the mind for emanation. How does this differ from the Ash'ari view? What does 'preparation' mean?
5
Examine al-Razi's view
Al-Razi's view is that knowledge is necessary for correct reflection, due to God's creation and necessity. How is this different from the Ash'ari denial of obligation?

Simple conversion is merely a means in proof. The author then promises a demonstration of the figures to show the intrinsic necessity of the conclusion. He then distinguishes two understandings of 'necessity' (luzum): (1) in reality itself (fi nafs al-amr), where the conclusion is a necessary concomitant of the syllogism—if the premises are true, the conclusion is true; if false, false. (2) With respect to knowledge (bi hasab al-'ilm), which is more famous—here the necessity is understood as 'succession' (isti'qab), i.e., that knowledge of the conclusion occurs after knowledge of the syllogism, after noticing the inclusion of the conclusion (or its contradictory) in the premises. This is attributed to Ibn Sina. Then the author presents three theological schools on how this succession occurs. First, the Ash'ari view: it is 'ada (habit/custom)—God's habit to create knowledge of the conclusion after knowledge of the correct syllogism, with no obligation on God. Second, the Mu'tazila view: tawlid (generation)—the act of reflection generates the knowledge directly, with the servant as creator of his own acts. Third, the Philosophers' view: i'dad (preparation)—reflection prepares the mind, and then God emanates the knowledge. The author then presents a fourth view, approved by al-Razi: the conclusion is necessary for correct reflection, and this necessity is created by God, with no role for the servant's power. This differs from al-Ash'ari's denial of obligation. The passage thus categorizes the main kalam and falsafah positions and critiques the Mu'tazila as refuted.

Key Terms
العادة
التوليد
الإعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which school holds that reflection prepares the mind for an emanation from God?
A) Ash'ari
B) Mu'tazila
C) Philosophers
D) Al-Razi

Key Takeaway
The theological account of syllogistic entailment is not merely a logical point but a window into broader commitments about causality, divine action, and human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine a student claims that 'reflection directly produces knowledge of the conclusion by the student's own power.' Which school does this correspond to? How would an Ash'ari reply? Write your response.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

The Definition of Qiyas: Form vs Matter
Author defines qiyas by its form and matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the form and matter of a syllogism and explain why a contradictory proposition cannot serve as a premise.

Core Idea
A syllogism is defined by its form, not its matter — the structure ensures necessary conclusion.

The author defines qiyas by emphasizing its form (hay'a). He explains that the contradictory cannot be a premise because it would lead to begging the question (musadara) or falsehood. This distinction is crucial for evaluating the validity of arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find what is being defined. The passage defines قياس (syllogism). Note that the definition focuses on the form.
2
Extract the defining feature
Look for the phrase بهيئته (by its form). The author stresses that the syllogism is defined by its formal structure, not its content.
3
Understand the exclusion
The author explains why a contradictory proposition cannot be a premise: it would either become a petition principii (مصادرة) or include something false in the reasoner's claim. This preserves the logical soundness of the syllogism.
4
Distinguish form vs matter
Form (هيئة) is the arrangement or structure of the premises and conclusion; matter is the specific propositions used. Validity depends on form, not matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان حدود القياس انقسامه إلى شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم بهَيْئَتِهِ ، وإنما قال بهيئته لأن عين النقيضة أو نقيضها لا يمكن أن يكون مقدمة ، وإلا لصارت مصادرة، أو اشتمل القياس على كاذب في زعم المستدل، بل إنما يكون جزء مقدمة ، والقضية لا تكون جزء قضية ما دامت قضية . (وَإِلّا) يكن مذكورا بهيئته، بل بمادته فقط (فَاقْتِرَانِيُّ (١) ، فَإِنْ تَرَكّب (۲) مِنَ الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ الصَّرْفَةِ (۳) فَحَمْلِيُّ ، وَإِلَّا فَشَرْطِيٌّ ) .
Explanation of the limits of the syllogism and its division. Commentary on Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum: by its form. He only said 'by its form' because the very contradictory or its opposite cannot be a premise, otherwise it would become a petition principii, or the syllogism would contain something false in the view of the reasoner. Rather, it is only part of a premise, and a proposition does not become part of a proposition as long as it is a proposition. And if it is not mentioned by its form, but only by its matter, then it is conjunctive. Then if it is composed purely of categorical [propositions], it is categorical; otherwise it is conditional.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) by emphasizing the importance of its form (hay'a) distinct from its matter (madda). The author clarifies that a contradictory statement cannot serve as a premise in its full form because that would either beg the question (musadara) or introduce falsehood. Instead, a contradictory notion may be part of a premise. Then the passage divides the syllogism into two types: if the form is present with its structure, it is an istithna'i (exceptive) syllogism; if only the matter is present without the form, it is iqtirani (conjunctive). The iqtirani is further divided: if it is composed entirely of categorical propositions (hamliyya), it is called hamli (categorical); otherwise it is shartiyya (conditional). This classification is foundational for understanding the structure of arguments in Islamic logic, analogous to how in usul al-fiqh, a proof must have a valid form to be sound.

Key Terms
القياس
الهيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why can't a contradictory proposition be used as a premise in a syllogism?
A) Because it would make the syllogism invalid by form.
B) Because it would lead to a petition principii (musadara) or falsehood in the reasoner's view.
C) Because it is not a categorical proposition.
D) Because it is too complex.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a syllogism depends on its form, not its matter; a contradictory cannot be a premise as it would cause circularity or falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What Is Mantiq?

Division of Qiyas into Iqtirani and Istithna'i
Author divides qiyas based on form and proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a syllogism as iqtirani or istithna'i, and further as categorical or conditional based on its composition.

Core Idea
Syllogisms are divided into conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i) based on the presence of the form, then further into categorical and conditional.

The passage next divides qiyas: if the form is present structurally, it is istithna'i (exceptive); if only matter, it is iqtirani (conjunctive). Then iqtirani is split into categorical (hamli) if all propositions are categorical, otherwise conditional (shartiyya). This classification helps in applying the correct validity rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division principle
The author divides qiyas based on whether the form (hay'a) is explicitly mentioned or not. If the form is mentioned, it is istithna'i; if only matter, it is iqtirani.
2
Define istithna'i and iqtirani
Istithna'i: the conclusion is explicitly stated through an exception (e.g., modus ponens). Iqtirani: the conclusion is not explicitly stated but follows from the conjunction of premises.
Iqtirani is often called 'conjunctive' or 'conjunctive syllogism'.
3
Further division of iqtirani
Iqtirani syllogisms are subdivided: if all premises are categorical propositions (حمليات), it is hamli (categorical); if any premise is conditional, it is shartiyya (conditional).
4
Apply the classification
Given a syllogism, determine whether it is iqtirani or istithna'i, and if iqtirani, whether it is hamli or shartiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان حدود القياس انقسامه إلى شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم بهَيْئَتِهِ ، وإنما قال بهيئته لأن عين النقيضة أو نقيضها لا يمكن أن يكون مقدمة ، وإلا لصارت مصادرة، أو اشتمل القياس على كاذب في زعم المستدل، بل إنما يكون جزء مقدمة ، والقضية لا تكون جزء قضية ما دامت قضية . (وَإِلّا) يكن مذكورا بهيئته، بل بمادته فقط (فَاقْتِرَانِيُّ (١) ، فَإِنْ تَرَكّب (۲) مِنَ الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ الصَّرْفَةِ (۳) فَحَمْلِيُّ ، وَإِلَّا فَشَرْطِيٌّ ) .
Explanation of the limits of the syllogism and its division. Commentary on Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum: by its form. He only said 'by its form' because the very contradictory or its opposite cannot be a premise, otherwise it would become a petition principii, or the syllogism would contain something false in the view of the reasoner. Rather, it is only part of a premise, and a proposition does not become part of a proposition as long as it is a proposition. And if it is not mentioned by its form, but only by its matter, then it is conjunctive. Then if it is composed purely of categorical [propositions], it is categorical; otherwise it is conditional.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) by emphasizing the importance of its form (hay'a) distinct from its matter (madda). The author clarifies that a contradictory statement cannot serve as a premise in its full form because that would either beg the question (musadara) or introduce falsehood. Instead, a contradictory notion may be part of a premise.
